The Disjunctive Conditional: Definition and Key Distinction
Author defines the disjunctive conditional as a judgment of incompatibility between two propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the disjunctive conditional (al-munfaṣila) and identify its core logical feature of mutual exclusivity.

Core Idea
The disjunctive conditional asserts that two propositions cannot both be true and cannot both be false.

In this lesson, we examine the definition of the disjunctive conditional (al-sharṭiyya al-munfaṣila). The passage distinguishes it from the connected conditional and clarifies that the judgment of incompatibility (tanāfī) refers to the impossibility of both parts being true or both false. This is a crucial concept in logic because it underlies valid disjunctive syllogisms, which are used extensively in Islamic legal reasoning and theology.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the sentence that defines the disjunctive conditional. It appears right after 'وَإِمَّا مُنْفَصِلَةٌ'. Notice it uses the phrase 'بِتَنَافِي نِسْبَتَيْنِ'.
The definition is: 'إِنْ حُكِمَ فِيْهَا بِتَنَافِي نِسْبَتَيْنِ'.
2
Identify the two conditions of incompatibility
Al-Dasuqi explains that 'tanāfī' means 'impossibility of their coexistence in truth and falsehood'. This gives two conditions: man' al-jam' (cannot both be true) and man' al-khuluw (cannot both be false).
These two conditions together define full incompatibility.
3
Distinguish from categorical propositions about concepts
Al-Attar warns against confusing the disjunctive proposition with a categorical statement about concepts. For example, 'this is either one or many' is disjunctive if we mean the two full propositions; if we mean the concepts, it is categorical.
The disjunctive is a compound of two complete propositions, not a simple subject-predicate.
4
Apply the definition to an example
Consider 'Either the prayer is valid or it is invalid'. Check that these two propositions cannot both be true and cannot both be false. This confirms it is a valid disjunctive conditional.
Useful for checking your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسامُ الشَّرطيَّةِ الْمُنْفَصِلة (وَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ) بالرفع ؛ عطفٌ على قوله : «متصلة»؛ أي: الشَّرطيَّة إِمَّا متّصلة إن حكم فيها بثبوتِ نسبةٍ أو نَفيها على تقدير أخرى كما مرَّ. [ حقيقيَّة ] وإِمَّا منفصلةٌ (إنْ حُكِمَ فِيْهَا بِتَنَافِي نِسْبَتَيْنِ، الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ: إِنْ حُكِمَ فِيْهَا بِتَنَافِي نِسْبَتَيْنِ) أي : بامتناع اجتماعهما في الصدقِ والكذب، وهذا في الموجبة. العطار (قَوْلُهُ : إِنْ حُكِمَ فِيْهَا لا يَخفَى أَنَّ المقسَّمَ مُلاحظ في الأقسام، فالمعنى قضيَّةٌ شَرطيَّةٌ مُحْكِمَ فيها ... إلخ، فَلَا يرد عليه قولنا : هذا واحد، يُنافي: هذا كثير، وقولنا : هذا بياضُ ؛ يُنافي : هذا سواد، وأمَّا إذا قُلْنَا : هذا إما واحدٌ وإِمَّا كثير؛ فإنْ أردنا المنافاة بين هذا واحد وهذا كثير؛ فالقضيَّةُ مُنفصلةٌ مُركَّبَةٌ مِن قضيَّتَيْنِ، ومنعُ الجمع باعتبار الصدقِ والتَّحقُّقِ بين القضيَّتَيْنِ، وإن أردنا المنافاة بين مفهومي الواحد والكثير في الصدق والحمل على هذا؛ فَالقضيَّةُ حَملِيَّةٌ مُركَّبَةٌ مِن موضوع، (قَوْلُهُ : فيه ... إلخ يجاب عنه بأن الإضافة لأدنى ملابسة كأنه قال : هي التي حكم فيها بسلب نسبة على تقدير أخرى على وجه الاتفاق، وكذا يقال في : بسلب اللزوم. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
The divisions of the disjunctive conditional. (And disjunctive) in the nominative, coordinated to his saying 'connected': that is, the conditional is either connected if a judgment is made in it of the establishment of a relation or its negation on the assumption of another, as passed. Or it is disjunctive if a judgment is made in it of the incompatibility of two relations. Al-Dasuqi: His saying 'if a judgment is made in it of the incompatibility of two relations' means: the impossibility of their coexistence in truth and falsehood, and this is in the positive. Al-Attar: His saying 'if a judgment is made in it' [commentary]: It is not hidden that the divided [the genus] is observed in the divisions, so the meaning is a conditional proposition in which a judgment is made... etc., so it is not subjected to our saying 'this is one contradicts this is many', and our saying 'this is whiteness contradicts this is blackness'. But if we say 'this is either one or many' — if we intend the incompatibility between 'this is one' and 'this is many', then the proposition is a disjunctive composed of two propositions, and the prevention of collection is in terms of truth and actuality between the two propositions. And if we intend the incompatibility between the concepts of 'one' and 'many' in truth and predication on this [subject], then the proposition is a categorical composite of a subject... (His saying: 'regarding it... etc.' It is answered that the annexation is for the minimal connection, as if he said: it is that in which a judgment is made of negating a relation on the assumption of another in the manner of agreement, and similarly is said regarding the negation of implicative relation. End quote. Al-Shurunbulali.
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage defines the disjunctive conditional (al-munfaṣila). It begins by stating that conditional propositions are divided into two types: the connected (muttaṣila), which judges a relation (positive or negative) on the assumption of another, and the disjunctive (munfaṣila), which judges the incompatibility (tanāfi) of two relations. The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that 'judgment of incompatibility of two relations' means the two parts cannot both be true and cannot both be false at the same time — this is the positive disjunctive. Al-Attar then clarifies a potential confusion: if we say 'This is either one or many', we might be pointing to the incompatibility between the two propositions (the disjunctive judgment), or we might be stating a categorical proposition about the concepts. In the disjunctive, we treat the two parts as complete propositions and rule out their coexistence or both being false. The footnote from al-Shurunbulali ties back to the earlier discussion of the accidental conditional, noting that similar phrasing can apply. The passage thus establishes the core definition of the disjunctive conditional as one that asserts a mutual exclusivity of truth and falsehood between its two component propositions.

Key Terms
منفصلة
تنافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'judgment of incompatibility of two relations' (al-ḥukm bi-tanāfī nisbatayn) in the disjunctive conditional mean?
A) The two propositions are both true.
B) The two propositions are both false.
C) The two propositions cannot be both true and cannot be both false.
D) The two propositions cannot be both true but can be both false.

Key Takeaway
The disjunctive conditional is defined by the mutual exclusivity of truth and falsehood between its two component propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the statement 'Either this act is obligatory or it is forbidden.' Determine whether this is a disjunctive conditional. Explain which two propositions are involved and how the condition of incompatibility applies.
?
Al-Attar warns against confusing the disjunctive conditional with a categorical proposition about concepts. Provide your own example from fiqh (or kalam) where this confusion might occur, and clarify the correct interpretation.

Two Types of Disjunctive Conditional Defined
Author defines maniat al-jam' wal-khuluw and maniat al-jam' with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between maniat al-jam' wal-khuluw and maniat al-jam' by identifying their truth conditions in Islamic legal examples.

Core Idea
The key is whether both parts can be false: if yes, it is maniat al-jam'; if not, it is maniat al-jam' wal-khuluw.

The author introduces two types of disjunctive conditionals (al-shartiyya al-munfasila) based on the scope of incompatibility. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments in fiqh and kalam where disjunctions are used to present alternatives. This lesson parses the definitions and examples to solidify the concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of judgment
Determine whether the proposition judges incompatibility (tanafi) or non-incompatibility of the two parts. The affirmative asserts incompatibility; the negative denies it.
The judgment is about the relation between the two parts, not their individual truth values.
2
Distinguish the scope of incompatibility
Check if the incompatibility covers both truth and falsehood, or only truth. 'Maniat al-jam' wal-khuluw' means the two parts cannot be both true and cannot be both false. 'Maniat al-jam'' means they cannot be both true, but may be both false.
The phrase 'aw sidqan faqat' ('or truth only') signals the second type.
3
Apply to an Islamic ruling example
Take the statement: 'Every action is either obligatory or forbidden.' Can an action be both? No. Can it be neither? Yes, if it is permissible. So this is maniat al-jam' (truth exclusion only). Conversely, 'A number is either even or odd' is maniat al-jam' wal-khuluw because it cannot be both or neither.
Use usul al-fiqh categories to test the conditions.
4
Analyze the negative form
The negative version denies incompatibility. For maniat al-jam' wal-khuluw, the negative means the parts can be both true and both false. For maniat al-jam', the negative means they can be both true. Construct an example: 'Not at all is it that a thing is either black or a writer' – both can be true or false.
The example uses a non-Islamic illustration; replace with an Islamic one like 'An act is either recommended or disliked' – they can coincide in some cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي : إمَّا موجبةٌ، أو سالبةٌ . فالموجبةُ : هي التي حكم فيها بتنافي نسبتين في الصدق والكذب معاً. كقولنا : «هذا العددُ إمَّا زوج أو فرد»، فإنَّ زوجيَّةَ العددِ وفرديَّته متنافيان في الصِّدقِ والكذب؛ أي: لا يصدقانِ وَلَا يكذبان . والسَّالبة : هي حُكِمَ فيها بعدم تنافي نسبتَين في الصّدقِ والكذبِ كَقولِنَا : «ليس ألبية إمَّا أن يكون هذا أسود أو كاتباً»، فإنَّهما يصدقان ويكذبانِ، وَلَا مُنافاة بينهما صدقاً وكذباً مَانِعَةُ الْجَمْعِ (أَوْ صِدْقَاً فَقَطْ) عطفٌ على قولِهِ : صدقاً وكذباً؛ أي: إنْ كانَ الحكم بتنافي نِسبَتَيْنِ ، أو عدم تنافيهِمَا في الصِّدقِ فقط (فَمَانِعَةُ الجَمْعِ). وهى أيضاً : إمَّا موجبة، أو سالبة .
It (the disjunctive conditional) is either affirmative or negative. The affirmative is that in which a judgment is made of the incompatibility of two relations in truth and falsehood together. As our saying: 'This number is either even or odd', for the evenness and oddness of the number are incompatible in truth and falsehood; i.e., they are not both true and not both false. The negative is that in which a judgment is made of the non-incompatibility of two relations in truth and falsehood, as our saying: 'Not at all is it that this is either black or a writer', for they are both true and both false, and there is no incompatibility between them in truth and falsehood. Maniat al-jam' (with 'or' in truth only) is a conjunction upon his saying 'truth and falsehood'; i.e., if the judgment is of the incompatibility of two relations, or their non-incompatibility, only in truth (then it is maniat al-jam'). It also is either affirmative or negative.
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage defines the two main types of disjunctive conditional (al-shartiyya al-munfasila). The author first discusses the type that excludes both truth and falsehood (maniat al-jam' wal-khuluw), which is the standard exclusive disjunction. The affirmative form asserts that the two parts cannot be both true nor both false; the example given is a number being either even or odd. The negative form denies this incompatibility, meaning the two parts can be both true and both false; the example is a thing being black and a writer (which can coincide). Then the author introduces the second type, maniat al-jam', which only excludes truth: the two parts cannot be both true, but they can be both false. This is similar to exclusive disjunction in modern logic (xor) but without the requirement that one must be true. Each of these types also has affirmative and negative forms. In Islamic sciences, similar distinctions apply: e.g., 'Every action is either obligatory or forbidden' (maniat al-jam' wal-khuluw, since an action cannot be both nor neither in the same respect) versus 'Every statement is either true or false' (maniat al-jam', since a statement cannot be both true and false, but can be neither if it is meaningless).

Key Terms
موجبة
سالبة
تنافي
مانعة الجمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Every action is either obligatory or forbidden.
B) Every action is either recommended or disliked.
C) Every action is either permissible or not permissible.
D) Every action is either valid or invalid.

Key Takeaway
The fundamental distinction between the two types of disjunctive conditionals is whether the incompatibility includes both truth and falsehood or only truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Affirmative and Negative of Maniat al-Jam'
Affirmative and negative of maniat al-jam'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the affirmative and negative forms of maniat al-jam' and apply them to Islamic legal examples.

Core Idea
Maniat al-jam' rules that two parts cannot both be true, but its negation allows both true.

In Islamic logic, disjunctive statements are analyzed for their truth conditions. This lesson focuses on 'maniat al-jam'' (exclusive disjunction in truth only). The author defines two forms: the affirmative asserts incompatibility in truth; the negative denies that incompatibility. Understanding these forms is essential for evaluating arguments in fiqh and kalam, especially in conditional syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the disjunctive
In the example 'This thing is either a tree or a stone', the parts are 'it is a tree' and 'it is a stone'. Note how they are related: they are alternatives.
2
Understand the truth condition of affirmative
The affirmative asserts that the two parts cannot both be true. Check the example: a thing cannot be both a tree and a stone, but it can be neither (if it is human).
This matches the definition of maniat al-jam'.
3
Understand the truth condition of negative
The negative denies incompatibility, meaning the two parts can both be true. The example 'It is not that this thing is either not a tree nor not a stone' is a double negation that allows both parts to be true (in logical sense).
This is the opposite of the affirmative.
4
Apply to Islamic legal example
In fiqh, 'This act is either obligatory or forbidden' is an affirmative maniat al-jam' because the act cannot be both, but can be neither. In contrast, 'It is not that this act is neither obligatory nor forbidden' is negative, implying both can be true—though logically possible, in fiqh this might be rare.
Always consider the real-world applicability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ فالموجبةُ : هي التي حُكم فيها بتنافي الجزأين في الصدق فقط كَقولِنَا : هذا الشَّيءُ إِمَّا شجرٌ وإِما حجرٌ»، فإنَّهما لا يصدقان، ولكن يكذبان بأن يكون إنساناً . والسَّالبةُ : هي التي حُكِمَ فيها بعدم تنافي الجزأين في الصدق فقط كَقولنا : «ليس إما أن يكون هذا الشَّيء لا شجراً وَلا حجراً». فإنَّهما يصدقان ولا يكذبان وإلا ؛ لَكان شجراً وحجّراً معاً .
Chapter: On the divisions of the conditional. The affirmative is that in which a judgment is made of the incompatibility of the two parts in truth only, as our saying: 'This thing is either a tree or a stone', for they are not both true, but they are both false in that it is a human. The negative is that in which a judgment is made of the non-incompatibility of the two parts in truth only, as our saying: 'It is not that this thing is either not a tree nor not a stone', for they are both true and not both false; otherwise, it would be a tree and a stone together.
Source pages p. 611

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of maniat al-jam' (exclusive disjunction in truth alone). The affirmative form asserts that the two parts cannot be simultaneously true; the example given is something being both a tree and a stone – impossible. However, they can both be false (e.g., a human is neither a tree nor a stone). The negative form denies this incompatibility in truth, meaning the two parts can be both true; the example is the negation of the disjunction: 'It is not that this thing is neither a tree nor a stone' – which is equivalent to saying it is either a tree or a stone, both of which can be true (if it is both a tree and a stone, but that's impossible in reality – the example is intended to show logical possibility). The negative form allows both parts to be true, and they cannot both be false, otherwise the assertion would be true. In Islamic logic, this distinction helps analyze conditional statements in arguments, such as in the qiyas istithna'i. For example, in a legal ruling: 'Either this act is obligatory or it is permissible' is a maniat al-jam' because an act cannot be both, but can be neither if it is disliked.

Key Terms
الجزأين
تنافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following correctly represents the affirmative (positive) maniat al-jam' in fiqh?
A) 'This act is either obligatory or forbidden' (both cannot be true).
B) 'This act is either recommended or disliked' (both cannot be false).
C) 'This act is either valid or invalid' (both cannot be true or false).
D) 'This act is either permissible or not permissible' (both cannot be true).

Key Takeaway
In maniat al-jam', affirmative prohibits simultaneous truth of both parts, while negative permits it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Understanding the Exclusive Disjunction (مانعة الخلو)
Author defines the exclusive disjunction with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish between affirmative and negative exclusive disjunctions by analyzing their truth conditions.

Core Idea
The exclusive disjunction forbids both parts being false, not both being true.

In this passage, the author introduces a key type of conditional proposition: the exclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو). This distinction is crucial for correct reasoning in Islamic logic, especially when evaluating arguments that involve disjunctive claims. The author provides a positive example (Zayd in the sea or not drowning) and a negative example (not a tree or a stone) to illustrate the property of mutual exclusion in falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the affirmative exclusive disjunction
Find the example 'Zayd is either in the sea or does not drown.' Notice that the ruling is 'تنافي الجزأين في الكذب' (mutual exclusion in falsehood), meaning both parts cannot be false simultaneously.
The text explicitly states this ruling for the affirmative case.
2
Analyze the truth conditions for the affirmative example
Consider the scenario: 'Zayd is in the sea' is true, 'does not drown' is true. If both were false (i.e., Zayd is not in the sea and does drown), that would be a contradiction. So the disjunction requires at least one part true.
The author explains that if both false, Zayd would drown on land, which is impossible.
3
Identify the negative exclusive disjunction
Find the example 'It is not that this thing is either a tree or a stone.' Here, the ruling is that there is no mutual exclusion in falsehood (لا تنافي), meaning both parts can be false.
The negative form explicitly denies the property of the affirmative.
4
Compare the two forms
The affirmative exclusive disjunction forces at least one part to be true (not both false). The negative form removes this requirement, allowing both parts to be false. Recognize that this differs from the inclusive 'or' where both can be true as well.
This distinction is fundamental in logic and fiqh (e.g., options in legal rulings).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ. وَهِيَ: إمَّا موجبةٌ، أو سالبةٌ. فالموجبةُ: كَقولنا: زيدٌ إما أن يكونَ في البحر أو لا يغرق، حكم فيها بتنافي الجزأين في الكذب؛ لأنَّ الكونَ في البحر مع عدمِ الغرقِ يصدقان ولا يكذبان، وإلا لَغَرِقَ في البر. والسالبة: كقولنا: ليس إما أن يكون هذا الشيء شجراً أو حجراً، حكم بعدم تنافي الجزأين في الكذب، وإلا لَكَانَ شجراً وحجراً معاً.
Section: On the divisions of the conditional proposition. It is either affirmative or negative. The affirmative: like our saying: 'Zayd is either in the sea or does not drown.' It is ruled that the two parts mutually exclude falsehood; because being in the sea with not drowning are both true and not false, otherwise he would drown on land. The negative: like our saying: 'It is not that this thing is either a tree or a stone.' It is ruled that there is no mutual exclusion of the two parts in falsehood, otherwise it would be a tree and a stone together.
Source pages p. 613

Commentary
This passage defines the exclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو), which is a type of conditional proposition in logic. The author first states that conditional propositions are divided into affirmative and negative. The affirmative example is: 'Zayd is either in the sea or does not drown.' The ruling here is that the two parts (being in the sea and not drowning) cannot both be false. The reason given is that if both were false, then Zayd would drown on land (since being in the sea is false means he is on land, and not drowning is false means he drowns, leading to a contradiction). This shows that in an affirmative exclusive disjunction, it is impossible for both parts to be false; they can both be true (if Zayd is in the sea and not drowning). The negative example is: 'It is not that this thing is either a tree or a stone.' In this negative case, it is not required that the two parts mutually exclude falsehood; they can both be false (e.g., the thing is neither tree nor stone). The phrase 'otherwise it would be a tree and a stone together' explains that if the mutual exclusion in falsehood were required, then the thing would have to be both tree and stone simultaneously, which is impossible. Thus, the negative form affirms that both parts can be false. This distinction is crucial for understanding the truth conditions of exclusive disjunctions in logic.

Key Terms
مانعة الخلو (māni‘at al-khulū): A type of disjunctive conditional that forbids the absence (khuluww) of both parts simultaneously, i.e., requires at least one part true, but allows both to be true. [Note: In later texts, 'مانعة الخلو' is sometimes contrasted with 'مانعة الجمع' (which forbids both being true). This definition is standard.]
تنافي الجزأين في الكذب (tanāfī al-juz‘ayn fī al-kadhib): Mutual exclusion of the two parts in falsehood, meaning it is impossible for both parts to be false simultaneously. This is the key property of the affirmative exclusive disjunction. [Note: No significant shift; this term is consistently used in logic texts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the affirmative exclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو), what is the relationship between the two parts?
A) They cannot both be true.
B) They cannot both be false.
C) They cannot both be true and cannot both be false.
D) They must both be false.

Key Takeaway
The exclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو) in its affirmative form requires that at least one part be true, forbidding both being false, while the negative form removes this requirement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the fiqh statement: 'A person who breaks the fast intentionally must either fast two consecutive months or feed sixty poor people.' Identify whether this is an affirmative exclusive disjunction and explain the truth condition regarding both parts being false.
?
Create your own affirmative exclusive disjunction using an Islamic example (e.g., from kalam or fiqh) and state the ruling on whether both parts can be false.

Types of Disjunctive Conditional and the Oppositional Distinction
Author defines three disjunctive types and oppositional vs accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of disjunctive conditional and classify a given disjunctive proposition as oppositional or accidental.

Core Idea
Three disjunctive types differ in truth possibilities; oppositional vs accidental depends on essential opposition.

In this section, the author defines the three types of disjunctive conditional (munfasila) and introduces the crucial distinction between oppositional (inadiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya) disjunctions. This classification is essential for correctly analyzing conditional arguments in logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
List the three divisions
Find the three types of munfasila mentioned: haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', and mani'at al-khuluww.
These are the fundamental categories of disjunctive conditionals.
2
Understand truth conditions for each
For each type, state what it prevents: haqiqiyya prevents both conjunction (both true) and void (both false); mani'at al-jam' prevents conjunction only; mani'at al-khuluww prevents void only.
This determines the logical behavior of the disjunction.
3
Distinguish oppositional from accidental
The author says each type is oppositional ('inadiyya) if the opposition between parts is due to their essences, otherwise accidental (ittifaqiyya). Check whether the opposition is inherent or external.
Oppositional disjunctions are stronger and more informative.
4
Apply to examples
Analyze the given examples: even/odd (haqiqiyya 'inadiyya), tree/stone (mani'at al-jam' 'inadiyya), Zayd in sea or not drowning (mani'at al-khuluww 'inadiyya). Verify the truth conditions match.
These classical examples solidify the concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ. فَالْمُنْفَصِلَةُ ثَلَاثَةُ أَقْسَامٍ: حَقِيقِيَّةٌ، وَمَانِعَةُ الْجَمْعِ، وَمَانِعَةُ الْخُلُوِّ. وَكُلٌّ مِنْهَا عِنَادِيَّةٌ إِنْ كَانَ التَّنَافِي بَيْنَ الْجُزْأَيْنِ لِذَاتِ الْجُزْأَيْنِ، وَإِلَّا فَاتِّفَاقِيَّةٌ. فَالْعِنَادُ لِذَاتِ الْجُزْأَيْنِ هُوَ أَنْ يَكُونَ بَيْنَهُمَا مَا يَقْتَضِي التَّنَافِيَ فِي الصِّدْقِ أَوِ الْكِذْبِ أَوْ فِيهِمَا. وَمِثَالُ الْحَقِيقِيَّةِ الْعِنَادِيَّةِ: التَّنَافِي بَيْنَ الزَّوْجِ وَالْفَرْدِ. وَمِثَالُ مَانِعَةِ الْجَمْعِ الْعِنَادِيَّةِ: بَيْنَ الشَّجَرِ وَالْحَجَرِ. وَمِثَالُ مَانِعَةِ الْخُلُوِّ الْعِنَادِيَّةِ: كَوْنُ زَيْدٍ فِي الْبَحْرِ أَوْ لَا يَغْرَقُ.
Section on the divisions of the conditional. The disjunctive is three divisions: haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', and mani'at al-khuluww. Each of them is oppositional if the opposition between the two parts is due to the essence of the two parts, otherwise it is accidental. The opposition due to the essence of the two parts is that there is between them what necessitates opposition in truth or falsehood or both. An example of the oppositional haqiqiyya: the opposition between even and odd. An example of the oppositional mani'at al-jam': between tree and stone. An example of the oppositional mani'at al-khuluww: Zayd being in the sea or not drowning.
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage defines the divisions of the disjunctive conditional. The author states that the disjunctive has three types: haqiqiyya (real disjunctive), which prevents both conjunction and void; mani'at al-jam' (preventive of conjunction), which prevents both parts from being true together but allows both to be false; and mani'at al-khuluww (preventive of void), which prevents both parts from being false together but allows both to be true. He then introduces the distinction between 'inadiyya (oppositional) and ittifaqiyya (accidental). 'Inadiyya occurs when the opposition between the two parts is due to their essences, as explained by al-Dasuqi: when the parts are considered, there is inherent opposition in truth, falsehood, or both. The examples illustrate: for haqiqiyya, 'even and odd' cannot be both true nor both false. For mani'at al-jam', 'tree and stone' cannot both be true (a thing cannot be both) but can both be false (if something is neither). For mani'at al-khuluww, 'Zayd being in the sea or not drowning' cannot both be false (if he is not in the sea, he will not drown, so the pair cannot be simultaneously false) but can both be true (if he is in the sea and does not drown). This classification is foundational for analyzing conditional propositions in logic.

مانعة الجمع

Key Takeaway
The three types of disjunctive conditional are distinguished by what they prevent: both truth and falsehood (haqiqiyya), only conjunction (mani'at al-jam'), or only void (mani'at al-khuluww); whether it is oppositional or accidental depends on whether the opposition is essential to the parts.

Distinction Between 'Inadiyya and Ittifaqiyya Conditionals
Author defines 'inadiyya and ittifaqiyya with an example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'inadiyya and ittifaqiyya conditional propositions and determine the basis of incompatibility in any given disjunctive statement.

Core Idea
'Inadiyya = conceptual clash; Ittifaqiyya = accidental conflict.

Conditional propositions (al-shartiyya) are divided into two types based on the source of incompatibility between their parts. The author clarifies that when the concepts themselves are inherently opposed, the disjunction is 'inadiyya; when the opposition arises only from external circumstances, it is ittifaqiyya. This distinction is foundational for analyzing disjunctive arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts (disjuncts) of the conditional
Read the conditional statement and list the two component propositions. For example, in 'Either this is black or a writer,' the disjuncts are 'this is black' and 'this is a writer.'
2
Examine the conceptual relationship
Determine whether the meanings (mafahim) of the disjuncts are inherently contradictory. Ask: Can something be both black and a writer in its essence? If yes, the concepts are not incompatible.
Conceptual incompatibility means one concept necessarily negates the other, like 'black' and 'non-black'.
3
Classify based on the basis of incompatibility
If the concepts are inherently opposed, it is 'inadiyya. If the incompatibility is due only to factual context (e.g., this particular person happens to be black and not a writer), it is ittifaqiyya.
The author's example illustrates ittifaqiyya: blackness and being a writer are not conceptually opposed, but in this case they are incompatible because the person is black and not a writer.
4
Derive the subcases from the example
From the ittifaqiyya example, note that both parts cannot be true together (since one is false) and cannot both be false (since one is true). This shows the hakiqiyya (true exclusive) form. Then consider variants: man'at al-jam' (both cannot be true but can be false) and man'at al-khuluw (both cannot be false but can be true).
These subcases can be extracted by modifying the factual scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيةِ فالعناديَّة : ما حُكِمَ فيها بالتَّنافي لذاتِ الجزأين؛ أي: حُكِمَ بأَنَّ مفهوم أحدِهما منافٍ لمفهوم الآخَرِ . (وَإِلَّا ) : أي : وإن لم يكن التّنافي لذات الجزأين؛ (فَاتِّفَاقِيَّةٌ)، فهي الَّتِي مُحكِمَ فيها بالتَّنافي لذاتِ الجزأين، بل لمجرَّدِ أَنِ اتَّفَقَ في الواقع أن يكونَ بينهما منافاة، وإن لم يقتض أن مفهوم أحدهما منافياً لمفهوم الآخَرِ كَقولنا للأسود اللاكاتب: «إما أن يكون هذا أسود أو كاتباً»، فإنَّه لا منافاة بين مفهومي الأسود والكاتب، لكن اتَّفق تحقُّقُ السَّوادِ وانتفاء الكتابة، فلا يصدقان؛ لانتفاء الكتابة، ولا يكذبان ؛ لوجودِ السَّواد، هذا في الحقيقية، أمَّا مانعةُ الجمع والخلو، فيمكن استخراجهما من هذا المثال.
A section on the divisions of the conditional. The 'inadiyya is that in which judgment of incompatibility is made due to the essences of the two parts; i.e., judgment that the concept of one is incompatible with the concept of the other. (Otherwise): i.e., if the incompatibility is not due to the essences of the two parts, then it is ittifaqiyya. That is the one in which judgment of incompatibility is not made due to the essences of the two parts, but merely because it accidentally happened in external reality that there is an incompatibility between them, even though the concept of one is not incompatible with the concept of the other, like our saying of the black non-writer: 'Either this is black or a writer,' for there is no incompatibility between the concepts of blackness and being a writer, but it happened that blackness existed and writing was absent, so they are not both true (because of the absence of writing) and they are not both false (because of the existence of blackness). This is in the case of the hakiqiyya. As for man'at al-jam' and man'at al-khuluw, they can be extracted from this example.
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the divisions of conditional propositions (shartiyya). The author distinguishes two types of disjunctions: 'inadiyya (incompatible) and ittifaqiyya (accidental). 'Inadiyya is characterized by an intrinsic incompatibility between the two component propositions, meaning that the conceptual content (mafhum) of one is inherently opposed to the conceptual content of the other. For example, 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he is not drowning'—the concepts themselves imply contradiction. In contrast, ittifaqiyya lacks such intrinsic incompatibility; the opposition arises only from factual circumstances. The example given is saying of someone who happens to be black and not a writer: 'Either this is black or a writer.' While blackness and being a writer are not conceptually opposed, in this particular case the factual context (the person is black and not a writer) makes it impossible for both to be true (since one is false) and impossible for both to be false (since one is true). This yields a disjunction that is true exactly when exactly one part is true—this is the 'hakiqiyya' (true exclusive disjunction). The author then notes that from this example one can derive the two other common forms: man'at al-jam' (prohibitive of conjunction) where both cannot be true but both can be false, and man'at al-khuluw (prohibitive of emptiness) where both cannot be false but both can be true. This highlights the logical richness of conditional propositions.

الاتفاقية
الحقيقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

C) Both are based on intrinsic concepts.

Key Takeaway
The basis of incompatibility—whether intrinsic to the concepts or accidental to the facts—determines whether a disjunctive conditional is 'inadiyya or ittifaqiyya.

Deriving Exclusive and Exhaustive Disjunctions
Deriving disjunction types from an example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive mani'at al-jam' (exclusive) and mani'at al-khuluw (exhaustive) disjunctions from a given haqiqiyya example by negating one disjunct and verifying the truth conditions.

Core Idea
Mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khuluw are derivations from haqiqiyya, not separate types.

In Islamic logic, disjunctions are classified into three types: haqiqiyya (true disjunction), mani'at al-jam' (exclusive), and mani'at al-khuluw (exhaustive). The latter two are not independent; they are derived from the haqiqiyya by negating one part. Al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar demonstrate this derivation using the example of a black non-writer. This lesson will walk you through the logical steps to extract both types from a single example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original haqiqiyya example
Start with the example: 'Either this man is black or a writer' given the assumption that he is black and not a writer. This is a haqiqiyya (true disjunction) because one part is true and the other false.
The example is specific to a black non-writer person.
2
Understand the truth conditions of haqiqiyya
In haqiqiyya, both parts cannot be true together and cannot be false together. Here, 'black' is true, 'writer' is false, so it satisfies the condition.
3
Negate the first part to obtain mani'at al-jam'
Negate 'black' to get 'not black', forming the statement: 'Either this is not black or a writer'. Now check: both parts cannot be true together (since 'not black' is false and 'writer' is false, they are not both true), and they can both be false (which happens). This is exactly mani'at al-jam'.
Mani'at al-jam' allows both false but forbids both true.
4
Negate the second part to obtain mani'at al-khuluw
Negate 'writer' to get 'not writer', forming: 'Either this is black or not a writer'. Check: both parts cannot be false together (since 'black' is true and 'not writer' is true, they are not both false), and they can both be true (which happens). This is mani'at al-khuluw.
Mani'at al-khuluw allows both true but forbids both false.
5
Compare the derived types
Notice that both derived forms are simply the original haqiqiyya with one disjunct negated. The change in truth conditions defines the type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : أَنْ يَكُوْنَ) أي : كونُ بينهما، فالمصدرُ فاعلُ اتَّفق .
(قَوْلُهُ : وَإِنْ لَمْ يَقْتَضِ أي : والحالُ أنَّ الواقع لم يقتضِ أَنَّ مَفهوم ... إلخ . (قَوْلُهُ : كَقَوْلِنَا لِلْأَسْوَدِ اللَّا كَاتِبِ ) أي : إذا فرضَ هكذا أنَّه أسود وليس بكاتب، فإذا قُلْتَ: إما أن يكون هذا الرَّجلُ أسودَ أو كاتباً ; فَلَا يجتمعان فيه لِفَرضِ انتفاء الكتابةِ، وَلَا يرتفعان فيهِ لوجودِ السَّوادِ فيه ; أي: بالنّسبة لخصوص هذا الشَّخص، وهذا مثال الحقيقية. (قَوْلُهُ: هَذَا فِي الْحَقِيقِيَّةِ المشار إليهِ المثالُ المذكورُ ; أي: هذا المثال المذكورُ مثال للاتفاقية في المنفصلة الحقيقية.
(قَوْلُهُ : فَيُمْكِنُ اسْتِخْرَاجُهُمَا مِنْ هَذَا الْمِثَالِ فَإِنَّهُ لَو قِيْلَ: إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ هذا لَا أسودَ أو كاتباً ; كانت مانعة الجمعِ ; لأنَّهما لا يصدقان; إذْ لَا يجتمعُ لَا أسود
العطار
والمساوي لِنَقيضِهِ كَمَا في الحقيقيَّةِ، أو الأخص منه كَمَا في مانعة الجمع، أو الأعمّ كَمَا في مانعة الخلو، ولو أريد التنافي المعتبرُ في التَّناقض ; لم يدخل إلا المتّصلة المركَّبَةُ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقيضِهِ فقط; تَأَمَّلْ.
(قَوْلُهُ : فَيُمْكِنُ اسْتِخْرَاجُهُمَا ) فإنَّهُ لو قِيْلَ : إِمَّا أنْ يكون هذا لا أسود أو كاتباً ;
وكاتب ; لأنَّ الفرضَ أَنَّهُ أسود، ولكنْ يكذبانِ لانتفاء لا أسود والكاتب معاً الواقع ; لأنَّ الفرضَ أَنَّهُ أَسودُ غيرُ كاتب.
ولو قِيلَ: إِمَّا أنْ يكونَ هذا أسودَ أوْ لا كاتباً ; كانَتْ مانعة الخلو; لأنَّهما لا يكذبانِ; لِعَدَمِ تَحقُّقِ اللا أسود والكتابة في الواقع بحسب الفرض، ويصدقان لتحققِ السَّوادِ واللاكتابة بحسب الواقع. انتهى ياسين.
Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'that it be' means the being between them, so the masdar is the subject of 'occurred'. His statement: 'and if not' means: and the state is that reality did not require that the concept... etc. His statement: 'like our saying about the black non-writer' means: if it is assumed that he is black and not a writer, then when you say: 'Either this man is black or a writer' – they do not both exist in him due to the assumed negation of writing, and they do not both disappear in him due to the existence of blackness in him; i.e., regarding this particular person, and this is an example of the true disjunction. His statement: 'this is concerning the true disjunction' – the indicated example is the mentioned one; i.e., this mentioned example is an example of the accidental in the true disjunction. His statement: 'they can be extracted from this example' – for if it is said: 'Either this is not black or a writer', it would be exclusive (mani'at al-jam'), because they are not both true, since 'not black' and 'writer' do not both exist together. Al-'Attar: And the equal to its contradictory as in the true disjunction, or the more specific than it as in the exclusive, or the more general as in the exhaustive. And if the incompatibility considered in contradiction is intended, then only the conditional composed of the thing and its contradictory enters; reflect. His statement: 'they can be extracted' – for if it is said: 'Either this is not black or a writer'... and writer; because the assumption is that he is black, but they are both false because of the simultaneous non-existence of not-black and writer in reality; because the assumption is that he is black and not a writer. And if it is said: 'Either this is black or not a writer', it would be exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw), because they are not both false; due to the non-realization of not-black and writing in reality according to the assumption, and they are both true due to the realization of blackness and not-writing according to reality. End of Yasin.
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the derivation of the exclusive (mani'at al-jam') and exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw) disjunctions from the example of the black non-writer. Al-Dasuqi first clarifies the components of the original definition, then shows that by negating one part of the example (e.g., saying 'not black or writer'), we obtain the exclusive disjunction where both parts cannot be true together but can be false together. Similarly, by negating the other part ('black or not writer'), we obtain the exhaustive disjunction where both parts cannot be false together but can be true together. Al-'Attar adds a general observation: the logical relation in these disjunctions can be equal to the contradictory (as in haqiqiyya), more specific (as in mani'at al-jam'), or more general (as in mani'at al-khuluw). He also notes that if the incompatibility were that of contradiction, only conditionals with a term and its contradictory would apply.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع (mani'at al-jam'): Exclusive disjunction where both parts cannot be true together but may be false together
مانعة الخلو (mani'at al-khuluw): Exhaustive disjunction where both parts cannot be false together but may be true together
التنافي بالتضاد (al-tanafi bil-tadadd): Incompatibility by opposition, used by al-'Attar to describe the relation between parts in the disjunctions

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Haqiqiyya
B) Ittifaqiyya
C) Mani'at al-jam'
D) Mani'at al-khuluw

Key Takeaway
Mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khuluw are obtained by negating one disjunct of a haqiqiyya, with distinct truth conditions.

Division of Conditionals by Quantity: Universal, Particular, Personal, Indefinite
Division of conditionals by quantity and relation to judgment types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a conditional proposition is universal, particular, personal, or indefinite based on the scope of its judgment.

Core Idea
Conditionals, like categoricals, are quantified by the scope of the implication or opposition.

In the previous lessons, we learned about categorical propositions and their quantifiers. Now we turn to conditional propositions. Just as categoricals can be universal or particular, conditionals also come in four quantifiers: universal, particular, personal, and indefinite. This division hinges on whether the relation (implication, opposition, or agreement) holds for all individuals, some individuals, one specific individual, or is left unspecified. In this lesson, we will parse the definitions provided by the author and the commentators to understand these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the four quantifiers
The Arabic terms are: كليَّة (universal), جزئية (particular), شخصيَّة (personal), مهملة (indefinite). These correspond to the scope of the conditional relation.
Personal conditionals involve a specific subject, like 'If Zaid is visiting, then honor him.'
2
Identify the relation type
The judgment is about the relation: luzum (implication) for conjunctive, 'inad (opposition) for disjunctive, and ittifaq (agreement) for accidental. The quantifier tells us how broadly that relation holds.
For example, 'Whenever the sun rises, it is day' is a universal conditional because the implication holds for all times.
3
Read the commentary
Al-Dasuqi and Al-'Attar explain that 'other' refers to agreement, which can appear in both conjunctive and disjunctive. The hashiya critiques an example, offering a corrected version.
This shows that scholars debated the precise formulations, but the core division remains clear.
4
Test your understanding
Given a conditional, ask: Does the relation (e.g., implication) apply to every instance? If yes, universal. If to some, particular. If to one specific individual, personal. If unspecified, indefinite.
Example: 'Some travelers may shorten prayer' is particular. 'A traveler may shorten prayer' is indefinite because it's not specified whether all or some.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[تقسيم الشرطية إلى كليَّةٍ وجزئية، وشخصيَّة، ومُهمَلَة ]
(ثُمَّ الحُكْمُ) باللُّزوم والعنادِ وغيرِهما (فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ) المتّصلة والمنفصلة :
الدسوقي
وكاتب ; لأنَّ الفرضَ أَنَّهُ أسود، ولكنْ يكذبانِ لانتفاء لا أسود والكاتب معاً الواقع ; لأنَّ الفرضَ أَنَّهُ أَسودُ غيرُ كاتب.

It was exclusive; because they are not both true but are both false; due to the non-existence of not-black and writing together in reality, and if it is said: 'Either this is black or not a writer', it would be exhaustive; because they are not both false and are both true due to the realization of blackness and not-writing according to reality. Thus in the hashiya, it was said: and it is not appropriate for the statement of the sharh in this example, and the good is that it be said: that example is for the true disjunction if said about the black non-writer, as the sharh said: and it is itself an example for exclusive if we say it about the white non-writer, and it is itself an example for exhaustive if we say it about the black writer. The matter is easy.

Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section on the division of conditional propositions by quantity: universal, particular, personal, and indefinite. The matn (likely from Tahzeeb al-Mantiq) states that the judgment (hukm) in conditionals, whether by implication (luzum) for conjunctive, opposition ('inad) for disjunctive, or other (i.e., agreement), is subject to these quantifiers. Al-Dasuqi then repeats the derivation of exclusive and exhaustive forms from the earlier example, but the main point is the connection to the new division. Al-'Attar clarifies that 'other' refers to agreement (ittifaq) in both conjunctive and accidental disjunctives. There is a critique in the hashiya about the example not perfectly fitting, and a suggestion to use different subjects (black/non-writer, white/non-writer, black/writer) to correctly illustrate each type. The passage ends with a recommendation to use 'waw' (and) instead of 'aw' (or) in the phrasing.

Key Terms
كليَّة (kulliyya): Universal conditional, where the judgment applies to all instances
جزئية (juz'iyya): Particular conditional, where the judgment applies to some instances
شخصيَّة (shakhsiyya): Personal conditional, where the judgment applies to a specific individual
مُهمَلَة (muhmala): Indefinite conditional, where quantity is not specified
لزوم (luzum): Implication, the relation in conjunctive conditionals
عناد ('inad): Opposition, the relation in disjunctive conditionals (especially haqiqiyya)
اتفاق (ittifaq): Agreement, the relation in accidental conditionals

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which types of relations are considered in the division of conditionals by quantity?
A) Only implication
B) Only opposition
C) Implication, opposition, and agreement
D) Only agreement

Key Takeaway
Conditionals are quantified by the scope of their judgment: universal (all instances), particular (some), personal (one specific), or indefinite (unspecified).

The Three Judgments in Conditional Propositions
Sharh on judgments in conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly classify any conditional proposition as expressing necessity (luzum), incompatibility ('inad), or agreement (ittifaq).

Core Idea
Every conditional proposition embeds one of three relations: necessity, incompatibility, or mere agreement.

In this passage, the commentator explains that the judgment in conditionals is not one but three types. Depending on whether the conditional is conjunctive or disjunctive, the relation differs. Let's explore these three and see how they apply to Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Determine whether the proposition is conjunctive (muttasila, 'if-then') or disjunctive (munfasila, 'either-or').
Examples: 'If the sun rises, then it is day' is conjunctive; 'Either the world is eternal or temporally originated' is disjunctive.
2
Determine the relation in conjunctive conditionals
If the antecedent always necessitates the consequent in all times and states, it is necessity (luzum). If it merely happens to be true, it is agreement (ittifaq).
Necessity: 'Whenever God exists, He is knowing.' Agreement: 'If man is an animal, then the sun is hot.'
3
Determine the relation in disjunctive conditionals
If the parts cannot both be true, it is man'at al-jam' (incompatibility of conjunction). If they cannot both be false, it is man'at al-khuluw (incompatibility of emptiness). If neither, it is agreement.
Example of man'at al-jam': 'Either this is black or white.' Example of man'at al-khuluw: 'Either this is black or not a writer.'
4
Apply the commentator's clarification
The commentator notes that 'ghayrihima' (other than them) refers to agreement. Some scholars suggest omitting it because the discussion focuses on necessity and incompatibility.
Reflect on why agreement is often considered less important in logical analysis.

Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the classification of conditional propositions (shartiyya). It begins by discussing the kinds of judgments (ahkam) that can be made in conditional propositions: necessity (luzum) in conjunctive conditionals (muttasila), incompatibility ('inad) in disjunctive conditionals (munfasila), and agreement (ittifaq) which is common to both. The commentator clarifies that 'ghayrihima' (other than them) refers to agreement. The passage then provides examples of two sub-types of disjunctive conditionals: man'at al-jam' (preventive of conjunction) and man'at al-khuluw (preventive of emptiness). An example is given: 'Either this is black or not a writer' shows man'at al-khuluw because both can be true (if he is black and not a writer). The commentator corrects a confusion about the appropriateness of including 'ghayrihima', noting that the discussion is only about necessity and incompatibility conditionals, so it would be better to omit the phrase. A note suggests that the conjunction 'aw' (or) should be replaced with 'waw' (and). Then a new chapter (fasl) on the divisions of the conditional begins. The phrase 'in kana 'ala jami' al-taqadir' (if it is upon all estimations) is explained: 'taqadir' (estimations) are the times and states/conditions (awda') that pass over the antecedent. The commentator addresses a potential misunderstanding: universal conditional propositions can have non-temporal antecedents (e.g., 'Whenever God existed, He is knowing') or antecedents that are time itself (e.g., 'Whenever time existed, the sphere is moving'). This does not contradict the definition because the necessity still holds for all times in the sense of concurrent existence. The author of the original (al-musannif) notes that mentioning 'all states' suffices to include times and circumstances.

لُزُوم
عَنَاد
اتِّفَاق
مَنْفَصِلَة
مُتَّصِلَة

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which judgment applies to conjunctive conditionals?
A) Necessity and agreement
B) Incompatibility and agreement
C) Necessity only
D) Incompatibility only

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions carry one of three relations: necessity (for conjunctive), incompatibility (for disjunctive), or agreement (for both), but agreement is often excluded from logical analysis.

(2) 'Either the marriage is valid or it is invalid.' (3) 'If the sun rises, then the prayer time begins.' Identify the type of judgment for each.

Disjunctive Conditionals: Mani'at al-Jam' and Mani'at al-Khuluw
Examples of disjunctive conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between preventive of conjunction (mani'at al-jam') and preventive of emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw) in disjunctive conditionals and evaluate their truth conditions.

Core Idea
In disjunctive conditionals, the key is whether the parts cannot both be true or cannot both be false.

This passage provides detailed examples of the two main types of disjunctive conditionals: those that prevent both parts from being true (mani'at al-jam') and those that prevent both from being false (mani'at al-khuluw). Mastering this distinction is crucial for understanding exclusive and inclusive 'or' in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive proposition
Look for the particle 'إمّا' (either...or) in the conditional. Determine the two parts (disjuncts).
Example: 'Either this is black or not a writer.'
2
Check the truth possibilities
Determine if the two parts can be true together, false together, or both. Mani'at al-jam' means they cannot both be true (at least one is false). Mani'at al-khuluw means they cannot both be false (at least one is true).
If both parts are true in reality, then it is not mani'at al-jam'.
3
Apply the definitions to the example
Given the assumption that the man is black and not a writer, the proposition 'Either this is black or not a writer' is mani'at al-khuluw because both parts are true; they cannot be false together.
If the assumption were different, the classification could change.
4
Contrast with other types
A proposition can also be exclusive (both cannot be true and cannot be false) which is the strongest form, or inclusive (neither condition holds).
The passage focuses on the two basic types; agreement (ittifaq) is the inclusive case.

:
(قَوْلُهُ : مِنَ الْأَزْمَانِ ... إِلَخ) بيان للتَّقادِيرِ، قال عبد الحكيم: لا يتوهَّمُ مِن هذا أنَّه يخرج منه القضايا الشَّرطيَّةُ الكُلّيَّةُ اللُّزوميَّةُ والعناديَّةُ الَّتي المقدَّمُ فيها غيرُ زماني؛ نحو: كُلَّمَا كانَ اللهُ موجوداً؛ كانَ عالِماً، أو نفسُ الزَّمانِ؛ نحو: كُلَّما كانَ الزَّمانُ موجوداً؛ كانَ الفلكُ مُتحرِّكاً، لأنَّ كَوْنَ الشَّيء غير زماني، بمعنى أنَّهُ غير واقع في الزَّمانِ وَلَا في ظرفِهِ؛ لا يُنافي أنْ يكونَ لزومُ الشَّيء له في جميعِ الأزمنة بمعنى مقارنتِهِ إيَّاهُ، وَلَا كونُهُ نفسَ الزَّمانِ أنْ يكونَ لزوم شيءٍ له فى أجزائه .ا.هـ. هذا وقد قال المصنِّفُ في شرح الأصل: جميعُ الأوضاعِ مُغنٍ عن ذكرِ الأزمنة والأحوالِ والتَّقاديرِ؛ لأنَّهُ في كُلِّ زمانٍ وَعَلى كُلِّ حالٍ، وتقدير لا يخلو عن فَثبوتُ الحُكْم على جميعِ الأوضاع يستلزم ثبوته في جميعِ الأزمان والأحوالِ والتَّقادير.
(قَوْلُهُ : وَالأَوْضَاعِ) أي: الأحوال، قال عبد الحكيمِ: لَمَّا كانَ الوضعُ اللُّغوي
Division of the conditional into universal, particular, individual, and indefinite. Then the judgment, by necessity, incompatibility, and other than them, in the conditional, both conjunctive and disjunctive. Al-Dasuqi: And writer; because the assumption is that he is black, but they are both false due to the non-existence of non-black and writer together in reality; because the assumption is that he is black not a writer. And if it is said: Either this is black or not a writer; it would be preventive of emptiness; because they are not both false, due to the non-realization of non-black and writing in reality according to the assumption, and they are both true due to the realization of blackness and non-writing according to reality. End of Yasin. His statement: 'by necessity' means in the conjunctive; and his statement: 'and incompatibility' means in the disjunctive; and his statement: 'and other than them' which is agreement in the agreement type, whether conjunctive or disjunctive. And his statement: in the conjunctive, refers to necessity and agreement. And his statement: Al-‘Attar: and the disjunctive, refers to incompatibility and agreement. It was preventive of conjunction because they are not both true but are both false due to the non-existence of non-black and writing together in reality. And if it is said: Either this is black or not a writer; it would be preventive of emptiness because they are not both false and are both true due to the realization of blackness and non-writing according to reality. Thus in the gloss. It is said: That is inappropriate for the commentator's statement in this example. The good response is that this example is for the real [conditional] if said in the black non-writer, as the commentator said: It is the very example for preventive of conjunction if we say it in the white non-writer, and it is the very example for preventive of emptiness if we say it in the black writer. End. And the matter in that is easy. His statement: 'and other than them' which is agreement in the conjunctive and disjunctive; thus it is said. In it is that this discussion does not relate except to the conjunctive of necessity and the disjunctive of incompatibility, so it would be more appropriate to delete 'or other than them'; reflect. His statement: 'or the disjunctive' preferred is 'waw'. Chapter: On the divisions of the conditional. 'If it is upon all estimations' of times and states. Al-Dasuqi: And his saying 'If it is... etc.' is the predicate of the judgment. And his saying 'constant' is preferred to be estimated between 'was' and 'upon', for it is dependent on that which is the predicate of 'was' and it is not a dependent of the antecedent as its delay to it suggests. Rather its dependent is 'the constant' which is a description of the estimations. And the estimation: 'If it is constant upon all estimations constant for the antecedent.' And perhaps what occurred in the correction is a scribal error. End from Sheikh Yasin. His statement: 'upon all estimations'—'upon' here and in what follows in the commentator means 'with'. And his statement: 'of times' is an explanation of estimations. He meant by times: the times that pass over the antecedent. His statement: 'and states' i.e., circumstances. And its conjunction to what precedes is a conjunction of the general to the specific, because what is meant by states are the circumstances occurring to it, i.e., to the antecedent, due to its conjunction with matters. Al-‘Attar: His statement: 'of times... etc.' is an explanation of the estimations. Abd al-Hakim said: It should not be imagined from this that there are excluded from it the universal conditional propositions of necessity and incompatibility whose antecedent is non-temporal, like: 'Whenever God existed, He is knowing' or time itself, like: 'Whenever time existed, the sphere is moving', because the thing being non-temporal, meaning it does not occur in time nor in its container, does not negate that the thing's necessity is for it in all times in the sense of its concurrence with it, nor its being time itself that the necessity of something for it is in its parts. End. And here the author said in the commentary on the original: 'All states' is sufficient from mentioning times, circumstances, and estimations, because it is in every time and in every condition, and an estimation is not free from, so the establishment of the judgment upon all states necessitates its establishment in all times and circumstances and estimations. His statement: 'and states' i.e., circumstances. Abd al-Hakim said: Since the linguistic term...
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the classification of conditional propositions (shartiyya). It begins by discussing the kinds of judgments (ahkam) that can be made in conditional propositions: necessity (luzum) in conjunctive conditionals (muttasila), incompatibility ('inad) in disjunctive conditionals (munfasila), and agreement (ittifaq) which is common to both. The commentator clarifies that 'ghayrihima' (other than them) refers to agreement. The passage then provides examples of two sub-types of disjunctive conditionals: man'at al-jam' (preventive of conjunction) and man'at al-khuluw (preventive of emptiness). An example is given: 'Either this is black or not a writer' shows man'at al-khuluw because both can be true (if he is black and not a writer). The commentator corrects a confusion about the appropriateness of including 'ghayrihima', noting that the discussion is only about necessity and incompatibility conditionals, so it would be better to omit the phrase. A note suggests that the conjunction 'aw' (or) should be replaced with 'waw' (and). Then a new chapter (fasl) on the divisions of the conditional begins. The phrase 'in kana 'ala jami' al-taqadir' (if it is upon all estimations) is explained: 'taqadir' (estimations) are the times and states/conditions (awda') that pass over the antecedent. The commentator addresses a potential misunderstanding: universal conditional propositions can have non-temporal antecedents (e.g., 'Whenever God existed, He is knowing') or antecedents that are time itself (e.g., 'Whenever time existed, the sphere is moving'). This does not contradict the definition because the necessity still holds for all times in the sense of concurrent existence. The author of the original (al-musannif) notes that mentioning 'all states' suffices to include times and circumstances.

مَانِعَة الْجَمْع
مَانِعَة الْخُلُوّ
عناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Given the assumption that a man is black and a writer, the proposition 'Either this is black or not a writer' will be:
A) Mani'at al-jam'
B) Mani'at al-khuluw
C) Both true and false
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The two fundamental types of disjunctive conditionals are determined by whether the disjuncts cannot both be true (man'at al-jam') or cannot both be false (man'at al-khuluw).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the fiqhi statement: 'Either the fasting is valid or it is invalid.' Under which assumption would this be mani'at al-jam'? Under which assumption would it be mani'at al-khuluw? Provide a brief explanation.

Propositions
advanced
12 min
Universal Conditionals and the Condition of Estimations
Definition of universal conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to interpret the condition 'upon all estimations' (ʻalā jamīʻ al-taqādīr) that defines universal conditional propositions and apply it to examples including non-temporal antecedents.

Core Idea
A universal conditional holds in every time and state that the antecedent can be in.

This passage defines when a conditional proposition is considered universal: it must hold 'upon all estimations' of times and states. The commentator clarifies that even non-temporal antecedents (like God's existence) can satisfy this condition. This is crucial for understanding universal logical statements in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional proposition
Find a conditional statement that claims universality, often beginning with 'كُلَّمَا' (whenever).
Example from kalam: 'Whenever God exists, He is knowing.'
2
Understand 'upon all estimations'
This means the judgment holds for every possible time (zaman) and state (wad') that the antecedent can have.
Estimations (taqadir) include times and circumstances.
3
Check for non-temporal antecedents
Some antecedents are timeless (e.g., God's existence) or are time itself. The commentator says this does not affect universality because the judgment still holds in all times concurrently.
Example: 'Whenever time exists, the sphere is moving' – the antecedent is time itself.
4
Apply the condition
If the judgment holds in every estimation, the proposition is universal (kulliyya). If not, it is particular or indefinite.
The author of the original text notes that mentioning 'all states' is sufficient to cover times and estimations.

(قَوْلُهُ : مِنَ الْأَزْمَانِ ... إِلَخ) بيان للتَّقادِيرِ، قال عبد الحكيم: لا يتوهَّمُ مِن هذا أنَّه يخرج منه القضايا الشَّرطيَّةُ الكُلّيَّةُ اللُّزوميَّةُ والعناديَّةُ الَّتي المقدَّمُ فيها غيرُ زماني؛ نحو: كُلَّمَا كانَ اللهُ موجوداً؛ كانَ عالِماً، أو نفسُ الزَّمانِ؛ نحو: كُلَّما كانَ الزَّمانُ موجوداً؛ كانَ الفلكُ مُتحرِّكاً، لأنَّ كَوْنَ الشَّيء غير زماني، بمعنى أنَّهُ غير واقع في الزَّمانِ وَلَا في ظرفِهِ؛ لا يُنافي أنْ يكونَ لزومُ الشَّيء له في جميعِ الأزمنة بمعنى مقارنتِهِ إيَّاهُ، وَلَا كونُهُ نفسَ الزَّمانِ أنْ يكونَ لزوم شيءٍ له فى أجزائه .ا.هـ. هذا وقد قال المصنِّفُ في شرح الأصل: جميعُ الأوضاعِ مُغنٍ عن ذكرِ الأزمنة والأحوالِ والتَّقاديرِ؛ لأنَّهُ في كُلِّ زمانٍ وَعَلى كُلِّ حالٍ، وتقدير لا يخلو عن فَثبوتُ الحُكْم على جميعِ الأوضاع يستلزم ثبوته في جميعِ الأزمان والأحوالِ والتَّقادير.
(قَوْلُهُ : وَالأَوْضَاعِ) أي: الأحوال، قال عبد الحكيمِ: لَمَّا كانَ الوضعُ اللُّغوي
Division of the conditional into universal, particular, individual, and indefinite. Then the judgment, by necessity, incompatibility, and other than them, in the conditional, both conjunctive and disjunctive. Al-Dasuqi: And writer; because the assumption is that he is black, but they are both false due to the non-existence of non-black and writer together in reality; because the assumption is that he is black not a writer. And if it is said: Either this is black or not a writer; it would be preventive of emptiness; because they are not both false, due to the non-realization of non-black and writing in reality according to the assumption, and they are both true due to the realization of blackness and non-writing according to reality. End of Yasin. His statement: 'by necessity' means in the conjunctive; and his statement: 'and incompatibility' means in the disjunctive; and his statement: 'and other than them' which is agreement in the agreement type, whether conjunctive or disjunctive. And his statement: in the conjunctive, refers to necessity and agreement. And his statement: Al-‘Attar: and the disjunctive, refers to incompatibility and agreement. It was preventive of conjunction because they are not both true but are both false due to the non-existence of non-black and writing together in reality. And if it is said: Either this is black or not a writer; it would be preventive of emptiness because they are not both false and are both true due to the realization of blackness and non-writing according to reality. Thus in the gloss. It is said: That is inappropriate for the commentator's statement in this example. The good response is that this example is for the real [conditional] if said in the black non-writer, as the commentator said: It is the very example for preventive of conjunction if we say it in the white non-writer, and it is the very example for preventive of emptiness if we say it in the black writer. End. And the matter in that is easy. His statement: 'and other than them' which is agreement in the conjunctive and disjunctive; thus it is said. In it is that this discussion does not relate except to the conjunctive of necessity and the disjunctive of incompatibility, so it would be more appropriate to delete 'or other than them'; reflect. His statement: 'or the disjunctive' preferred is 'waw'. Chapter: On the divisions of the conditional. 'If it is upon all estimations' of times and states. Al-Dasuqi: And his saying 'If it is... etc.' is the predicate of the judgment. And his saying 'constant' is preferred to be estimated between 'was' and 'upon', for it is dependent on that which is the predicate of 'was' and it is not a dependent of the antecedent as its delay to it suggests. Rather its dependent is 'the constant' which is a description of the estimations. And the estimation: 'If it is constant upon all estimations constant for the antecedent.' And perhaps what occurred in the correction is a scribal error. End from Sheikh Yasin. His statement: 'upon all estimations'—'upon' here and in what follows in the commentator means 'with'. And his statement: 'of times' is an explanation of estimations. He meant by times: the times that pass over the antecedent. His statement: 'and states' i.e., circumstances. And its conjunction to what precedes is a conjunction of the general to the specific, because what is meant by states are the circumstances occurring to it, i.e., to the antecedent, due to its conjunction with matters. Al-‘Attar: His statement: 'of times... etc.' is an explanation of the estimations. Abd al-Hakim said: It should not be imagined from this that there are excluded from it the universal conditional propositions of necessity and incompatibility whose antecedent is non-temporal, like: 'Whenever God existed, He is knowing' or time itself, like: 'Whenever time existed, the sphere is moving', because the thing being non-temporal, meaning it does not occur in time nor in its container, does not negate that the thing's necessity is for it in all times in the sense of its concurrence with it, nor its being time itself that the necessity of something for it is in its parts. End. And here the author said in the commentary on the original: 'All states' is sufficient from mentioning times, circumstances, and estimations, because it is in every time and in every condition, and an estimation is not free from, so the establishment of the judgment upon all states necessitates its establishment in all times and circumstances and estimations. His statement: 'and states' i.e., circumstances. Abd al-Hakim said: Since the linguistic term...
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the classification of conditional propositions (shartiyya). It begins by discussing the kinds of judgments (ahkam) that can be made in conditional propositions: necessity (luzum) in conjunctive conditionals (muttasila), incompatibility ('inad) in disjunctive conditionals (munfasila), and agreement (ittifaq) which is common to both. The commentator clarifies that 'ghayrihima' (other than them) refers to agreement. The passage then provides examples of two sub-types of disjunctive conditionals: man'at al-jam' (preventive of conjunction) and man'at al-khuluw (preventive of emptiness). An example is given: 'Either this is black or not a writer' shows man'at al-khuluw because both can be true (if he is black and not a writer). The commentator corrects a confusion about the appropriateness of including 'ghayrihima', noting that the discussion is only about necessity and incompatibility conditionals, so it would be better to omit the phrase. A note suggests that the conjunction 'aw' (or) should be replaced with 'waw' (and). Then a new chapter (fasl) on the divisions of the conditional begins. The phrase 'in kana 'ala jami' al-taqadir' (if it is upon all estimations) is explained: 'taqadir' (estimations) are the times and states/conditions (awda') that pass over the antecedent. The commentator addresses a potential misunderstanding: universal conditional propositions can have non-temporal antecedents (e.g., 'Whenever God existed, He is knowing') or antecedents that are time itself (e.g., 'Whenever time existed, the sphere is moving'). This does not contradict the definition because the necessity still holds for all times in the sense of concurrent existence. The author of the original (al-musannif) notes that mentioning 'all states' suffices to include times and circumstances.

أَوْضَاع
كُلِّيَّة
شَرْطِيَّة

Key Takeaway
A universal conditional must hold in every estimation of times and states, and this includes antecedents that are timeless or are time itself.

Definition of Universal Conditional (Shartiyya Kulliyya)
Definition and explanation of universal conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for a universal conditional, distinguish between its constituent terms (times and states), and correctly parse its grammatical structure.

Core Idea
A universal conditional holds in every conceivable time and state for its antecedent.

This passage introduces the universal conditional, a key proposition type in logic. The author provides a precise definition and illustrates it with a classic example. He then clarifies the scope of 'all situations' by breaking it down into times and states, and even fine-tunes the grammatical reading to avoid ambiguity. Mastering this definition equips you to evaluate conditionals used in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the sentence that begins with 'إِنْ كَانَ عَلَى جَمِيعِ التَّقَادِيْرِ...' and ends with 'فَكُلِّيَّةٌ'. This is the core definition: a conditional is universal if the consequent is established for the antecedent in all situations.
2
Examine the example
The example 'كُلَّمَا كَانَ زَيْدٌ إِنْسَاناً فَهُوَ حَيَوَانٌ' shows a universal conditional: animality necessarily accompanies humanity in every time and state.
3
Understand 'azman' and 'awda''
The author clarifies that 'all situations' includes times (الأزمان) and states (الأوضاع). Times are the temporal moments the antecedent passes through; states are the accompanying conditions (e.g., standing, sleeping).
States are considered general with respect to times.
4
Correct the grammatical role of 'thābitan'
The word 'ثَابِتاً' should be placed as the predicate of 'كَانَ', not as an attribute of the antecedent. This ensures the reading is 'if it is established in all situations that are established for the antecedent' rather than a misinterpretation.
This grammatical move preserves the logical scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ. إِنْ كَانَ عَلَى جَمِيعِ التَّقَادِيْرِ مِنَ الْأَزْمَانِ وَالْأَوْضَاعِ ثَابِتاً لِلْمُقَدَّمِ فَكُلِّيَّةٌ. أَيْ: فَالشَّرْطِيَّةُ كُلِّيَّةٌ. كَقَوْلِنَا: كُلَّمَا كَانَ زَيْدٌ إِنْسَاناً فَهُوَ حَيَوَانٌ. فَالْحُكْمُ بِلُزُوْمِ الْحَيَوَانِيَّةِ لِلْإِنْسَانِ ثَابِتٌ عَلَى جَمِيعِ التَّقَادِيرِ مِنَ الْأَزْمَانِ وَالْأَوْضَاعِ. قَوْلُهُ: عَلَى جَمِيعِ هُنَا بِمَعْنَى «مَعَ». وَقَوْلُهُ: مِنَ الْأَزْمَانِ بَيَانٌ لِلتَّقَادِيرِ، وَأَرَادَ بِالْأَزْمَانِ: الْأَزْمَانَ الَّتِي تَمُرُّ عَلَى الْمُقَدَّمِ. وَقَوْلُهُ: وَالْأَوْضَاعُ أَيِ: الْأَحْوَالُ، وَالْمُرَادُ بِالْأَوْضَاعِ الْأَحْوَالُ الْحَاصِلَةُ لِلْمُقَدَّمِ بِسَبَبِ اقْتِرَانِهِ مَعَ الْأُمُورِ. وَقَوْلُهُ: ثَابِتاً الْأَوْلَى أَنْ يُقَدِّرَهُ بَيْنَ كَانَ وَعَلَى، فَإِنَّهُ مُتَعَلِّقٌ عَلَى الَّذِي هُوَ خَبَرُ كَانَ، وَلَيْسَ هُوَ مُتَعَلِّقاً لِلْمُقَدَّمِ كَمَا يُوهِمُهُ تَأْخِيرُهُ إِلَيْهِ، بَلْ مُتَعَلِّقُهُ الثَّابِتَةُ الَّذِي هُوَ صِفَةٌ لِلتَّقَادِيرِ. وَالتَّقْدِيرُ: إِنْ كَانَ ثَابِتاً عَلَى جَمِيعِ التَّقَادِيرِ الثَّابِتةِ لِلْمُقَدَّمِ.
Chapter: On the Divisions of the Conditional. If [the consequent] is established for the antecedent in all situations of times and states, then [the conditional] is universal. That is: the conditional is universal. Like our saying: Whenever Zayd is a human, he is an animal. So the judgment of the necessity of animality for humanity is established in all situations of times and states. His statement: 'في جميع' here means 'with'. And his statement: 'من الأزمان' is an explanation of 'situations', and he meant by 'times' the times that pass over the antecedent. And his statement: 'والأوضاع' i.e. states, and what is meant by 'states' are the states that occur to the antecedent due to its conjunction with things. And his statement: 'ثابتاً' it is better to place it between 'كان' and 'على', for it is attached to the predicate of 'كان', and it is not attached to the antecedent as its delay to it might suggest; rather its attachment is to 'الثابتة' which is an adjective for 'التقادير'. And the reconstruction is: If it is established in all the situations that are established for the antecedent.
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage defines the universal conditional (شَرْطِيَّة كُلِّيَّة) and gives its conditions. It begins with a chapter heading on the divisions of conditionals. The definition states: if the consequence (the judgment of necessity) is established for the antecedent across all situations (times and states), then the conditional is universal. The author then clarifies that 'in all situations' means 'together with' the antecedent at all times and in all states. He explains that 'times' refers to the temporal moments that the antecedent goes through, and 'states' (أوضاع) refers to the various conditions or circumstances that may coincide with the antecedent, such as eating, drinking, standing, etc. These states are general (عام) with respect to times. A common misunderstanding is that this definition might exclude conditionals with a non-temporal antecedent (like 'Whenever God exists, He is knowing') or with time itself as antecedent (like 'Whenever time exists, the celestial sphere moves'). The author clarifies that even if the antecedent is not temporal, the necessity of the consequent being concomitant in all times (i.e., at every moment) is sufficient; similarly, if the antecedent is time itself, the necessity holds in all its parts. The passage also addresses the grammatical point of the word 'ثابتاً' (established): it is best analyzed as the predicate of 'كان' (was) and not as an attribute of 'المقدم' (the antecedent), to avoid confusion. The example provided is the classic one: 'Whenever Zayd is human, he is an animal.' Here, the relationship (luzūm) between humanity and animality holds across all times and states, making the conditional universal. This definition is essential for distinguishing universal from particular conditionals in logic.

Key Terms
شَرْطِيَّة كُلِّيَّة
تَقَادِير
أَوْضَاع
لُزُوم

D) Sometimes when Zayd sleeps, he dreams.

Key Takeaway
The universal conditional is defined by the necessity of the consequent for the antecedent in all times and states, and its proper understanding requires attention to both the conceptual scope and grammatical parsing.
