Why Conditionals Consider Only Possible States with the Antecedent
Author explains why conditional propositions consider only conditions possible with the antecedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logicians restrict the positions (awḍāʿ) in conditional propositions to those that can possibly combine with the antecedent, and apply this restriction to examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Without the possibility restriction, every universal conditional would be false.

In this passage, the commentators explain a subtle but crucial point about conditional propositions: when evaluating a conditional like 'If A then B', we do not consider all imaginable states, but only those that can coexist with the antecedent. This ensures that the conditional is not automatically false. The example 'Whenever the horse were a human, it would be an animal' shows how this works even with an impossible antecedent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core logical problem
The text states that if positions (states) were not restricted, every universal conditional would be false. Why? Because one could assume a position where the antecedent is true and the consequent false (for conjunctive conditionals) or where the antecedent is true and the consequent is not incompatible (for disjunctive conditionals). This would violate the intended meaning.
2
Define the restriction 'possible conjunction with the antecedent'
The restriction is not that the positions themselves must be possible in reality, but only that they can coexist with the antecedent. For example, 'horse is human' is impossible, but positions like 'laughing' or 'writing' can coexist with the assumption of humanity. These are the positions considered.
3
Apply to conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals
For a conjunctive conditional like 'Whenever the horse were a human, it would be an animal', we consider only positions that can combine with 'being human' (e.g., laughing, writing). For a disjunctive conditional like 'The number is either even or odd', we consider positions that can combine with 'evenness' (e.g., being multiple of 2).
The same logic applies to non-real conditionals (ghayr haqiqiyya).
4
State the principle behind the restriction
Without the restriction, the conditional would require the antecedent to entail or be incompatible with two contradictories in some positions, which is impossible. Therefore, the restriction is necessary for the conditional to be meaningful and sometimes true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ
الممكنة الاجتماع مع المقدَّمِ.
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : المُمْكِنَةِ الاجتماع ... إلخ) احترازُ عَنِ الأحوال الغير الممكنة الاجتماع مع المقدَّمِ ؛ كَكَونِ المقدَّمِ حجراً ؛ فإنَّ الحيوانية لا تثبتُ لهُ في تلك الحالة، وَكَعَدَمِ الحيوانية ؛ فإنَّ الحيوانية لا تستلزم الإنسانية على تقدير كونها مع عدم الحيوانية.
وفي هذا إشارة إلى تقييدِ الأوضاع فيما تقدَّمَ في كلام المصنّف بالأوضاع الممكنة الاجتماع مع المقدَّم ، وقولُه : (مطلقاً)؛ حالٌ مِن (بعض).
العطار
على مَا اشتهر أنَّ الظُّروف بعد المعارفِ أحوال ، ولعدم خفاء تقدير متعلّق على الأحوال؛ لم يُقدِّرُهُ كَمَا قُدِّرَ مُتعلّق للمقدَّم ا.هـ. والحق أنَّ الوجهين سَائغانِ، فلا مزية لأحدِهِمَا على الآخَرِ وَلَا فساد.
جميعِ
(قَوْلُهُ : : المُمْكِنَةِ الاِجْتِمَاعِ مَعَ الْمُقَدَّمِ إشارة إلى أنَّ هذا القيد مُلاحظ في كلام المصنّف ، ولم يذكره استغناءً بِشهرتِهِ، قال المصنِّفُ في شرح الرسالة : وَلَمْ يُشترط إمكان تلك الأوضاع في نفسها ليشمل ما إذا كانَ المقدَّمُ كاذباً كَقولنا : كُلَّمَا كَانَ الفَرَسُ إنساناً ؛ كانَ حيواناً، فإنَّ معناه لزوم حيوانيَّةِ الفرس للإنسانية مع جميع الأوضاع التي يمكن اجتماعها مع إنسانيَّةِ الفرس ؛ مِن كَونِهِ ضاحكاً وكاتباً وناطقاً إلى غير ذلك، وإن كانت محالةٌ في نفسها ، وإذا قُلْنَا : إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو فرداً ؛ فَمعناه تنافي فرديَّتِهِ لِزَوجِيَّتِهِ مع جميع الأوضاع التي يمكن اجتماعُهَا معَ الزَّوجيَّةِ، وَكَذا قياسُ غيرِ الحقيقية، وإنّما قيَّد الأوضاع بإمكان الاجتماع معَ المقدَّم ؛ لئلا يلزمَ مِن إطلاقها وتعميمها أن لا تصدقَ كُلِّيَّةُ الشَّرطيَّةِ أصلاً، لأنَّ بعض الأوضاع مِمَّا لا يصح معه اللُّزوم والعناد، وهو ما إذا فُرِضَ المقدَّمَ معَ عدمِ التّالي أو معَ عدمِ لزومِ التّالي له، بلْ معَ لزومِ نقيض التّالي له؛ فإِنَّهُ حينئذٍ لا يلزم التّالي؛ ضرورة امتناع استلزام الشَّيء لِلنَّقيضَيْنِ ، وَكَذَا إِذا فُرِضَ المقدَّمُ مع وجودِ التَّالي أو مع عدم عنادِهِ إِيَّاهُ ، بلْ مِعَ عنادِهِ لِنَقيض التّالي؛ لا يكونُ التّالي مُعانداً له؛ لامتناع معاندةِ الشَّيء لِلنَّقيضين .ا.هـ.
Section: On the divisions of the conditional proposition. The possible conjunction with the antecedent. Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'the possible conjunction... etc.') is a restriction from the states that are not possible to combine with the antecedent; such as the antecedent being a stone, for animality is not established for it in that state; and such as the absence of animality, for animality does not necessitate humanity on the assumption that it is together with the absence of animality. And in this is an indication of restricting the positions in what preceded in the author's speech to the positions possible to combine with the antecedent, and his statement (absolutely) is a state from (some). Al-Attar: According to what is well-known that the circumstantial particles after definite nouns are states, and because of the lack of hiddenness of estimating a relation for the states, he did not estimate it just as a relation was estimated for the antecedent. End. And the truth is that both aspects are permissible, so there is no preference of one over the other nor corruption. Allamah? (His statement: 'the possible conjunction with the antecedent' is an indication that this restriction is observed in the author's speech, and he did not mention it relying on its fame. The author said in his commentary on the Risalah: 'And the possibility of those positions in themselves was not stipulated, to include when the antecedent is false, like our saying: Whenever the horse were a human, it would be an animal, for its meaning is the necessary concomitance of the animality of the horse to humanity with all the positions that can combine with the humanity of the horse; such as its being laughing, writing, speaking, etc., even if they are impossible in themselves. And if we say: The number is either even or odd, its meaning is the incompatibility of its oddness to its evenness with all the positions that can combine with evenness, and likewise the analogy for non-real conditionals. And he only restricted the positions to the possibility of conjunction with the antecedent so that from their absoluteness and generality it would not necessarily follow that the universal conditional is never true, because some positions are such that concomitance and incompatibility are not sound with them, that is, when the antecedent is assumed with the non-existence of the consequent or with the non-necessity of the consequent for it, rather with the necessity of the contradictory of the consequent for it; for then the consequent does not follow, due to the impossibility of a thing entailing two contradictories. And likewise, if the antecedent is assumed with the existence of the consequent or with its not being incompatible with it, rather with its incompatibility with the contradictory of the consequent, the consequent is not incompatible with it, due to the impossibility of a thing being incompatible with two contradictories. End.'
Source pages p. 621

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the section on divisions of conditional propositions (al-shartiyyah). The main point is to clarify the restriction that the 'positions' (al-awḍāʿ) considered in evaluating the truth of a conditional must be those that can possibly combine with the antecedent. The author explains that this restriction is implicit in the original text and serves to avoid making all universal conditionals false. He gives examples: 'Whenever the horse were a human, it would be an animal' — here the antecedent is false, but the conditional is still meaningful because we consider only positions (like being laughing, writing) that can coexist with 'humanity' in the horse, even if that humanity itself is impossible. Similarly, in a disjunctive conditional like 'The number is either even or odd', we consider only positions that can combine with evenness. If we did not restrict positions, then for any conditional, we could assume a position where the antecedent is true and the consequent false (or where the antecedent is true and the contradictory of the consequent holds), which would make the conditional false. The commentary by al-Dasūqī and al-ʿAṭṭār elaborates on the meaning of 'possible conjunction' and the treatment of circumstantial particles.

Partial vs Personal Conditionals
Author distinguishes partial from personal conditional propositions based on specificity of conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between partial (juz'iyyah) and personal (shakhsiyyah) conditional propositions and correctly classify given examples.

Core Idea
Partial conditionals leave the condition vague; personal conditionals name it.

In the study of conditional propositions (al-shartiyyah), logicians divide them based on whether the judgment (entailment or incompatibility) holds for all conditions, some conditions, or specific conditions. This passage focuses on the case where the judgment holds for some conditions — it is either partial (juz'iyyah) if the conditions are left unspecified, or personal (shakhsiyyah) if a specific condition is mentioned. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting conditional arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion
The key criterion is whether the judgment (hukm) applies to some conditions of the antecedent without specifying which, or to a specific mentioned condition. If the judgment is not universal (i.e., not for all conditions), it falls under this division.
2
Recognize partial conditionals
A partial conditional is one where the judgment holds for some conditions absolutely (muṭlaqan), meaning no particular condition is singled out. The example given is: 'It may be that when a thing is an animal or a human.' Here, the entailment of being human is not always true; it holds only when the thing is actually human, but that condition is not explicitly stated.
The Arabic term juz'iyyah is used.
3
Recognize personal conditionals
A personal conditional is one where the judgment holds for a specific condition that is explicitly mentioned in the proposition. The example: 'If you come to me today I will honor you.' The condition 'today' is stated, so the entailment is limited to that specific time.
The Arabic term shakhsiyyah is used.
4
Test with Islamic examples
Apply the distinction to sample conditional statements from Islamic sciences. For instance, in fiqh: 'If the sale is valid, ownership transfers.' Is this partial or personal? It does not specify a condition, so it is partial if there are unstated conditions. Compare with: 'If the sale is valid and the buyer pays, ownership transfers.' The added condition makes it closer to personal if the condition is specific.
Practice classifying and justifying.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

- على بعض التَّقاديرِ والأزمانِ مُطلقاً

(قَوْلُهُ: «مُطْلَقَاً) أي : مُبهماً غيرَ مُعيَّن بأنْ لمْ يُذكَرْ في القضيَّةِ، وقولُهُ: (مُعيَّناً)؛ أي: بأنْ كانَ مَذكوراً في القضيَّةِ.
(قَوْلُهُ : إِنْ جِئْتَنِي الْيَوْمَ . . . إِلَخ) أي : فَلَزومُ التّالي للمقدَّم في بعض الأزمنة.

(قَوْلُهُ: إِنْ جِئْتَنِي الْيَوْمَ أَكْرَمْتُكَ) فَإِنَّ الحُكْمَ بِلزومِ الإكرامِ ليس إلا على الوضعِ المعيَّنِ مِن تلك الأوضاع، وهو المجيء اليوم، ومثال المنفصلة: هذا الشَّيء على تقديرِ كَونِهِ عدداً؛ إما أن يكون زوجاً أو فرداً ، فالحكم بالعنادِ فيها على وضع مُعيَّن،
Or some of them) with jarr, a conjunction on 'all conditions'; i.e., if the judgment is not established on all conditions of times and positions, but rather on some conditions and times, then it is not free from being:
- on some conditions and times absolutely
- or on some of them specifically.
If it is on some of them (absolutely) without specification, (then it is partial) like our saying: 'It may be that when a thing is an animal or a human,' for the judgment of entailment is not on all times and positions, but on some of them absolutely.
(Or specifically) a conjunction on his statement 'absolutely'; i.e., if the judgment is on some times specifically, (then it is personal) like our saying: 'If you come to me today I will honor you.'
Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'on some conditions and times') is a conjunction of specific on general, and it would be more appropriate to say: on some conditions of times and positions, as an explanation of conditions. (His statement: 'not on all times... etc.') i.e., the judgment of the consequent is with some states of the antecedent, namely its being rational, not with all of them; because among its states is that it may be together with animality a neighing, for example, and among the times is the time in which the humanity of the thing does not exist, but only its animality, for example.
(His statement: 'absolutely') i.e., ambiguous, not specified, in that it is not mentioned in the proposition. And his statement: (specifically) i.e., in that it is mentioned in the proposition.
(His statement: 'If you come to me today... etc.') i.e., the entailment of the consequent to the antecedent is in some times.
Al-Attar: (His statement: 'If you come to me today I will honor you') for the judgment of the entailment of honor is only on the specific position among those positions, which is coming today. And an example of the disjunctive: 'This thing, on the assumption of its being a number, is either even or odd,' so the judgment of incompatibility in it is on a specific position.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage continues the division of conditional propositions (al-shartiyyah). It deals with the case where the judgment (entailment or incompatibility) is not true for all conditions (times and positions) but only for some. The author splits this into two subtypes: if the judgment holds for some conditions without specifying which conditions, the conditional is called 'partial' (juz'iyyah); if the judgment holds for a specific condition that is mentioned in the proposition, it is called 'personal' (shakhsiyyah). Examples: 'It may be that when a thing is an animal or a human' — here the entailment is not always true; it is only true when the thing is actually human (one of the conditions), but the condition is not explicitly stated, so it is partial. In contrast, 'If you come to me today I will honor you' explicitly mentions the specific condition 'today', making it personal. Al-Dasūqī explains that 'on some conditions and times' is a conjunction of specific on general; the proper wording should be 'on some conditions from among times and positions' to clarify. Al-ʿAṭṭār gives an additional example for disjunctive conditionals: 'This thing, on the assumption of its being a number, is either even or odd' — the judgment of incompatibility is on the specific position of the thing being a number.

Classifying Conditional Propositions by Range
Author classifies conditional propositions based on range of situations and times.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a conditional proposition as universal, particular, or singular based on the range of situations and times over which its implication holds.

Core Idea
Just as categoricals are quantified by subjects, conditionals are quantified by situations and times.

The logical analysis of conditional propositions (al-shartiyya) requires more than just understanding their structure; it also requires evaluating the scope of their implication. Just as categorical propositions are universal or particular based on how many individuals they cover, conditional propositions have a parallel classification based on how many situations (awḍāʿ) and times (azmina) the implication covers. This lesson will guide you through that classification using the examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
Locate the 'if' clause (muqaddam) and the 'then' clause (tali) in the conditional statement. The implication (luzūm) connects them.
2
Check if the implication holds for all situations and times
Ask: Does the antecedent's occurrence always lead to the consequent, regardless of when or under what conditions? If yes, the conditional is universal (kulliyya). If not, proceed.
Universal example from the text: 'If the sun rises, day comes' (implied by the classification).
3
If not universal, determine whether the implication holds for some situations/times without specification
If the implication is true for some cases but not all, and no specific time or situation is singled out, then it is particular (juzʾiyya). Example: 'It may be that if something is an animal, it is a human' – the implication holds for some animals (humans) but not all, and no specific time is mentioned.
The word 'may be' (qad) indicates particularity.
4
If the implication holds for a specified time or situation, classify as singular
If the conditional includes a specific time or situation (e.g., 'today'), then it is singular (shakhṣiyya). Example: 'If you come to me today, I will honor you' – the implication is tied to a particular time.
Singular conditionals are analogous to singular categoricals like 'Zayd is standing'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي: إن لم يكن الحكم ثابتاً على جميع التقادير من الأزمان والأوضاع، بل يكون على بعض التقادير والأزمان، فلا يخلو من أن يكون : على بعض التقادير والأزمان مطلقاً أو على بعضها معيناً. فإن كان على بعضها مطلقاً من غير تعيين فجزئية نحو قولنا: قد يكون إذا كان الشيء حيواناً أو إنساناً، فإن الحكم باللزوم ليس على جميع الأزمان والأوضاع، بل على بعضها مطلقاً. أو معيناً عطف على قوله مطلقاً؛ أي: إن كان الحكم على بعض الأزمان معيناً فشخصية، كقولنا: إن جئتني اليوم أكرمتك. فصل: في أقسام الشرطية. فعلم أن الأوضاع والأزمان في الشرطية بمنزلة الأفراد في الحملية.
That is: If the judgment is not established for all situations from the times and states, but rather is for some situations and times, then it is inevitably either: for some situations and times absolutely, or for some of them specifically. So if it is for some of them absolutely without specification, then it is particular, like our saying: 'It may be that whenever a thing is an animal, it is a human', for the judgment of implication is not for all times and states but for some of them absolutely. Or specifically, as an apposition to his saying 'absolutely'; that is, if the judgment is for some times specifically, then it is singular, like our saying: 'If you come to me today, I will honor you.' Section: On the divisions of the conditional. So it is known that the states and times in the conditional are in the position of individuals in the categorical.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) explaining the classification of conditional propositions (al-shartiyya) into three types based on the range of situations (awḍāʿ) and times (azmina) under which the implication holds. The author begins by considering the case where the judgment of implication does not hold for all situations and times, but only for some. He then divides this into two subcases: first, if it holds for some situations and times absolutely (muṭlaqan), i.e., without specifying which ones, then the conditional is particular (juzʾiyya), exemplified by 'It may be that if something is an animal, it is a human'—the implication is not always true (since not all animals are human) but true in some unspecified cases. Second, if it holds for some times specifically (muʿayyanan), i.e., when the time is mentioned, then it is singular (shakhṣiyya), like 'If you come to me today, I will honor you'—the implication is tied to a particular time. The author then introduces a new section headed 'On the divisions of the conditional' and states the key analogy: the situations and times in conditionals serve the same role as individuals (afrād) in categorical propositions (al-ḥamliyya). Just as categorical propositions are universal, particular, or singular based on whether the judgment applies to all, some, or one individual, so too conditional propositions are classified based on whether the implication applies to all situations and times, some without specification, or a specified one. This framework allows logicians to systematically analyze conditional statements.

Key Terms
أوضاع (awḍāʿ): The states or conditions of the antecedent (muqaddam) that accompany it, analogous to subjects in categorical propositions. [Note: No significant shift; stable term in logic.]
أزمان (azmina): Times associated with the antecedent, considered alongside the situations (awḍāʿ) as the range over which the implication is evaluated. [Note: No significant shift.]
جزئية (juzʾiyya): A particular conditional proposition where the implication holds for some situations/times, but not specified which ones. [Note: Stable across logic texts.]
شخصية (shakhṣiyya): A singular conditional proposition where the implication holds for a specified situation or time. [Note: Stable.]
شرطية (sharṭiyya): A conditional proposition consisting of an antecedent and consequent joined by an implication (luzūm) or incompatibility (ʿinād). [Note: No significant shift.]

Classifying Conditional Propositions: Singular, Quantified, Indefinite
Classification of conditionals by time/circumstance quantification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any conditional proposition as singular/specified (shakhsiyya/makhsusa), quantified (mahsura), or indefinite (muhmala) by identifying the quantification of time or circumstance.

Core Idea
Conditionals are quantified by circumstances and times, not by subjects—a crucial difference from categoricals.

Just as categorical propositions are universal, particular, or singular based on the individuals of the subject, conditionals have their own quantification system. The key is to recognize that the judgment in a conditional is about concomitance or opposition, and its quantity depends on the times or circumstances that accompany the antecedent. This lesson will equip you to parse any conditional and assign it to the correct category.

Guided Path
1
Identify whether a specific time or circumstance is mentioned
Read the conditional and look for a specific time (e.g., 'today') or a specific circumstance (e.g., 'if you enter this mosque'). If such a specific is present, the conditional is shakhsiyya (singular) or makhsusa (specified).
Shakhsiyya and makhsusa are often used interchangeably here.
2
If no specific, check for explicit quantity of time or circumstance
If the conditional uses words like 'whenever', 'always', 'sometimes' (e.g., 'كلما دخلت المسجد' or 'في بعض الأزمان'), then the quantity is stated: 'all' or 'some'. This makes it mahsura (quantified).
For example, 'Whenever it rains, the ground is wet' is a universal conditional.
3
If neither specific nor quantified, it is muhmala (indefinite)
If the conditional has no indication of time, circumstance, or quantity (e.g., 'إذا كان الموجود واجبًا' without any quantifier), it is indefinite.
In logical discourse, these are often treated as universal by default.
4
Apply the classification to an Islamic example
Take a conditional from fiqh: 'إذا زالت الشمس فصل الظهر' (When the sun passes the meridian, pray Dhuhr). This has a specific time? No, it's a universal condition: whenever the sun passes, pray. So it is mahsura (universal).
This classification affects how the conditional is used in syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ. فعُلِمَ أنَّ الأوضاع والأزمانَ في الشَّرطيَّةِ بمنزلة الأفراد في الحَمْلِيَّةِ. فإن كان الحكم باللُّزومِ والعناد في زمانٍ معيَّن؛ فشخصية ومخصوصةٌ، وإلا؛ فإن بيَّن كميَّة الزَّمان جميعه أو بعضه، فَمحصورةٌ، وإلا فَمهملةٌ. كذلك الشَّرطيَّةُ إن كان الحكم بالاتصال أو الانفصال فيها مع وضعٍ معيَّنٍ؛ فَهِيَ مَخصوصةٌ، وإلا؛ بأن بيَّن كميَّة الحكم أنه مع جميع الأوضاع أو بعضها؛ فهي محصورةٌ، وإلا فَمهملةٌ. وقوله: بمنزلة الأفراد؛ أي في الحملية؛ لأن الأفراد في الحملية محكوم عليها. وأما الأوضاع في الشرطية فالحكم باللزوم أو العناد فيها ليس عليها، بل مع ملاحظة مصاحبتها كلها أو بعضها للمقدم.
A section on the divisions of the conditional. It is known that the circumstances and times in the conditional are like individuals in the categorical. For if the judgment of concomitance or opposition is for a specific time, then it is singular and specified; otherwise, if the quantity of time is explained as all or some, then it is quantified; otherwise it is indefinite. Likewise, the conditional: if the judgment of conjunction or disjunction is with a specific circumstance, then it is specified; otherwise, if the quantity of the judgment is explained that it is with all circumstances or some, then it is quantified; otherwise it is indefinite. And his statement: 'like individuals' means in the categorical, because individuals in the categorical are what is judged about. As for circumstances in the conditional, the judgment of concomitance or opposition is not about them, but with consideration of their accompaniment all or some to the antecedent.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage classifies conditional propositions. The author begins with a section heading ('fasl') announcing the topic: divisions of the conditional. He then states a key principle: just as categorical propositions are quantified based on the individuals of the subject (e.g., 'every man', 'some man'), conditional propositions are quantified based on the circumstances (awda') and times (azman) that accompany the antecedent. The author then explains the division. If the judgment of concomitance (luzum) in a connected conditional (muttasila) or opposition ('inad) in a disjunctive conditional (munfasila) is for a specific time (e.g., 'if you come to me today'), then the conditional is singular (shakhsiyya) if one time, or specified (makhsusa) if a specific time is mentioned. Otherwise, if the quantity of time is explicitly stated as all or some, it is quantified (mahsura, i.e., universal or particular). If not, it is indefinite (muhmala). The same applies for circumstances: if the judgment is with a specific circumstance, it is specified; if the quantity of circumstances is explained (all or some), it is quantified; otherwise indefinite. The author then clarifies the analogy: in categoricals, individuals are the subjects of judgment; in conditionals, the circumstances and times are not the subjects of judgment but rather the judgment concerns the relationship (concomitance or opposition) while considering whether the circumstance accompanies the antecedent universally or partially. This classification is essential for understanding the quantification of conditional propositions, which are used in syllogisms of conditional form. For example, a universal conditional: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal.'

شخصية
مخصوصة
محصورة
مهملة

Classify the following conditional from kalam: 'كلما كان الشيء حادثًا كان له محدث' (Whenever something is temporally originated, it has an originator). Is it shakhsiyya, mahsura, or muhmala? Explain.
?
Rewrite the indefinite conditional 'إن زيدًا عالم فإنه فاضل' (If Zaid is knowledgeable, then he is virtuous) as a quantified universal conditional by adding the appropriate quantifier for time or circumstance.

Quantification of Conditional Propositions: Specific, Quantified, Indefinite
Author classifies conditional propositions into specific, quantified, and indefinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any conditional proposition (shartiyya) into one of three quantity types: specific (makhsusa/shakhsiyya), quantified (mahsuura), or indefinite (muhmala).

Core Idea
Conditional propositions have quantity too—based on time or conditions, not subjects.

In logic, just as categorical propositions are quantified by 'all' or 'some', conditional propositions are quantified by the time or conditions under which the implication holds. This passage from al-Dasuqi's commentary on al-Tahdhib introduces the threefold classification: specific (makhsusa/shakhsiyya), quantified (mahsuura), and indefinite (muhmala). Understanding this allows you to properly evaluate the scope of conditional statements used in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify proposition type
Determine whether the statement is a conditional (e.g., 'If P, then Q') or a categorical. The classification applies only to conditionals.
Categoricals have their own quantification based on subjects.
2
Check for specific time/condition
If the judgment of implication or disjunction is tied to a single, specific time (for categorical time) or a single specific condition (for conditionals), the proposition is 'specific' (makhsusa/shakhsiyya).
Example: 'If Zayd comes today, I will honor him' is specific to this time.
3
Check for quantified time/conditions
If the judgment explicitly specifies a quantity—'all' (every time/condition) or 'some' (at least one)—then the proposition is 'quantified' (mahsuura).
Example: 'Whenever Zayd comes, I honor him' is universal quantification.
4
Otherwise, indefinite
If no time or condition is specified, and no quantifier is given, the proposition is 'indefinite' (muhmala). Its logical force is ambiguous until context clarifies.
Example: 'If Zayd comes, I honor him' without any qualifier is indefinite.
5
Apply to conditionals with conditions (wad')
For conditional propositions (shartiyya muttasila or munfasila), replace 'time' with 'condition' (wad'). The judgment is 'with a specific condition' (specific), 'with all conditions or some' (quantified), or unspecified (indefinite).
Al-Dasuqi stresses that conditions are the analogue of individuals in categoricals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان الحكم باللُّزومِ والعناد في زمانٍ معيَّن؛ فشخصية ومخصوصةٌ، وإلا ؛ فإن بيَّن كميَّة الزَّمان جميعه أو بعضه، فَمحصورةٌ، وإلا فَمُهملةٌ. كذلك الشرطيَّة إن كان الحكم بالاتصال أو الانفصال فيها مع وضع معيَّن؛ فهي مخصوصة، وإلا؛ بأن بيَّن كميَّة الحكم أنه مع جميع الأوضاع أو بعضها؛ فهي محصورة، وإلا، فمهملة. وقوله : بمنزلة الأفراد؛ أي: في الحملية؛ لأن الأفراد في الحملية محكوم عليها. وأما الأوضاع في الشرطية؛ فالحكم باللزوم أو العناد فيها ليس عليها، بل مع ملاحظة مصاحبة كلها أو بعضها للمقدم. (قوله : جميعه) بدلٌ من كميَّة، بدل مفصل من مجمل، وكان الأنسب أن يقول: فإن بيَّن كميَّة التقادير جميعها أو بعضها؛ لأجل أن يشمل الزمان والأوضاع.
If the judgment of implication or incompatibility is in a specific time, then it is personal and specific. Otherwise, if it specifies the quantity of time, all of it or some of it, then it is quantified. Otherwise, it is indefinite. Similarly for the conditional: if the judgment of conjunction or disjunction in it is with a specific condition, then it is specific. Otherwise, if it specifies the quantity of the judgment that it is with all conditions or some of them, then it is quantified. Otherwise, it is indefinite. And his saying: 'like individuals' means in the categorical, because individuals in the categorical are the subjects of judgment. As for the conditions in the conditional, the judgment of implication or incompatibility is not upon them, but rather with consideration of the accompaniment of all or some of them to the antecedent. (His saying: 'all of it') is a substitute for 'quantity', a detailed substitute for a general one, and it would have been more appropriate to say: 'if it specifies the quantity of all the conditions or some of them' so as to include time and conditions.
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage defines the three quantities for conditional propositions, paralleling the quantification of categoricals. It explains that if the judgment (of implication or incompatibility) is tied to a specific time, it is personal/specific (shakhsiyya/makhsusa). If the quantity of time (all or part) is specified, it is quantified (mahsuura). If not, it is indefinite (muhmala). The same threefold division applies to conditionals: if the judgment is tied to a specific condition (wad'), it is specific; if the quantity of conditions (all or some) is specified, it is quantified; otherwise indefinite. The commentator (al-Dasuqi) clarifies that in categoricals, individuals are the subjects of judgment, whereas in conditionals, the judgment is not about the conditions themselves but about the accompaniment of conditions to the antecedent. He also notes that the phrase 'جميعه' is a substitute for 'كمية', and suggests that 'جميع التقادير' would have been better to cover both time and conditions.

Key Terms
شخصية
مخصوصة
محصورة
مهملة
وضع
تقادير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a conditional proposition states 'If Zayd comes, I honor him' without specifying how many times this holds, what is its quantity?
A) Specific (makhsusa)
B) Quantified (mahsuura)
C) Indefinite (muhmala)
D) Universal

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions, like categoricals, can be specific, quantified, or indefinite, depending on whether the judgment is tied to a specific time/condition, a quantified range, or no specification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following conditional propositions from fiqh: (a) 'If water is available, perform wudu'' (general ruling) – is it specific, quantified, or indefinite? (b) 'If the imam is absent, so-and-so leads' (only on that occasion) – classify and explain your reasoning.

Identifying Quantifiers for Universal Affirmative Conditionals
Author lists quantifier particles for universal affirmative conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct quantifier particles for universal affirmative conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals and distinguish them from indefinite conditionals.

Core Idea
The quantifier tells you the scope but not the necessity of the connection.

In this passage, the author teaches us the specific Arabic particles that indicate a universal affirmative judgment in conditional propositions. Knowing these particles is essential for properly analyzing arguments in Islamic logic, especially when conditional statements are used in proofs.

Guided Path
1
Define 'sawr' (quantifier)
Find where the author says that which clarifies quantity is called sawr. Understand that it is a particle that indicates whether the proposition holds for all or some cases.
2
List quantifiers for conjunctive conditionals
Look for the list: kullama, mahma, and mata. These are particles meaning 'whenever' or 'when' and are used in conditionals of the form 'whenever A, then B'.
They all indicate universal affirmative quantity, but they are interchangeable in this context.
3
Identify the quantifier for disjunctive conditionals
The author states that for universal affirmative disjunctives, the sawr is 'da'iman' (always). Example: 'Always a number is either even or odd.'
4
Recognize indefinite conditionals
The commentary explains that if no quantifier is used, the proposition is indefinite (muhmala). For example, 'in ji'tani ukrimka' (If you come, I honor you) is indefinite because it does not specify all or some conditions.
5
Practice identifying sawr in examples
Take an Arabic conditional like 'متى كان العدد زوجاً فإنه ينقسم على اثنين' (Whenever a number is even, it is divisible by two). Identify 'mata' as the sawr, making it a universal affirmative conjunctive conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيةِ (وَإِلَّا فَمُهْمَلَةٌ). وما بهِ بيانُ الكَمِّيَّةِ يُسمَّى سُوراً. - فشور الموجبة الكلية : - من المتّصلة: «كلَّما، ومهما، ومتى». - ومن المنفصلة: دائماً». (قَوْلُهُ : وَإِلَّا ، فَمُهْمَلَةٌ) أي: وإلا بأن أطلق اللُّزوم أو العناد فيها، ولم يُبيِّن كميَّةً التقادير من كونها كُلاً، أو بعضاً مُعيَّناً، أو غير مُعيَّن، فالقضيَّةُ مُهملةٌ نحو : إِنْ جئتَني أكرِمْكَ. (قَوْلُهُ: كُلَّمَا ... إلخ) نحو : كُلَّما أو مهما أو متى كانَتِ الشَّمس طالعةٌ؛ فالنَّهَارُ موجود . (قَوْلُهُ : وَمِنَ الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ دَائِمَاً كَقَولِنَا : دائماً العددُ إمَّا زوج أو فرد، ودائماً هذا الشَّيْءُ إِمَّا شجر أو حجر، ودائماً زيد إما في البحر، وإمَّا أَنْ لَا يغرقُ . (قَوْلُهُ : فَسُوْرُ المُوْجِبَةِ الكُلِّيَّة ذهبَ الشَّيخُ إلى أنَّ كلمةَ «إنْ» شديدةُ الدَّلالةِ على اللُّزومِ، وَالوْ» و«مهما» كَالمتوسط، و«إذا» و«كُلَّما» و«لَمَّا»؛ لا دلالة لها عليهِ، وجعل صاحب المطالعِ مَهْمَا وَلَوْ أيضاً من هذا القبيل، وَزَيَّفَ شارحُهَا ذلكَ كُلَّهُ وقال : أدواتُ الشَّرْطِ لا دلالة لها على أكثرَ مِنَ الاتّصالِ والانفصال، فإذا أُريدَ إفادة اللُّزومِ؛ قُيّدَتِ القضيَّةُ بِاللُّزومِ، وَإِذا أُريدَ إفادةُ الاتِّفَاقِ؛ قُيِّدَتْ بِهِ، وَإِذَا لَمْ يُقيَّدُ بأحدهما كانَتْ مُطلقةٌ لا تفيدُ أكثرَ مِنَ الاتِّصالِ، فَكُلَّمَا كانَتِ الشَّمسُ طالعة فَالنَّهارُ موجودٌ ; مطلقةٌ تحتمل الاتّفاق واللزومَ ، وَ : كُلَّما كانَتِ الشَّمسُ طالعةً فَالنَّهارُ موجودٌ لزوماً ; مُوجّهة لزوميَّةٌ، وَاتِّفاقاً ; مُوجّهة اتفاقيَّةٌ، وبهذا عرفتَ أَنَّ اللُّزوم والاتفاق كَيفيَّتَانِ زائدتان على النّسبةِ المعتبرة فى الشَّرطيَّةِ ، والنِّسبة المعتبرة فيها مُجرَّدُ الاتصال أو الانفصال ا.هـ. عصام، ولا تتوهَّمْ أنَّ الجهة قد تكون في الشَّرطيَّاتِ كَالحمليَّاتِ، فإِنَّ اللُّزوم والاتفاق وإنْ كانَا صِفَتَيْنِ لِلنِّسبة ; لكنهما باعتبارهما لا تعدُّ مُوجَّهةً بالجهةِ المعتبرة المبحوث عنها في الحمليَّاتِ، وقد تقدَّمَ مَا في ذلك .
Chapter: On the divisions of the conditional. (And otherwise it is indefinite). And that by which the quantity is explained is called the quantifier. So the quantifiers of the universal affirmative: from the conjunctive: 'kullama', 'mahma', and 'mata'. And from the disjunctive: 'da'iman'. (His saying: 'And otherwise it is indefinite') means: if the implication or incompatibility is left unrestricted and the quantity of the conditions—whether all, some specific, or unspecified—is not specified, then the proposition is indefinite, like 'If you come to me, I honor you.' (His saying: 'kullama... etc.') example: 'Whenever the sun is rising, the day exists.' (His saying: 'And from the disjunctive: da'iman') like our saying: 'Always a number is either even or odd', and 'Always this thing is either a tree or a stone', and 'Always Zayd is either in the sea or he does not drown.' (His saying: 'So the quantifier of the universal affirmative') The shaykh held that the word 'in' strongly indicates necessity, 'law' and 'mahma' are intermediate, and 'idha', 'kullama', 'lamma' have no indication of it. The author of al-Matali' considered 'mahma' and 'law' to be of this type as well. But its commentator refuted all that and said: The conditional particles indicate nothing more than conjunction and disjunction. So if one wants to convey necessity, the proposition is qualified by necessity; if one wants to convey coincidence, it is qualified by it; and if it is not qualified by either, then it is absolute, conveying nothing more than conjunction. Thus 'Whenever the sun is rising, day exists' is absolute, admitting both coincidence and necessity. And 'Whenever the sun is rising, day exists necessarily' is a necessary modal, and 'coincidentally' is a coincidental modal. By this you know that necessity and coincidence are two modalities additional to the relation considered in the conditional, and the relation considered in it is merely conjunction or disjunction. End quote of Isam. And do not imagine that modality can be in conditionals as in categoricals, for necessity and coincidence, although they are two attributes of the relation, are not considered as the modality discussed in categoricals. And what pertains to that has preceded.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the quantifiers (sawr) for universal affirmative conditionals. It first defines the sawr as that which clarifies quantity. Then it lists: for conjunctive conditionals, the sawr are 'kullama', 'mahma', and 'mata'; for disjunctive, it is 'da'iman'. The commentator expands by explaining that 'wa illa fahmuhmala' means if the implication or incompatibility is not quantified, the proposition is indefinite, giving the example 'in ji'tani ukrimka' (if you come, I honor you). He then gives examples for each particle with 'kullama...' and 'da'iman...'. A longer critical note from al-Atar (citing Isam) reports a debate: some scholars (the shaykh, possibly al-Sayyid al-Sharif) held that 'in' strongly indicates necessity, 'law' and 'mahma' are intermediate, while 'idha', 'kullama', 'lamma' have no indication. The author of al-Matali' (maybe al-Urmawi) included 'mahma' and 'law' in the intermediate category. However, the commentator refutes all this, stating that conditional particles only indicate conjunction or disjunction; necessity or coincidence must be explicitly stated. Thus 'kullama' without qualification is absolute (mutlaqa) and ambiguous. Finally, a warning that necessity and coincidence are not considered modal qualifiers (jiha) in conditionals the way they are in categoricals.

سور
كلما
مهما
متى
دائماً

C) دَائِمَاً

Key Takeaway
The quantifiers 'kullama', 'mahma', 'mata' for conjunctive and 'da'iman' for disjunctive mark universal affirmative conditionals, but they do not indicate necessity.

The Debate on Implication of Necessity in Conditional Particles
Scholarly debate on whether conditional particles imply necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the positions of the Shaykh, the author of al-Matali', and the commentator on whether conditional particles imply necessity, and explain the final resolution.

Core Idea
Conditional particles only indicate conjunction or disjunction; necessity must be explicitly stated.

This passage reports a scholarly debate: do conditional particles like 'in' and 'kullama' carry an inherent implication of necessary connection? The Shaykh and the author of al-Matali' thought some did, but the commentator refuted this, arguing that particles are neutral. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to analyze conditional arguments in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
State the Shaykh's view
The Shaykh (likely al-Sayyid al-Sharif) held that 'in' strongly indicates necessity, 'law' and 'mahma' are intermediate, and 'idha', 'kullama', 'lamma' have no indication of necessity.
Reference in text: 'ذهب الشيخ إلى أن كلمة إن شديدة الدلالة على اللزوم، ولو ومهما كالمتوسط، وإذا وكلما ولما لا دلالة لها عليه'
2
Identify the modification by the author of al-Matali'
The author of al-Matali' (possibly al-Urmawi) considered 'mahma' and 'law' also to have intermediate indication, expanding the category.
Text: 'وجعل صاحب المطالع مهما ولو أيضاً من هذا القبيل'
3
Understand the commentator's refutation
The commentator (likely from the sharh) refutes all these positions, stating that conditional particles only indicate conjunction (ittisal) or disjunction (infisal). Necessity (luzum) and coincidence (ittifaq) are external qualifiers that must be explicitly added, e.g., 'luzuman' or 'ittifaqan'.
Text: 'وقال: أدوات الشرط لا دلالة لها على أكثر من الاتصال والانفصال'
4
Apply the distinction
Take 'kullama kanat al-shams tāliʿatan fa al-nahāru mawjūd' (Whenever the sun is rising, day exists). According to the commentator, this is absolute (mutlaqa), meaning it could be coincidence or necessity. To specify necessity, one must say 'luzuman'.
5
Distinguish from categorical modality
The passage warns that luzum and ittifaq are not the same as the modal jiha discussed in categorical propositions. They are attributes of the conditional relation, not of the terms.
Text: 'ولا تتوهم أن الجهة قد تكون في الشرطيات كالحمليات...'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قَوْلُهُ : وَإِلَّا ، فَمُهْمَلَةٌ) أي: وإلا بأن أطلق اللُّزوم أو العناد فيها، ولم يُبيِّن كميَّةً التقادير من كونها كُلاً، أو بعضاً مُعيَّناً، أو غير مُعيَّن، فالقضيَّةُ مُهملةٌ نحو : إِنْ جئتَني أكرِمْكَ. (قَوْلُهُ : وَمِنَ الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ دَائِمَاً كَقَولِنَا : دائماً العددُ إمَّا زوج أو فرد، ودائماً هذا الشَّيْءُ إِمَّا شجر أو حجر، ودائماً زيد إما في البحر، وإمَّا أَنْ لَا يغرقُ . Chapter: On the divisions of the conditional. (And otherwise it is indefinite). And that by which the quantity is explained is called the quantifier. So the quantifiers of the universal affirmative: from the conjunctive: 'kullama', 'mahma', and 'mata'. And from the disjunctive: 'da'iman'. (His saying: 'And otherwise it is indefinite') means: if the implication or incompatibility is left unrestricted and the quantity of the conditions—whether all, some specific, or unspecified—is not specified, then the proposition is indefinite, like 'If you come to me, I honor you.' (His saying: 'kullama... etc.') example: 'Whenever the sun is rising, the day exists.' (His saying: 'And from the disjunctive: da'iman') like our saying: 'Always a number is either even or odd', and 'Always this thing is either a tree or a stone', and 'Always Zayd is either in the sea or he does not drown.' (His saying: 'So the quantifier of the universal affirmative') The shaykh held that the word 'in' strongly indicates necessity, 'law' and 'mahma' are intermediate, and 'idha', 'kullama', 'lamma' have no indication of it. The author of al-Matali' considered 'mahma' and 'law' to be of this type as well. But its commentator refuted all that and said: The conditional particles indicate nothing more than conjunction and disjunction. So if one wants to convey necessity, the proposition is qualified by necessity; if one wants to convey coincidence, it is qualified by it; and if it is not qualified by either, then it is absolute, conveying nothing more than conjunction. Thus 'Whenever the sun is rising, day exists' is absolute, admitting both coincidence and necessity. And 'Whenever the sun is rising, day exists necessarily' is a necessary modal, and 'coincidentally' is a coincidental modal. By this you know that necessity and coincidence are two modalities additional to the relation considered in the conditional, and the relation considered in it is merely conjunction or disjunction. End quote of Isam. And do not imagine that modality can be in conditionals as in categoricals, for necessity and coincidence, although they are two attributes of the relation, are not considered as the modality discussed in categoricals. And what pertains to that has preceded.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the quantifiers (sawr) for universal affirmative conditionals. It first defines the sawr as that which clarifies quantity. Then it lists: for conjunctive conditionals, the sawr are 'kullama', 'mahma', and 'mata'; for disjunctive, it is 'da'iman'. The commentator expands by explaining that 'wa illa fahmuhmala' means if the implication or incompatibility is not quantified, the proposition is indefinite, giving the example 'in ji'tani ukrimka' (if you come, I honor you). He then gives examples for each particle with 'kullama...' and 'da'iman...'. A longer critical note from al-Atar (citing Isam) reports a debate: some scholars (the shaykh, possibly al-Sayyid al-Sharif) held that 'in' strongly indicates necessity, 'law' and 'mahma' are intermediate, while 'idha', 'kullama', 'lamma' have no indication. The author of al-Matali' (maybe al-Urmawi) included 'mahma' and 'law' in the intermediate category. However, the commentator refutes all this, stating that conditional particles only indicate conjunction or disjunction; necessity or coincidence must be explicitly stated. Thus 'kullama' without qualification is absolute (mutlaqa) and ambiguous. Finally, a warning that necessity and coincidence are not considered modal qualifiers (jiha) in conditionals the way they are in categoricals.

Key Takeaway
Conditional particles are neutral regarding necessity; necessity and coincidence are external qualifiers that must be explicitly added.

Conditional Particles: Connection vs. Necessity
Critical refinement of conditional particle interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the basic conditional relation (ittiṣāl/infiṣāl) and the additional qualities of lazūm and ittifāq, and explain why conditional particles alone do not indicate necessary connection.

Core Idea
Conditional particles only indicate connection or disconnection; necessity is an explicit qualification.

In this passage, al-ʿAṭṭār presents a critical refinement of how conditional particles are interpreted. The commentator refutes the view that particles like 'in' imply necessary connection. Instead, they indicate merely connection or disconnection. Understanding this prevents over-interpretation of conditional propositions in logical argumentation, a common pitfall even among advanced students.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
First, note al-Shaykh's view: 'in' strongly indicates lazūm, 'law' and 'mahmā' are intermediate, and 'idhā', 'kullamā', 'lammā' do not indicate it. Then the author of al-Maṭāliʿ adds 'mahmā' and 'law' to the intermediate group. Finally, the commentator refutes all these, arguing particles indicate only connection or disconnection.
The third position is the one adopted in this passage.
2
Extract the commentator's core claim
The commentator states: 'Adawāt al-sharṭ lā dalāla lahā ʿalā akthara min al-ittiṣāl wa al-infiṣāl' – conditional particles indicate nothing beyond connection or disconnection. If necessity or coincidence is intended, the proposition must be explicitly qualified (e.g., 'luzūman' or 'ittifāqan').
3
Apply the distinction to an example
The passage gives: 'Kullamā kānat al-shamsu ṭāliʿatan fa-l-nahāru mawjūd' – this is absolute, admitting both coincidence and necessity. Only when saying 'luzūman' or 'ittifāqan' does it become a qualified proposition. This shows that the particle 'kullamā' alone does not determine the modality.
The same reasoning applies to all conditional particles.
4
Differentiate lazūm/ittifāq from modal jiha
The passage warns against confusing lazūm and ittifāq with the modality (jiha) discussed in categorical propositions. Although both are attributes of the relation, in conditionals they are not considered the same as the modalities (necessity, possibility, etc.) investigated in categorical logic. This is a precise terminological boundary.
This distinction is crucial for avoiding category errors in logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العطار (قَوْلُهُ : فَسُوْرُ المُوْجِبَةِ الكُلِّيَّة ذهبَ الشَّيخُ إلى أنَّ كلمةَ «إنْ» شديدةُ الدَّلالةِ على اللُّزومِ، وَ«لَوْ» و«مهما» كَالمتوسط، و«إذا» و«كُلَّما» و«لَمَّا»؛ لا دلالة لها عليهِ، وجعل صاحب المطالعِ مَهْمَا وَلَوْ أيضاً من هذا القبيل، وَزَيَّفَ شارحُهَا ذلكَ كُلَّهُ وقال : أدواتُ الشَّرْطِ لا دلالة لها على أكثرَ مِنَ الاتّصالِ والانفصال، فإذا أُريدَ إفادة اللُّزومِ؛ قُيّدَتِ القضيَّةُ بِاللُّزومِ، وَإِذا أُريدَ إفادةُ الاتِّفَاقِ؛ قُيِّدَتْ بِهِ، وَإِذَا لَمْ يُقيَّدُ بأحدهما كانَتْ مُطلقةٌ لا تفيدُ أكثرَ مِنَ الاتِّصالِ، فَكُلَّمَا كانَتِ الشَّمسُ طالعة فالنَّهارُ موجودٌ ؛ مطلقةٌ تحتمل الاتّفاق واللزومَ ، وَ : كُلَّما كانَتِ الشَّمسُ طالعةً فَالنَّهارُ موجودٌ لزوماً ؛ مُوجّهة لزوميَّةٌ، وَاتِّفاقاً ؛ مُوجّهة اتفاقيَّةٌ، وبهذا عرفتَ أَنَّ اللُّزوم والاتفاق كَيفيَّتَانِ زائدتان على النّسبةِ المعتبرة فى الشَّرطيَّةِ ، والنِّسبة المعتبرة فيها مُجرَّدُ الاتصال أو الانفصال ا.هـ. عصام، ولا تتوهَّمْ أنَّ الجهة قد تكون في الشَّرطيَّاتِ كَالحمليَّاتِ، فإِنَّ اللُّزوم والاتفاق وإنْ كانَا صِفَتَيْنِ لِلنِّسبة ؛ لكنهما باعتبارهما لا تعدُّ مُوجَّهةً بالجهةِ المعتبرة المبحوث عنها في الحمليَّاتِ، وقد تقدَّمَ مَا في ذلك .
Al-‘Aṭṭār: (His saying: 'The quantifier of the universal affirmative…') The Shaykh held that the word 'in' strongly indicates necessity, and 'law' and 'mahmā' are intermediate, while 'idhā', 'kullamā', and 'lammā' do not indicate it at all. The author of al-Maṭāliʿ made 'mahmā' and 'law' also of this category. Their commentator refuted all that and said: The instruments of condition do not indicate anything more than connection and disconnection. So if one intends to express necessity, the proposition is qualified with necessity; if one intends coincidence, it is qualified with it; and if not qualified with either, it is absolute and indicates only connection. Thus, 'Whenever the sun is rising, then day exists' is absolute, admitting both coincidence and necessity. And 'Whenever the sun is rising, then day exists necessarily' is a necessary qualified proposition, and 'coincidentally' is a coincidental qualified proposition. By this you know that necessity and coincidence are two additional qualities beyond the considered relation in the conditional, and the considered relation in it is mere connection or disconnection. End quote of ‘Iṣām. And do not imagine that the modality may be in conditionals as in categoricals, for necessity and coincidence, though they are attributes of the relation, are not considered as the modality investigated in categoricals by virtue of being so. And what has preceded on that is sufficient.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the interpretation of conditional particles. It first presents the view of 'al-Shaykh' (likely Ibn al-Hajib or similar) that the particle 'in' strongly implies lazūm (necessary connection), while 'law' and 'mahmā' are intermediate, and 'idhā', 'kullamā', 'lammā' do not imply lazūm at all. Then the author of 'al-Maṭāliʿ' (a text on logic) added 'mahmā' and 'law' to the intermediate category. However, the commentator on that work (likely 'Iṣām al-Asfarāyīnī or similar) refutes all these views entirely. He argues that conditional particles (adawāt al-sharṭ) indicate nothing more than connection (ittiṣāl) or disconnection (infiṣāl). If one wishes to express necessity (lazūm), the proposition must be explicitly qualified as such (e.g., 'luzūman'). Similarly, if one wishes to express coincidence (ittifāq), it must be qualified. Without such qualification, the proposition is absolute (muṭlaqa) and implies only connection. Examples are given: 'kullamā kānat al-shamsu ṭāliʿatan fa-l-nahāru mawjūd' – this is absolute, allowing either interpretation. Only when explicitly saying 'luzūman' or 'ittifāqan' does it become a qualified proposition. The passage concludes that lazūm and ittifāq are additional qualities (kayfiyyatān) beyond the basic relation of connection/disconnection. Importantly, it warns against confusing these with modal modalities (jiha) in categorical propositions; in conditionals, these are not considered the same as the modalities discussed in categorical logic. This is a precise clarification of the scope of conditional particles, guarding against over-interpretation.

Key Terms
لزوم
اتفاق
اتصال
انفصال
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Necessary connection (لزوم)
B) Coincidental connection (اتفاق)
C) Mere connection or disconnection (اتصال أو انفصال)
D) Both necessity and coincidence equally

Key Takeaway
Conditional particles do not carry modal force; lazūm and ittifāq are explicit qualifications that must be stated if intended.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Quantifiers of Conditional Propositions: A Complete Taxonomy
Author enumerates the four quantifiers for conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct quantifier for any given conditional proposition type (conjunctive or disjunctive) and distinguish quantified from unquantified conditionals.

Core Idea
Every conditional proposition must be quantified; four quantifiers cover affirmative and negative, universal and particular.

Conditional propositions (sharṭiyya) are the backbone of logical reasoning in Islamic scholarship, especially in uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām. But a conditional without a quantifier is ambiguous—it could be universal or particular. This lesson gives you the precise Arabic quantifiers (sūr) that remove that ambiguity, allowing you to build airtight arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Determine whether the proposition is conjunctive (muttaṣila) – expressing a connection like 'if...then' – or disjunctive (munfaṣila) – expressing an exclusive or inclusive alternative. This decides which quantifiers are available.
2
Determine desired quantity and quality
Decide whether you need a universal (all cases) or particular (some cases) quantifier, and whether the proposition is affirmative or negative. The four combinations are: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
3
Select the correct quantifier
For conjunctive universal affirmative: use 'kullamā' (كلما), 'mahmā' (مهما), or 'matā' (متى). For disjunctive universal affirmative: use 'dā’iman' (دائماً). For universal negative (both types): 'laysa albattata' (ليس ألبتة). For particular affirmative (both): 'qad yakūn' (قد يكون). For particular negative (both): 'qad lā yakūn' (قد لا يكون).
Al-Dasūqī clarifies that 'laysa' gives negation and 'albattata' gives universality.
4
Apply the quantifier to the proposition
Insert the quantifier at the beginning of the conditional statement. For example, 'Kullamā kānat al-shamsu ṭāli‘atan fa al-nahāru mawjūd' (Whenever the sun is rising, day exists). Without a quantifier, the proposition is 'muhmala' (unquantified) – note that the particles 'law', 'in', 'immā' are not quantifiers but connectors.
An unquantified conditional like 'In ji’tanī ukrimka' (If you come, I honor you) could be universal or particular; you must supply a quantifier to be precise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيةِ (وَإِلَّا فَمُهْمَلَةٌ). ومَا بِهِ بيانُ الكَمِّيَّةِ يُسمَّى سُوراً . فسور الموجبة الكلية : من المتّصلة: «كلَّما، ومهما، ومتى». ومن المنفصلة: دائماً». الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَإِلَّا ، فَمُهْمَلَةٌ) أي: وإلا بأن أطلق اللُّزوم أو العناد فيها، ولم يُبيِّن كميَّةً التقادير من كونها كُلاً ، أو بعضاً مُعيَّناً، أو غير مُعيَّن، فالقضيَّةُ مُهملةٌ نحو : إِنْ جئتَني أكرِمْكَ. (قَوْلُهُ: كُلَّمَا ... إلخ) نحو : كُلَّما أو مهما أو متى كانَتِ الشَّمس طالعةٌ؛ فالنَّهَارُ موجود . (قَوْلُهُ : وَمِنَ الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ دَائِمَاً كَقَولِنَا : دائماً العددُ إمَّا زوج أو فرد، ودائماً هذا الشَّيْءُ إِمَّا شجر أو حجر، ودائماً زيد إما في البحر، وإمَّا أَنْ لَا يغرقُ . وسورُ السَّالبة الكلية منهما : «ليس ألبتة . وسورُ الموجبة الجزئية منهما : قد يكون . والسالبة الجزئية منهما : «قد لا يكون». وإطلاق لفظة «لو ، وإنْ ، وإمَّا فى الاتصال والانفصال؛ للإهمال. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : مِنْهُمَا ) أي : مِنَ المتّصلة والمنفصلة . (قَوْلُهُ : لَيْسَ أَلْبَتَّةَ) فالسَّلْبُ أُخِذَ مِن (ليس) ، والكُلّيَّةُ مِن (ألبتَّة)، وذلكَ كَقولِنَا في المتّصلة : ليس ألبتَّةَ إذا كانَتِ الشَّمسُ طالعة فاللَّيلُ مَوجودٌ، وفي المنفصلة نحو : ليسَ ألبتَّةَ إِمَّا أنْ تكونَ الشَّمسُ طالعة، وإمَّا أَنْ يكونَ النَّهارُ مَوجوداً . (قَوْلُهُ : وَسُوْرُ الْمُوْجِبَةِ الْجُزْنِيَّةِ مِنْهُمَا ) أي : من المتصلة والمنفصلة. (قَوْلُهُ : قَدْ يَكُوْنُ كَقولنا : في المتَّصلة ؛ قد يكونُ : إِذا كانَ الشَّيءُ حيواناً ؛ كَانَ إنساناً، وفي المنفصلة؛ قد يكونُ : إِمَّا أَنْ تكونَ الشَّمس طالعةٌ، أو اللَّيلُ موجوداً . (قَوْلُهُ : قَدْ لَا يَكُوْنُ كَقولِنَا : فى المتَّصلة : قد لا يكونُ إذا كانَتِ الشَّمس طالعة ؛ كانَ اللَّيلُ موجوداً، وفي المنفصلة : قد لا يكون إما أن تكونَ الشَّمس طالعةٌ، أو النَّهارُ مَوجوداً، ومثلُ : قد لا يكون دخول حرفِ السَّلْبِ على سور الإيجابِ الكُلّي كَلَيْسَ كُلَّمَا، وَلَيْسَ مَهمَا ، وَلَيْسَ مَتَى في المتّصلة، وليس دائماً في المنفصلة؛ لأنَّه إذا حصل رفع الإيجابِ الكُلّيّ؛ تحقَّقَ السَّلْبُ الجزئي على ما تقدَّم. (قَوْلُهُ: وَإِطْلَاقُ ... إلخ) أي : عن التَّقييدِ بسورِ الكُلّيّ، وسور الجزئي ؛ نحو: لَوْ كَانَتِ الشَّمس طالعةٌ ؛ فالنَّهارُ موجود، ونحوَ إنْ كانَتِ الشَّمس طالعة ؛ فالنَّهارُ موجود، ونحو : زيدٌ إِمَّا في البحر، وإما ألا يغرق. (قَوْلُهُ : لَفْظَةِ لَوْ وَإِنْ أي : في المتّصلة، ومثلُ «إنْ»؛ «إذا»، نحو: إذا كانَتِ الشَّمس طالعة؛ فالنَّهارُ مَوجودٌ ، وَقولُهُ : (وإمَّا ) ، أي : في المنفصلة، فقوله: (في الاتصال)؛ راجع لـ «لَوْ» و«إن»، وقوله : (والانفصال)؛ راجع لإما. العطار (قَوْلُهُ: فِي الاِتِّصَالِ وَالِانْفِصَالِ) لفٌ على ترتيب النَّشرِ، فالاتصال راجع للفظة لَوْ وَإِنْ ، والانفصال لـ «إما» ومِثْلُها «أو» .
Chapter: On the divisions of the conditional. (And if not, it is unquantified.) That by which the quantity is explained is called a quantifier. The quantifier of the universal affirmative: from the conjunctive: 'kullamā, mahmā, matā'. And from the disjunctive: 'dā’iman'. Al-Dasūqī: (His saying: 'And if not, it is unquantified') meaning: if the connection or opposition is used absolutely and the quantity of the antecedents is not specified – whether all, a specific part, or unspecific – then the proposition is unquantified, like: 'If you come to me, I will honor you.' (His saying: 'kullamā... etc.') Example: 'Whenever or wherever or when the sun is rising, then day exists.' (His saying: 'And from the disjunctive: dā’iman' like our saying: 'Always the number is either even or odd', and 'Always this thing is either tree or stone', and 'Always Zayd is either in the sea or he does not drown'.) And the quantifier of the universal negative from them: 'laysa albattata'. And the quantifier of the particular affirmative from them: 'qad yakūn'. And the particular negative from them: 'qad lā yakūn'. And the unrestricted use of the words 'law', 'in', and 'immā' in connection and disconnection is for unquantifiedness. Al-Dasūqī: (His saying: 'from them') i.e., from the conjunctive and disjunctive. (His saying: 'laysa albattata'): the negation is taken from 'laysa', and universality from 'albattata'. That is like our saying in the conjunctive: 'It is absolutely not that if the sun is rising then night exists', and in the disjunctive: 'It is absolutely not that either the sun is rising or day exists'. (His saying: 'And the quantifier of the particular affirmative from them') i.e., from the conjunctive and disjunctive. (His saying: 'qad yakūn' like our saying: in the conjunctive: 'It may be that if a thing is an animal, then it is a human'; in the disjunctive: 'It may be that either the sun is rising or night exists'.) (His saying: 'qad lā yakūn' like our saying: in the conjunctive: 'It may be that if the sun is rising, night exists'; in the disjunctive: 'It may be that either the sun is rising or day exists'. And like: 'qad lā yakūn' entering the negation particle on the universal affirmative quantifier, like 'laysa kullamā', 'laysa mahmā', 'laysa matā' in the conjunctive, and 'laysa dā’iman' in the disjunctive; because when the universal affirmative is negated, the particular negative is realized, as previously mentioned. (His saying: 'And the unrestricted use... etc.') i.e., unrestricted by the universal or particular quantifier; like: 'If the sun is rising, then day exists', and like: 'If the sun is rising, then day exists', and like: 'Zayd is either in the sea or he does not drown.' (His saying: 'the words law and in') i.e., in the conjunctive, and like 'in' is 'idhā', e.g., 'When the sun is rising, then day exists.' And his saying: 'and immā', i.e., in the disjunctive. So his saying: 'in connection' refers to 'law' and 'in', and his saying: 'and disconnection' refers to 'immā'. Al-‘Atṭār: (His saying: 'in connection and disconnection') is a chiasmus according to the order of the exposition: 'connection' refers to the words 'law' and 'in', and 'disconnection' to 'immā' and its like 'aw'.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage is a definitional enumeration of the quantifiers (sūr) for conditional propositions (sharṭiyya). It begins by stating that if a conditional proposition lacks a quantifier, it is called 'muhmala' (unquantified). Then it lists the quantifiers for each quantity: For universal affirmative conjunctive conditionals, the quantifiers are 'kullamā', 'mahmā', and 'matā'. For universal affirmative disjunctive conditionals, the quantifier is 'dā’iman'. The commentator al-Dasūqī explains that 'muhmala' occurs when the condition is stated without specifying whether it applies to all cases, a specific part, or an unspecific part, giving the example 'In ji’tanī ukrimka' (If you come to me, I will honor you) which can be interpreted as universal or particular. For universal negatives, the quantifier is 'laysa albattata' (absolutely not). For particular affirmatives, it is 'qad yakūn' (it may be). For particular negatives, it is 'qad lā yakūn' (it may not be). The passage also notes that the particles 'law', 'in', and 'immā' are used without quantifiers to indicate unquantified conditionals. Al-Dasūqī then elaborates on each: For 'laysa albattata', he explains that 'laysa' provides negation and 'albattata' provides universality. For 'qad yakūn' and 'qad lā yakūn', he gives examples from both types of conditionals. He also mentions that the particular negative can be formed by negating the universal affirmative quantifier, e.g., 'laysa kullamā'. Finally, al-‘Aṭṭār clarifies the particle distribution: 'law' and 'in' (and similar 'idhā') are for conjunctive conditionals, while 'immā' (and similar 'aw') is for disjunctive conditionals. This is a foundational taxonomy for correctly quantifying conditional propositions in logical analysis.

ليس ألبتة

The Parts of a Conditional: Two Propositions in Origin
Author defines the antecedent and consequent of a conditional as being originally two independent propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent and consequent of any conditional proposition and explain that they were originally two independent propositions.

Core Idea
Every conditional is a compound whose parts were once independent propositions.

You are already familiar with categorical propositions (حمليات) that assert something of a subject. Now we turn to conditional propositions (شرطيات), which join two propositions with a particle like 'if' or 'either'. At first glance, a conditional seems like a single statement, but this passage reveals its true structure: its two parts were originally separate propositions. Understanding this origin is key to analyzing compound arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two parts
In the Arabic passage, find the terms 'المقدّم' (antecedent) and 'التّالي' (consequent). These are the two parts (طرفا) of any conditional.
For example, in 'إن طلعت الشمس فالنهار موجود' (if the sun rises, then daytime exists), the antecedent is 'طلعت الشمس' and the consequent is 'النهار موجود'.
2
Identify their original independence
The passage stresses that although after composition (بعد التركيب) they form one proposition, originally (في الأصل) they were two separate propositions. Confirm this by mentally removing the conditional particle.
The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies that 'في الأصل' refers to the state before the particle joins them.
3
Recognize the possible compositions
The passage says 'إِمَّا حَمْلِيَّتَانِ' (either both categorical) but the commentary adds that they could also be both conditional, both disjunctive, or a mix. The examples show both categorical: for a conditional and for a disjunctive.
Example: 'المفهوم إما واجب أو ممكن أو ممتنع' (the concept is either necessary, possible, or impossible) is a disjunctive with three parts, which some scholars treat as multiple disjunctions.
4
Understand the regress argument
The commentary warns that if the parts themselves are compound, we must eventually reach simple categorical propositions, otherwise infinite regress (تسلسل) would occur. This shows that all conditionals ultimately rest on categorical foundations.
This is a key logical principle: every analysis terminates in simple propositions.

(His statement: 'either both categorical' means that the composition from the primary parts is confined to these and is resumable to this number of divisions, otherwise there is no conditional except that its composition is from categoricals, since there must be termination at the categorical, otherwise an infinite regress of parts would follow, and therefore...
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the composition of conditional propositions. It defines the two parts (tarafa) of a conditional as the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali). Although after combination they form a single proposition, they were originally two independent propositions. These original propositions can be either both categorical (hamliyya), both conditional (muttasila), both disjunctive (munfasila), or a mix, though the text only explicitly mentions 'either both categorical' and the commentary expands to other possibilities. The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that the 'originally' refers to before the connective (like 'in' or 'imma') joins them. He gives examples: a conditional like 'If it is a raven, it is black' (categorical + categorical), and a disjunctive like 'Either it is a raven or it is black' (also categorical + categorical). Al-Mutar notes a subtlety about disjunctions with three alternatives (e.g., 'the concept is either necessary, possible, or impossible'): some scholars consider such three-part disjunctions as multiple binary disjunctions, because a single disjunctive relation holds only between two terms. The passage also warns that to avoid infinite regress, the ultimate parts must be categorical propositions. This analysis is foundational for understanding the structure of compound propositions in logic.

The Two Parts of a Conditional: Original Propositions and Their Types
Author defines and classifies the original propositions of a conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent and consequent of a conditional proposition and classify whether each is categorical, conditional, or disjunctive based on the given examples.

Core Idea
The parts of a conditional are originally two independent propositions, not necessarily simple categoricals.

In Islamic logic, conditional propositions (al-sharṭiyyāt) are crucial for constructing complex arguments in kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh. Before analyzing a conditional, we must understand that its two parts—the antecedent and consequent—are originally separate propositions, even though they combine into one statement. This lesson unpacks that concept and shows how these parts can themselves be categorical, conditional, or disjunctive, as illustrated by the author's classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional proposition
Locate a conditional statement (e.g., 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal'). Note the connective (e.g., 'كلما', 'إن', 'إما').
The examples in the passage use 'كلما' and 'إما' for conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals.
2
Extract the antecedent (مقدم) and consequent (تالي)
Separate the two parts that are joined by the conditional connective. For 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal', the antecedent is 'this thing is a human' and the consequent is 'it is an animal'.
In the Arabic, the antecedent precedes the consequent in the conjunctive conditional, but order may vary in disjunctive.
3
Determine the propositional type of each part
Check whether each part is a simple subject-predicate proposition (categorical) or contains its own conditional connective (conditional) or disjunction (disjunctive). The passage gives examples of both categorical and conditional parts.
If the part has no internal conditional or disjunction, it is categorical (ḥamliyya).
4
Classify the pair accordingly
State whether both parts are categorical (both ḥamliyyatān), both conditional (both muttaṣilatān), or both disjunctive (both munfaṣilatān). The passage only exemplifies the first two; the third is mentioned as a possibility.
The author notes that disjunctive examples are analogous but not given here.

Source Text

Section: On the Divisions of the Conditional. (And the two parts of the conditional) i.e., the antecedent and consequent, even though after composition they are one proposition, yet (in the original they are two propositions). Either (both categorical) like our statement: 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal,' and 'Either this number is even or odd.' Commentary
This passage defines the components of conditional propositions. The author explains that the two parts of a conditional—namely the antecedent (مقدم) and consequent (تالي)—are originally two separate propositions, even though they become a single proposition after composition. This distinction is crucial for understanding the structure of conditionals. The author then classifies the original propositions into three possibilities: both categorical (حمليتان), both conditional (متصلتان), or both disjunctive (منفصلتان). The text provides examples for the first two types. For categorical components, it gives a conjunctive conditional example: 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal,' where both 'this thing is a human' and 'it is an animal' are categorical. For a disjunctive conditional example: 'Either this number is even or odd,' where both parts are also categorical. For conditional components, complex examples are given: 'Whenever if this thing is a human, then it is an animal, then whenever this thing is not an animal, then it is not a human' illustrates a conditional whose antecedent and consequent are themselves conditionals. Similarly, the examples about the sun and daytime show the same structure. The author thus clarifies that the building blocks of conditionals can be at different levels of complexity.

The Connected Conditional (Muttasila): Definition and Implication
Dasuqi defines muttasila with an example and explains luzūm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a muttasila proposition and extract its antecedent (malzūm) and consequent (lāzim).

Core Idea
A connected conditional asserts that if the antecedent is true, the consequent follows necessarily.

This lesson introduces the connected conditional (al-muttasila), which expresses a relation of implication (luzūm). Dasuqi uses the example 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal' and explains that it is composed of two categoricals. We will learn to identify the components and the logical relation.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the structure of a muttasila
Look for the form 'Whenever/If A, then B' in Arabic, often with 'كلما' or 'إن'. Identify the two categorical propositions embedded.
Dasuqi shows that our saying 'the thing is a human' is one categorical, and 'it is an animal' is another.
2
Identify antecedent and consequent
The first proposition is the antecedent (malzūm); the second is the consequent (lāzim). In the example, being human is malzūm, being animal is lāzim.
Dasuqi explicitly calls the first malzūm and the second lāzim.
3
Understand the implication relation
From the truth of the antecedent, the truth of the consequent follows. Conversely, from the negation of the consequent, the negation of the antecedent follows. Dasuqi states this explicitly.
This is the key property of luzūm: the consequent is necessary given the antecedent.
4
Test with Islamic examples
Practice with a fiqh example: 'If the sale is valid, then ownership transfers.' Identify malzūm and lāzim and check the implication.
This skill is essential for analyzing conditional rulings in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كقولنا : كلما كان هذا الشيء إنساناً فهو حيوان، وإما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً. (أو متصلتان) كقولنا : كلما إن كان هذا الشيء إنساناً فهو حيوان، فكلما لم يكن هذا الشيء حيواناً، فهو لم يكن إنساناً، وإما أن يكون إن كانت الشمس طالعة، فالنهار موجود، وإما ألا يكون إن كانت الشمس طالعة لم يكن النهار موجوداً. الدسوقي (قوله: كلما كان هذا الشيء إنساناً؛ فهو حيوان أي: فهما قضيتان حمليتان الأصل؛ لأن قولنا الشيء إنسان؛ حملية، وقولنا: هو حيوان؛ حملية أخرى، وهذا مثال للمتصلة. (قوله: وإما أن يكون هذا العدد... إلخ) مثال للمنفصلة، والأصل: العدد زوج العدد فرد، وهما قضيتان حمليتان. (قوله: كلما إن كان هذا الشيء إنساناً؛ فهو حيوان... إلخ) أي: فقد أحكم باللزوم بين كون الشيء إذا كان إنساناً؛ كان حيواناً، وبين كونه إذا كان غير حيوان؛ كان غير إنسان، فالأول ملزوم، والثاني لازم، ويلزم من نفي اللازم نفي الملزوم.
