(قوله: وإما ألا يكون: إن كانت الشمس... إلخ) في بعض الهوامش أن الصواب: إسقاط «لا». والظاهر أن الصواب إثباتها، وحذف «لم»؛ لأن حاصل المعنى إثبات العناد بين لزوم وجود النهار لطلوع الشمس، وبين عدم ذلك اللزوم، وهو ظاهر على ما قلناه، وأما على ما في بعض الهوامش؛ فيكون المعنى إثبات العناد بين لزوم وجود النهار لطلوع الشمس، وبين لزوم عدم وجوده له. العطار عمد إلى تقديم بحث الحمليات على الشرطيات لبساطتها بالنظر إليها، وبقية الكلام غني عن الشرح.
Our saying: 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal', and 'Either this number is even or odd'. (Or two connected [conditionals]): our saying: 'Whenever if this thing is a human, it is an animal, so whenever it is not an animal, it is not a human', and 'Either if the sun is rising, then daytime exists', and 'Either that it is not if the sun is rising, daytime does not exist.' Al-Dasuqi: His saying: 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal' means: they are two categorical propositions originally, because our saying 'the thing is a human' is a categorical, and our saying 'it is an animal' is another categorical, and this is an example of the connected [conditional]. His saying: 'And either this number... etc.' is an example of the disconnected, and the origin is: the number is even, the number is odd, and they are two categorical propositions. His saying: 'Whenever if this thing is a human, it is an animal... etc.' means: he has firmly established the implication (luzūm) between the thing's being a human and its being an animal, and between its not being an animal and its not being a human, so the first is the antecedent (malzūm), the second is the consequent (lāzim), and from the negation of the consequent follows the negation of the antecedent. His saying: 'And either: if the sun is rising... etc.' means: he has firmly established the incompatibility (ʿinād) between the implication of daytime's existence from sunrise and the absence of that implication. His saying: 'And either that it is not: if the sun is... etc.' in some margins that the correct [reading] is to drop 'lā'. And it appears that the correct [reading] is to affirm it and delete 'lam', because the meaning is the establishment of incompatibility between the implication of daytime's existence from sunrise and the absence of that implication, and that is clear according to what we said, but according to what is in some margins, the meaning would be the establishment of incompatibility between the implication of daytime's existence from sunrise and the implication of its non-existence from it. Al-Attar: He proceeded to present the discussion of categoricals before conditionals due to their simplicity in consideration, and the rest of the discourse is sufficient without explanation.
Source pages p. 628

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by Dasuqi on a matn of Tadhheeb. It begins by giving examples of connected (muttasila) and disconnected (munfasila) conditional propositions. Dasuqi explains that each conditional is composed of two categorical propositions. For muttasila, the example 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal' involves a relation of implication (luzum): from the antecedent (being human) the consequent (being animal) follows, and conversely, from the negation of the consequent the negation of the antecedent follows. For munfasila, the example 'Either this number is even or odd' involves a relation of incompatibility ('inad) between the two alternatives. Dasuqi then discusses a more complex example of muttasila with a double implication, and finally addresses a textual issue about the inclusion of the negation particles 'la' and 'lam' in one of the examples. Al-Attar adds a note that the discussion of categoricals was placed before conditionals due to their simplicity.

A connected conditional (muttasila) expresses a relation of implication from antecedent to consequent, and from negation of consequent to negation of antecedent.

The Disconnected Conditional (Munfasila): Definition and Incompatibility
Dasuqi defines munfasila with an example and explains ʿinād.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a munfasila proposition and recognize the relation of incompatibility (ʿinād) between its two alternatives.

Core Idea
A disconnected conditional asserts that two alternatives are incompatible; they cannot both be true.

This lesson focuses on the disconnected conditional (al-munfasila), which expresses a relation of incompatibility (ʿinād) between two alternatives. Dasuqi's example is 'Either this number is even or odd'. We will see how it is composed of two categoricals and understand the meaning of incompatibility.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the structure of a munfasila
It has the form 'Either A or B'. In Arabic, often with 'إما'. Dasuqi gives the example 'Either this number is even or odd'.
The two alternatives are categorical propositions: 'the number is even' and 'the number is odd'.
2
Identify the two alternatives
Extract the two categoricals: they are independent but related. In the example, they are 'the number is even' and 'the number is odd'.
Dasuqi notes that the origin is two categoricals.
3
Understand the incompatibility relation
ʿInād means the two alternatives cannot both be true simultaneously. Dasuqi uses the example of daytime and sunrise to illustrate a more complex case: the incompatibility is between the implication of daytime from sunrise and the absence of that implication.
This is a key property: the disjunction is true only when exactly one alternative holds.
4
Test with Islamic examples
Practice with an example from fiqh: 'Either the prayer is valid or invalid.' Identify the two alternatives and verify that they cannot both be true.
This helps in understanding exclusive alternatives in Islamic rulings.

Distinguishing Exclusive and True Disjunctions
Dasuqui distinguishes exclusive and true disjunctions by negation particles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given disconnected conditional is an exclusive disjunction (مانعة جمع) or a true disjunction (منفصلة حقيقية) by examining the presence of negation particles.

Core Idea
The negation particles 'lā' and 'lam' determine whether a disjunction is exclusive or true.

In this lesson, Dasuqi explains how the deletion of one of the two negation particles (lā or lam) transforms a true disjunction into an exclusive one. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing conditional propositions in Islamic logic, especially in legal and theological reasoning where disjunctions appear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two alternatives (disjuncts)
In the proposition, find the two parts that are joined by 'or' (أو). For example, 'the number is even or odd' – the disjuncts are 'even' and 'odd'.
2
Check for negation particles 'lā' and 'lam'
Look for the Arabic particles 'لا' (lā) and 'لم' (lam) in each disjunct. Dasuqi's rule depends on which of these is present after deletion.
The original expression is assumed to have both particles; deletion of one determines the type.
3
Apply Dasuqi's rule based on which particle remains
If the expression contains 'lam' but not 'lā' (i.e., 'lā' is deleted), it is an exclusive disjunction (مانعة جمع). If it contains 'lā' but not 'lam' (i.e., 'lam' is deleted), it is a true disjunction (منفصلة حقيقية).
Exclusive disjunction prohibits both disjuncts being true but allows both false; true disjunction requires exactly one true.
4
Verify using truth conditions
Check whether both disjuncts can be false simultaneously. In exclusive disjunction, both false is possible; in true disjunction, both false is impossible.
For example, 'the water is pure or impure' – both cannot be false, so it is true. 'The contract is valid or the price is paid' – both may be false, so it is exclusive.

Or two disconnected [conditionals]: our saying: 'Whenever always either the number is even or odd, then always either it is divisible into two equals or not divisible', and 'Either this number is even or odd', and 'Either that this number is not even or not odd.' Al-Dasuqi: It is known that the first is more general than the second, and that incompatibility exists between two contradictories, not between a thing and what is more specific than its contradictory, as is the meaning according to some margins. And it appears that one should say: the harm is only in affirming both 'lā' and 'lam', as occurs in the commentator's expression, and the deletion of one of the two negations is correct. For if 'lā' is deleted without 'lam', the proposition becomes exclusive disjunction (māniʿat jamʿ), because incompatibility exists in it between the thing and what is more specific than its contradictory; and if 'lam' is deleted without 'lā', the proposition becomes a true disjunction (munfaṣila ḥaqīqiyya), because incompatibility exists in it between the thing and its contradictory. End. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 629

Commentary
This passage opens a chapter on the types of conditionals, specifically focusing on disconnected (munfasila) propositions. Dasuqi provides an example: 'Whenever always either the number is even or odd, then always either it is divisible into two equals or not divisible.' He notes that the first part is more general than the second. Then he discusses the role of negation particles: when both 'la' and 'lam' are affirmed, the proposition is a true disjunction (munfasila haqiqiyya) involving two contradictories; when only one negation is dropped, it becomes an exclusive disjunction (man'at jam') that only forbids conjunction of the two terms but not their simultaneous absence. He then examines the correct wording of another example, suggesting that the conjunction particle should be 'or' (aw) rather than 'and' (waw) to preserve the disjunctive meaning. The marginal glosses by al-Sharnubi further refine the analysis, arguing about the relative generality and the correct textual variants.

Classifying Compound Conditional Propositions
Enumeration of six connected and three disconnected compound conditional subtypes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify any compound conditional proposition from Islamic logical texts into its subtype based on the components (hamliyya, muttasila, munfasila) and the overall connective structure.

Core Idea
Compound conditionals have six connected and three disconnected subtypes, each reflecting a distinct logical form used in Islamic reasoning.

This passage presents a detailed classification of compound conditional propositions (al-qadaya al-shartiyya al-murakkaba), which are fundamental building blocks for conditional syllogisms (al-qiyas al-istithna'i). Understanding these subtypes is essential for analyzing arguments in usul al-fiqh, kalam, and other Islamic sciences where conditional reasoning is used. The author lists six types of connected compounds (muttasila) and three types of disconnected compounds (munfasila), each with concrete examples involving natural phenomena and numbers. We will walk through these types, learn to recognize their structure, and see how they apply to Islamic juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two main categories
Recognize that the overall passage divides into two parts: connected compounds (muttasila) where the outer connective is 'if...then' (إن), and disconnected compounds (munfasila) where the outer connective is 'either...or' (إما). Within each, the inner components can be predicative (hamliyya), connected conditional (muttasila), or disconnected conditional (munfasila).
2
Identify the six connected subtypes
The connected compounds are six: (1) hamliyya + muttasila, (2) its converse (muttasila + hamliyya), (3) hamliyya + munfasila, (4) its converse, (5) muttasila + munfasila, (6) its converse. Note that 'converse' here means swapping the order of the two components while keeping the outer 'if' structure. Study the examples: the first example uses sunrise and daytime; the third uses number parity.
The passage says 'the second: its converse' – meaning swap the order of the two parts from the first type.
3
Identify the three disconnected subtypes
The disconnected compounds are three, each taking the form 'Either ... or ...' where the whole is a munfasila. The components again can be hamliyya, muttasila, or munfasila. The first: hamliyya + muttasila (converse of sunrise example). The second: hamliyya + munfasila (number and parity). The third: muttasila + munfasila (odd/even example).
Why are there only three disconnected types? Because the converse of each would be logically equivalent? The author does not list converse forms here; only three are given.
4
Map the total count
The passage concludes that the types of connected compounds are nine, and of disconnected compounds are three. The nine likely include six explicitly listed plus the three from the disconnected list? This is a point of interpretation. For now, note the numbers: 6 connected subtypes plus 3 disconnected subtypes, but the author's total of 9 for 'connected' may refer to all compounds that have an overall 'if' structure, including those whose second part is a munfasila? Actually the Arabic says 'أقسام المتصلات تسعة' and then 'وأقسام المنفصلات ثلاثة'. So it distinguishes two families: muttasilat (connected conditionals) have 9 types, and munfasilat (disconnected) have 3. Our passage lists 6 under the heading of connected and then 3 under disconnected. That sums to 9 total for the first family? This is a common interpretive challenge in reading logical texts.
Be aware that 'muttasilat' here may refer to all compound conditionals whose outer connective is 'if', including those where the second part is a munfasila. So the three types listed under 'disconnected from them' are actually considered part of the muttasilat? The text is ambiguous. For the purpose of this lesson, focus on the structural types as given.
5
Apply to Islamic reasoning
Take a real fiqh example: 'If this liquid is khamr, then it is haram.' Identify the types: The antecedent 'this liquid is khamr' is a single hamliyya (predicative). The consequent 'it is haram' is also a hamliyya. So this is a simple conditional, not compound. A compound example: 'If whenever a drink intoxicates, it is prohibited, then if this drink intoxicates, it is prohibited.' This would be a muttasila (the inner 'whenever...then...') inside an outer muttasila, i.e., a connected compound of type 2? Actually that would be muttasila + hamliyya type (the converse of first type). Practice constructing examples from fiqh.

فأقسام المتَّصَلات تسعة، وأقسام المنفصلات ثلاثة.
Section: On the types of the conditional proposition. The first: consisting of a predicative and a connected [conditional], such as: If the rising of the sun is a concomitant of the existence of daytime, then whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists. The second: its converse. The third: consisting of a predicative and a disconnected [conditional], such as: If this is a number, then it is either even or odd. The fourth: its converse. The fifth: consisting of a connected and a disconnected [conditional], such as: If whenever the sun is setting, nighttime exists, then either the sun is setting or nighttime does not exist. The sixth: its converse. And the disconnected from them are three: the first: consisting of a predicative and a connected [conditional], such as: Either the rising of the sun is not a concomitant of the existence of daytime, or it is that whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists. The second: consisting of a predicative and a disconnected [conditional], such as: Either the number is one, or it is either even or odd. The third: consisting of a connected and a disconnected [conditional], such as: Either it is that if the number is odd then it is not even, or the number is either even or odd. Thus the types of connected [compounds] are nine, and the types of disconnected [compounds] are three.
Source pages p. 631

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed classification of compound conditional propositions (القضايا الشرطية), which are formed by combining two propositions using either a connecting tool (أداة الاتصال) like 'إن' (if) or a disconnecting tool (أداة الانفصال) like 'إما' (either/or). The author enumerates six types of connected compounds (متصلة) and three types of disconnected compounds (منفصلة), each with examples. The connected compounds are based on the nature of their two component propositions: 
1) Predicative (حمليّة) + Connected (متصلة) – e.g., 'If sunrise is concomitant with daytime, then whenever the sun rises, daytime exists.' 
2) The converse of this (swapping the components). 
3) Predicative + Disconnected (منفصلة) – e.g., 'If this is a number, then it is either even or odd.' 
4) Its converse. 
5) Connected + Disconnected – e.g., 'If whenever the sun sets nighttime exists, then either the sun is setting or nighttime does not exist.' 
6) Its converse. 
These six are considered the connected compounds. Additionally, there are three disconnected compounds, where the entire conditional is a disconnected proposition (إما...أو). These are: 
1) Predicative + Connected – e.g., 'Either sunrise is not concomitant with daytime, or whenever the sun rises, daytime exists.' 
2) Predicative + Disconnected – e.g., 'Either the number is one, or it is either even or odd.' 
3) Connected + Disconnected – e.g., 'Either if the number is odd then it is not even, or the number is either even or odd.' 
The author concludes that the total number of connected types is nine (the six listed plus the three from the disconnected category? Actually the text says 'أقسام المتصلات تسعة' meaning the types of connected compounds are nine, implying the three under المنفصلة are also considered connected? There is some ambiguity, but the enumeration is clear.) The disconnected types are three. This classification is essential for constructing conditional syllogisms (قياس استثنائي) in logic.

Key Terms
شَرْطِيَّة
مُتَّصِلة
مُنفَصِلة
حَمْلية
عَكْس

Key Takeaway
Compound conditional propositions consist of two components that can be hamliyya, muttasila, or munfasila, and the outer connective (if or either/or) determines whether the whole is a connected or disconnected compound.

Conditional Parts: From Complete Propositions to Dependent Clauses
Author explains that adding 'if' or 'either' renders the parts of a conditional incomplete as propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the two parts of a conditional proposition are not complete propositions after the addition of the connecting or disconnecting tool.

Core Idea
Adding 'if' or 'either' turns a complete proposition into a dependent clause.

In the previous lesson, we learned that a conditional proposition is composed of two propositions. However, once they are combined using the tool of connection (like 'if') or disconnection (like 'either'), these parts lose their independence. This lesson explains this crucial transformation and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original complete propositions
Take the two clauses that form a conditional. Each one, when stated alone, is a complete proposition that can be true or false. For example, 'The sun is rising' and 'The day is clear'.
These are called 'complete propositions' (qadaya tammah).
2
Apply the connecting or disconnecting tool
Now add the tool. For connection, prefix 'if' (إن) to the first clause. For disconnection, add 'either' (إما) to each clause.
The tool is called 'adāt al-ittiṣāl' or 'adāt al-infiṣāl'.
3
Observe the loss of completeness
After adding the tool, the clause can no longer stand alone as a full judgment. 'If the sun is rising' does not assert anything; it merely sets a condition. It is no longer a proposition (qadiyya) in the sense of an independent statement.
The passage says: 'it departs from being a proposition'.
4
Generalize the principle
This applies to both parts of a conditional. Each part, though originally a complete proposition, becomes incomplete when the tool is added. This is a key point in understanding conditional syllogisms.
The incompleteness is functional: the part now depends on the other part to form a complete conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم طرفا القضيَّةِ الشَّرطيَّة، وإن كانا قبلَ التَّركيب قضيَّتين تامَّتَين إلا أَنَّهُمَا خَرَجَا بِزِيَادَةِ أَدَاةِ الاتِّصَالِ أَوْ الِانْفِصَالِ عَن التَّمَام، فإنَّ قولنا : الشَّمسُ طالِعةٌ قضيَّةٌ، فتكون تامَّةً فى الإفادة، لكن إذا زِدْنا أداةَ الاتصال عليه وقلنا : «إن كانتِ الشَّمس طالعة»؛ خرجَتْ عن أن تكون قضيَّةٌ، فتكون خارجةً عنِ التَّمامِ بزيادة أداة الاتصال . وكذا قولنا : العدد زوج قضيَّةٌ، وبزيادة أداة الانفصال عليه، خرجت عن التّمام. وبعد أن فَرَغْنا من تعريف القضايا وتقسيمها إلى الأقسام، فحانَ لنا أن نشرع في بيان الأحكام، وعلى الله التَّوكُل، وبه الاعتصام.
Then the two parts of the conditional proposition, even if they were prior to composition two complete propositions, have actually departed from completeness by the addition of the tool of connection or disconnection. For our statement 'the sun is rising' is a proposition, so it is complete in conveyance; but when we add the tool of connection to it and say 'if the sun is rising', it departs from being a proposition, so it becomes outside of completeness by the addition of the tool of connection. Likewise our statement 'the number is even' is a proposition, and by the addition of the tool of disconnection to it, it departs from completeness. And after we have finished from the definition of propositions and their division into the divisions, it is time for us to begin the explanation of the rulings, and upon God is reliance, and by Him is preservation.
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
This passage clarifies a crucial point about the structure of conditional propositions: the two component parts (طرفا القضية الشرطية) are originally complete propositions independently, but once they are combined using a connecting tool like 'إن' (if) or a disconnecting tool like 'إما' (either), they lose their status as independent propositions. For example, 'The sun is rising' (الشمس طالعة) is a complete proposition that conveys a meaning and can be true or false. However, when we prefix it with 'إن' to say 'If the sun is rising' (إن كانت الشمس طالعة), it becomes an incomplete clause that cannot stand alone as a judgment. Similarly, 'The number is even' (العدد زوج) is a complete proposition, but adding 'إما' to say 'Either the number is even' (إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً) makes it part of a larger disjunction. This understanding is important because these incomplete parts still function as premises in conditional syllogisms. The passage then transitions to the next topic: the rulings (أحكام) of propositions, specifically contradiction (تناقض) and conversion (عكس), indicating the end of the definitional and divisional section and the beginning of the discussion of logical operations on propositions.

Key Terms
طرفا القضية
تمام
قضية تامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The parts of a conditional proposition are not independent propositions; they become incomplete due to the addition of the conditional tool.

Propositional Completeness and Its Loss
Author defines propositional completeness and its loss via particles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a complete proposition from a dependent one by identifying conditional and disjunctive particles.

Core Idea
A proposition's completeness is destroyed by adding 'if' or 'either'.

In logic, a proposition must stand alone to convey a truth value. The author illustrates this with examples from daily language, showing how the particles 'if' (conditional) and 'either' (disjunctive) rob a statement of its independence. This concept is foundational for understanding compound propositions and syllogistic reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify a complete proposition
Look at the statement 'The sun is rising' – it is a proposition (qadiyya) because it can be true or false on its own.
The example comes from the source.
2
Add the conditional particle
Now add 'if' to get 'If the sun is rising'. The statement becomes dependent on a condition and no longer functions as a complete proposition.
3
Add the disjunctive particle
Similarly, take 'The number is even' and add 'either' – it loses completeness because it now implies an alternative.
The source emphasizes that both particles remove completeness.
4
Generalize the rule
Any statement that includes a conditional or disjunctive particle is not a complete proposition; it is a part of a larger compound.
This rule applies across all Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو الانفصال عن التمام)، فإنَّ قولنا : الشَّمسُ طالِعةٌ» قضيَّةٌ، فتكون تامَّةً فى الإفادة، لكن إذا زِدْنا أداةَ الاتصال عليه وقلنا : «إن كانتِ الشَّمس طالعة»؛ خرجَتْ عن أن تكون قضيَّةٌ، فتكون خارجةٌ عنِ التَّمامِ بزيادة أداة الاتصال . وكذا قولنا : العدد زوج قضيَّةٌ، وبزيادة أداة الانفصال عليه، خرجت عن التّمام. وبعد أن فَرَغْنا من تعريف القضايا وتقسيمها إلى الأقسام، فحانَ لنا أن نشرع في بيان الأحكام، وعلى الله التَّوكُل، وبه الاعتصام.
Or disjunction from completeness. For our statement 'The sun is rising' is a proposition, so it is complete in conveying meaning. But if we add the conditional particle and say 'If the sun is rising', it ceases to be a proposition, so it becomes outside completeness due to the addition of the conditional particle. Similarly, our statement 'The number is even' is a proposition, and by adding the disjunctive particle to it, it ceases to be complete. After we have finished defining propositions and dividing them into their divisions, it is time for us to begin explaining the judgments. And upon Allah is reliance, and with Him is protection.
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of completeness (tamam) in propositions. The author defines a proposition as a statement that independently conveys a truth value, complete in providing information. Using the example 'The sun is rising'—by itself it is a proposition. However, when we add the conditional particle 'if' (إن), as in 'If the sun is rising', the statement becomes dependent on a condition and no longer functions as a self-contained proposition. Similarly, the statement 'The number is even' is a proposition, but adding a disjunctive particle like 'either' removes its completeness. The author then transitions to the next topic: after finishing the definition and division of propositions, the work proceeds to discuss the judgments (ahkam) of propositions, such as contradiction and conversion, with reliance on Allah.

Defining Contradiction and Its Priority
Definition of contradiction and its priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define logical contradiction (tanaqud) as disagreement of two propositions by affirmation and negation, and explain why it takes precedence over other immediate inferences.

Core Idea
Contradiction is the foundation of all immediate inference — without it, conversion and reductio have no ground.

In this passage, the author places the section on contradiction before other judgments. Why? Because everything else depends on knowing what a contradiction is. The commentators unpack the definition and show that even the proof of conversion uses contradiction. Let's walk through the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find what the author states contradiction is. He says 'Contradiction is the disagreement of two propositions.' Notice he does not initially add 'by affirmation and negation' – why?
The author leaves out 'bi-ijab wa salb' because a later condition suffices to exclude other types of disagreement.
2
Unpack the essential condition
The definition includes 'such that it necessitates that one is true and the other false' (implied by 'لذاته'). This condition distinguishes logical contradiction from mere difference. Check: does any disagreement by restriction or modification produce this effect?
Al-Dasuqi and al-Attar explain that the phrase 'by affirmation and negation' is implicitly covered by the condition.
3
Understand why contradiction is prioritized
The author states it is 'worthy of precedence over the other judgments' because other things depend on it. What are those things? Al-Dasuqi identifies them: conversion (al-'aks) with its three divisions. The proof by contradiction (dalil al-khalf) is given as an example: to prove a conversion is valid, you assume its contradictory and derive a contradiction.
Thus, conversion relies on knowledge of the contradictory, which relies on knowing contradiction itself.
4
Evaluate the commentators' refinements
Al-Attar and al-Dasuqi discuss why the author omitted 'bi-ijab wa salb' and why he focused on propositional contradiction. Al-Attar notes that the definition is a hadd (real definition) here, not a rasm. Notice how the commentator defends the author's choices.
This shows how later scholars refine the original text while remaining faithful to its core.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي التَّنَاقُضِ. وهو حقيق بالتقديم على سائر الأحكام؛ لتوقف غيره عليه، فَلِذَا قدَّمه، وقال في تعريفه : (التَّنَاقُضُ : اخْتِلَافُ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ). قال الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى سَائِرِ الْأَحْكَامِ) أي: باقي الأحكام، وهو العكس بأقسامِهِ الثَّلاثة . (قَوْلُهُ : لِتَوَقُفِ ... إلخ) علَّة لِقولِهِ : (وهو حقيق... إلخ)، والمراد بالغيرِ؛ العكس المستوي، وعكسُ النَّقيض بقسميه ، ووَجهُ التَّوقُفِ مَا يَأْتِي أَنَّ مِن الأدلَّةِ الَّتي يستدلُّ بها على صحة العكس دليلُ الخُلْف، وهو إثبات المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه؛ بأنْ يُقالَ : لَوْ لم يصدق هذا العكس؛ لَصدقَ نقيضُه، فصار العكسُ مُتوقفاً على معرفة النقيض ، وَلَا شَكٍّ أنَّ مَعرفةَ النَّقيض متوقفةٌ على معرفة التناقض . (قَوْلُهُ : فَلِذَا ) أي : فَلِأجل التَّوقُفِ المذكور. (قَوْلُهُ : التَّنَاقُضُ) «أَلْ» للعهد أي : التَّناقض المعهود عند المناطقة، وأمَّا مَا أخرجَهُ مِن اختلاف المفردين؛ فهو تناقض لغوي. قال العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : التَّنَاقُضُ : اخْتِلَافُ) لم يقل : وَحَدُّوْهُ، كَمَا في الأصل؛ للاختلافِ في تعريفات المفهومات الاصطلاحيَّةِ ; هَل هِيَ حدود أو رسوم؟ ... وقال الدسوقي: الإيجاب والسَّلْب، ولم يقيّد المصنف بذلك ; لأنَّ قولَه: بحيث يلزم ... إلخ يُفيدُه، وخرجَ الاختلافُ بغيرِهِ كَالاختلافِ بالحصر والإهمال والعدول والتحصيل، فَلَا يُسمَّى تناقضاً اصطلاحاً. وقال العطار: الأصل هُنَا بالحدّيَّةِ، وفي الكُلِّيَّاتِ الخمس بالرَّسميَّةِ ; تحكُمْ غَيْرُ حَفيَّ، فَالاختلاف جنس بعيدٌ ; سواء كانَ التَّعريفُ حَدًّا أو رسماً ; لأنَّ العرض العام لا يؤخذُ في التعريف عند المتأخرين، ولم يقل : اختلافُ قَضيَّتَيْنِ بِالإيجاب والسَّلْبِ كَمَا قالَهُ صاحبُ الأصل وغيره لإغناء قيد لذاتِهِ عنه، إذ الاختلافُ بغير الإيجابِ والسَّلْبِ لَا يقتضي لِذَاتِهِ أن تكونَ إحداهُمَا صادقةٌ والأُخرى كاذبةٌ، وإنَّما ذكرُوا هذا القيد لتحقيق مفهوم التَّناقضِ وتوضيحِهِ; لَا لِلاحتراز عن شيءٍ ; لأنَّ مَفهومَهُ إِنَّما يُطلق على هذا الاختلافِ، وَلَوْ تُرِكَ لم يقدح في التعريف، وخَصَّصَ التَّعريف بتناقض القضايا ; لأنَّهُ المقصود بالنَّظرِ والمنتفعُ بِهِ في القياساتِ، وأمَّا التَّناقضُ في المفردات ; فقد قال السَّيِّدُ : إِنَّهُ يُعرَفُ بالمقايسةِ، فَلا حاجة إلى إدراجه في تعريف التَّناقض.
Section: On Contradiction. And it is worthy of precedence over the other judgments because other things depend on it. Therefore he prioritized it and said in its definition: 'Contradiction is the disagreement of two propositions.' Al-Dasuqi said: 'His statement: over the other judgments i.e. the remaining judgments, which are conversion with its three divisions. His statement: because other things depend on it etc. is a cause for his statement: And it is worthy etc. And by other is meant simple conversion and conversion of the contradictory with its two divisions. And the aspect of dependence is that which will come: among the proofs used for the validity of conversion is the proof by contradiction (reductio ad absurdum), which is establishing the desired by invalidating its contradictory; by saying: if this conversion were not true, then its contradictory would be true. Thus conversion depends on knowledge of the contradictory, and there is no doubt that knowledge of the contradictory depends on knowledge of contradiction. His statement: therefore i.e. because of the aforementioned dependence. His statement: contradiction: 'al' is for the covenant i.e. the contradiction well-known among logicians. As for what it excludes, from the disagreement of simple terms, that is linguistic contradiction. Al-Attar said: 'His statement: contradiction: disagreement, he did not say: and they defined it, as in the original, because of disagreement in the definitions of terminological concepts: are they definitions or descriptions? ... Al-Dasuqi said: Affirmation and negation, and the author did not restrict it by that because his statement: such that it necessitates etc. suffices for it. And disagreement other than it is excluded, like disagreement by restriction, neglect, modification, and unmodified, so it is not called contradiction terminologically. And al-Attar said: The original here is by definition (hadd), and in the five universals by description (rasm); an unwise judgment. So disagreement is a remote genus, whether the definition is a definition or a description, because the common accident is not taken in definitions according to the later scholars. And he did not say: disagreement of two propositions by affirmation and negation, as the author of the original and others said, because a condition for its essence suffices instead of it. For disagreement other than by affirmation and negation does not require by its essence that one of them be true and the other false. They only mentioned this condition to realize the concept of contradiction and clarify it, not to exclude anything, because its concept is only applied to this disagreement, and if it were omitted it would not harm the definition. And he specified the definition to contradiction of propositions because it is the intended object of consideration and the beneficial one in syllogisms. As for contradiction in simple terms, al-Sayyid said: it is known by analogy, so there is no need to include it in the definition of contradiction.'
Source pages p. 633

Commentary
This passage defines logical contradiction (al-tanaqud) and explains why the author placed this section before other topics on judgments. The author states that contradiction is worthy of priority because other judgments (such as conversion and reductio ad absurdum) depend on it. He then gives the definition: contradiction is the disagreement of two propositions. The commentators elaborate: Al-Dasuqi clarifies that 'other judgments' refers to conversion (including simple, accidental, and contrapositive) and that the dependence is shown by the fact that proving a conversion often uses reductio ad absurdum, which requires knowledge of the contradictory. Al-Attar discusses the omission of 'bi-ijab wa salb' (by affirmation and negation) in the definition, arguing that the condition 'such that it necessitates' suffices. He explains that the definition focuses on propositional contradiction because that is useful in syllogisms; term contradiction is known by analogy and need not be included. The passage thus establishes contradiction as the foundational concept for immediate inferences, using the example: if a conversion were false, its contradictory would be true, leading to a contradiction. This is a typical logical move: proving by contradiction (dafil al-khalf).

Key Terms
التناقض
العكس
دليل الخلف
الإيجاب والسلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Logical contradiction is the disagreement of two propositions that by its essence necessitates one true and the other false; this concept underpins all immediate inferences like conversion and proof by contradiction.

The Essential Definition of Logical Contradiction
Commentary defines logical contradiction and refines the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a pair of statements qualifies as logical contradiction (tanāquḍ) according to the refined definition given in the commentary.

Core Idea
Logical contradiction is difference by affirmation and negation that intrinsically entails truth and falsity.

In this passage, the commentator refines the definition of logical contradiction, specifying that it consists of two propositions differing by affirmation and negation such that one is necessarily true and the other false by their very nature. Not every difference counts—only this specific opposition. Understanding this definition is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition being refined
Read the passage and locate the core components: two propositions, affirmation and negation, intrinsic entailment of truth/falsity.
The commentator states that the condition 'by affirmation and negation' is not explicitly needed because 'such that it entails...' suffices.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'difference' (ikhtilāf). The differentia is 'by affirmation and negation such that by itself one is true and the other false'.
The commentator notes that 'difference' is a remote genus, and the definition can be a hadd (complete definition) or rasm (description).
3
Check what is excluded
Differences in restriction (hasr), modification (ta'dīl), etc., are excluded because they do not entail truth/falsity intrinsically.
For example, 'All humans are mortal' vs 'Some humans are mortal' is not a contradiction.
4
Understand why 'by affirmation and negation' is not explicitly needed
The commentator argues that the phrase 'such that it entails...' already implies the opposition is by affirmation and negation, so the explicit mention is not necessary for the definition to be correct.
The footnote criticizes this omission, arguing that explicit mention is needed to exclude certain differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيجاب والسَّلْب، ولم يقيّد المصنف بذلك ؛ لأنَّ قولَه (۱) : بحيث يلزم . . . إلخ) يُفيدُه ، وخرجَ الاختلافُ بغيرِهِ كَالاختلافِ بالحصر والإهمال والعدول والتحصيل، فَلَا يُسمَّى تناقضاً اصطلاحاً
العطار
الأصل هُنَا بالحدّيَّةِ، وفي الكُلِّيَّاتِ الخمس بالرَّسميَّةِ ؛ تحكُمْ غَيْرُ حَفيَّ، فَالاختلاف جنس بعيدٌ ؛ سواء كانَ التَّعريفُ حَدًّا أو رسماً ؛ لأنَّ العرض العام لا يؤخذُ في التعريف عند المتأخرين، ولم يقل : اختلافُ قَضيَّتَيْنِ بِالإيجاب والسَّلْبِ كَمَا قالَهُ صاحبُ الأصل وغيره لإغناء قيد لذاتِهِ عنه ، إذ الاختلافُ بغير الإيجابِ والسَّلْبِ لَا يقتضي لِذَاتِهِ أن تكونَ إحداهُمَا صادقةٌ والأُخرى كاذبةٌ، وإنَّما ذكرُوا هذا القيد لتحقيق مفهوم التَّناقضِ وتوضيحِهِ؛ لَا لِلاحتراز عن شيءٍ ؛ لأنَّ مَفهومَهُ إِنَّما يُطلق على هذا الاختلافِ ، وَلَوْ تُرِكَ لم يقدح في التعريف، وخَصَّصَ التَّعريف بتناقض القضايا ؛ لأنَّهُ المقصود بالنَّظرِ والمنتفعُ بِهِ في القياساتِ، وأمَّا التَّناقضُ في المفردات ؛ فقد قال السَّيِّدُ : إِنَّهُ يُعرَفُ بالمقايسةِ، فَلا حاجة إلى إدراجه في تعريف التَّناقض، واعترضَهُ العصامُ بأنَّ معرفة الاصطلاح بالمقايسة مِمَّا لا يعقل، وأجاب عبد الحكيم : بأنَّ معنى كونه معلوماً بالمقايسة؛ أنَّه بعد العلم بأنَّ نقيض كُلِّ شيءٍ رَفْعُهُ، وأَنَّ الصَّدَقَ والكَذِبَ في المفردات بمعنى الحمل ؛ يحصلُ تعريفُ التَّناقض في المفرداتِ بأنَّهُ اختلافُهُمَا بالإيجاب والسَّلْبِ بحيثُ يقتضي لِذاتِهِ حملُ أحدهما عدم حمل الآخَرِ ا.هـ. فإِنْ قُلْتَ: تخصيص البحثِ بتناقض القضايا يُنافي ما تقرَّرَ أنَّ قواعد الفنّ يجب أن تكونَ عامةً منطبقةً على جميع الجزئيات ؛ فَالجواب أنَّ عموم مباحثِهِم إِنَّما يجبُ أنْ يكونَ بالنِّسبةِ إلى أغراضِهِم ومقاصدِهِم وَلَمَّا لم يتعلَّق لهم بالتناقض بين المفردات غرض يُقيَّدُ بِهِ ؛ اختصَّ نظرُهُمْ بِتناقض القضايا
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ : لأن قوله . . . إلخ ) هذا هو رأي المصنف في شرح الأصل، فلذا حذف هذا القيد من تعريفه هو مع ذكر الأصل له ولكن يقال له : إنَّ قوله : بحيث ... إلخ يفيد أيضاً أن يكون الاختلاف بين قضيتين فلا داعي لذكر قضيتين كما لا داعي لذكر الإيجاب والسلب، وكون بعض الفصول يغني عن البعض لا يسوغ الحذف في التعاريف فكان ينبغي اتباع الأصل بذكر هذا القيد لإخراج نحو العدول والتحصيل، وإلا فلا تخرج إلا بقيد بحيث ... إلخ .
Affirmation and negation, and the author did not restrict by that because his statement (1): 'such that it entails... etc.' suffices for it. And differences other than it, like difference by restriction/inclusiveness, modification/emphasis, are excluded, so they are not called contradiction terminologically. Al-Attar: The original here is by definition, and in the five universals by description; you judge it is not hidden, so difference is a remote genus, whether the definition is by definition or description; because the common accident is not taken in the definition according to the later scholars. And he did not say 'difference of two propositions by affirmation and negation' as the author of the original and others said, because the condition 'by itself' suffices for it, since difference other than by affirmation and negation does not entail by itself that one is true and the other false. Rather, they mentioned this condition to realize the concept of contradiction and clarify it, not to be cautious about something, because its concept is only applied to this difference, and if it were left, it would not harm the definition. And he specified the definition to contradiction of propositions because it is the intended consideration and beneficial in syllogisms. As for contradiction in single terms, al-Sayyid said: it is known by analogy, so there is no need to include it in the definition of contradiction. Al-'Isam objected to him that knowing the technical term by analogy is unintelligible. 'Abd al-Hakim answered: the meaning of 'known by analogy' is that after knowing that the contradictory of everything is its removal, and that truth and falsehood in single terms are in the sense of predication, the definition of contradiction in single terms is obtained: it is their difference by affirmation and negation such that by itself the predication of one entails the non-predication of the other. End. If you say: specifying the discussion to contradiction of propositions contradicts what is established that the rules of the art must be general and applicable to all particulars. The answer: the generality of their discussions must only be with respect to their purposes and objectives, and since they have no purpose concerning contradiction between single terms that would be restricted by it, their consideration is restricted to contradiction of propositions. (1) (His statement: Because his saying ... etc.) This is the opinion of the author in his commentary on the original, so he omitted this condition from his definition while the original mentioned it, but it is said to him: his saying 'such that... etc.' also suffices that the difference is between two propositions, so there is no need to mention 'two propositions' just as there is no need to mention 'affirmation and negation'. But the fact that some parts suffice for others does not justify omission in definitions, so he should have followed the original by mentioning this condition to exclude things like modification and emphasis, otherwise they are not excluded except by the condition 'such that... etc.'
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition of logical contradiction (tanāquḍ) and a justification for restricting its study to propositions (qadāyā) rather than single terms (mufradāt). The commentary first notes that the author omitted the explicit condition 'by affirmation and negation' because the phrase 'such that it entails by itself that one is true and the other false' already implies it. Differences that are not by affirmation and negation, such as differences in restriction (hasr) or modification (ta'dīl), are not terminological contradictions. The commentator al-Attar explains that the definition uses 'difference' as a remote genus, and the omission of 'two propositions' is compensated by the condition 'by itself' which inherently implies two propositions. The purpose of mentioning 'by affirmation and negation' is to clarify the concept, not to be cautious, because the term 'contradiction' is only applied to this specific difference. Then the passage discusses why logicians focus on contradictory propositions (tanāquḍ al-qadāyā) rather than contradictory single terms: because it is beneficial in syllogisms. Al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani claimed that contradiction in single terms is known by analogy (muqāyasa), but al-'Isam al-Isfarayini objected that knowing a technical term by analogy is unintelligible. 'Abd al-Hakim al-Siyalkuti defended al-Sayyid by explaining that the meaning is that after knowing that the contradictory of a term is its negation (raf'uhu) and that truth in terms means predication (ḥaml), the definition of contradiction in single terms can be derived: two single terms differ by affirmation and negation such that one's predication entails the non-predication of the other. The passage concludes with an objection and answer: if the rules of logic should be general, why restrict to propositions? The answer is that the generality is only with respect to the logicians' purposes; since they have no purpose (gharaḍ) in studying contradictory single terms in themselves, they restrict it to propositions. The footnote critiques the author's omission of the condition 'two propositions' in his definition, arguing that even if 'such that...' implies it, one should still explicitly mention it to exclude non-contradictory differences like modification and emphasis.

Key Terms
التناقض (al-tanāquḍ): The opposition between two propositions such that by their very nature one is true and the other false, i.e., difference by affirmation and negation that entails truth and falsity. [Note: Stable across the tradition; this commentary refines the definition without altering its core.]
قضية (qadiyya): A proposition (declarative statement) that is the subject of logical contradiction and syllogistic reasoning. [Note: No significant shift.]
الإيجاب والسلب (al-ījāb wa al-salb): The pair of affirmation and negation; the specific way in which two propositions must differ to constitute contradiction. [Note: No significant shift.]
لذاته (li-dhātihi): 'By itself' or 'by its essence' — the opposition must entail truth/falsity intrinsically, not by external factors. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, which of the following pairs would NOT be considered a logical contradiction (tanāquḍ)?
A) 'The Prophet is truthful' and 'The Prophet is not truthful.'
B) 'All humans are mortal' and 'Some humans are mortal.'
C) 'Zakat is obligatory on gold' and 'Zakat is not obligatory on gold.'
D) 'The Qur'an is uncreated' and 'The Qur'an is created.'

Key Takeaway
Logical contradiction strictly requires two propositions differing by affirmation and negation with intrinsic entailment of truth and falsity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following pairs of statements, classify each as a genuine logical contradiction (tanāquḍ) or not, explaining why: (1) 'The world is created' and 'The world is not created.' (2) 'Knowledge is acquired' and 'Knowledge is not acquired through effort.' (3) 'The salat is wajib' and 'The salat is sunnah.'

Propositions

7 min
Why Logicians Focus on Contradiction in Propositions
Commentary justifies focusing on contradictory propositions and addresses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify why logicians restrict their study of contradiction to propositions (qadāyā) rather than single terms (mufradāt), using the arguments from the commentary.

Core Idea
Logic's generality serves its purposes; contradiction in single terms is not needed for syllogisms.

After defining contradiction, the commentator addresses a key question: why do logicians only discuss contradiction in propositions? After all, the rules should be general. The passage presents a debate involving al-Sayyid al-Sharif, al-'Isam, and 'Abd al-Hakim, and concludes that the restriction is justified by the logicians' aims. This lesson unpacks that debate and the underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question being answered
The commentator raises an objection: if the rules of logic are general, why specify the definition for propositions only? Find this objection and the answer.
The objection is: 'Takhṣīṣ al-baḥth bi-tanāquḍ al-qadāyā yunāfī mā taqarrara anna qawāʿid al-fann yajibu an takūna ʿāmmah...'
2
Understand al-Sayyid's claim about single terms
Al-Sayyid al-Sharif claimed that contradiction in single terms is known by analogy (muqāyasa), so it need not be included in the definition.
This is the first position: contradiction in single terms is derivable, not needing explicit study.
3
Examine al-'Isam's objection
Al-'Isam al-Isfarayini objected that knowing a technical term (iṣṭilāḥ) by analogy is unintelligible; it must be defined explicitly.
His point is that technical definitions require stipulation, not analogy.
4
Analyze 'Abd al-Hakim's defense
'Abd al-Hakim al-Siyalkuti explained that 'known by analogy' means that after knowing the general principle (the contradictory of something is its removal) and that truth in terms means predication, the definition of contradiction in single terms can be derived.
This shows that the concept is not unknown; it follows from broader principles.
5
Evaluate the final answer about generality
The passage concludes: the generality of discussions is only with respect to logicians' purposes. Since they have no purpose (gharaḍ) in studying contradictory single terms in themselves, they restrict their study to propositions.
This is a teleological justification: the scope of logic is determined by its aims.

Source Text

العطار

(۱) (قَوْلُهُ : لأن قوله . . . إلخ ) هذا هو رأي المصنف في شرح الأصل، فلذا حذف هذا القيد من تعريفه هو مع ذكر الأصل له ولكن يقال له : إنَّ قوله : بحيث ... إلخ يفيد أيضاً أن يكون الاختلاف بين قضيتين فلا داعي لذكر قضيتين كما لا داعي لذكر الإيجاب والسلب، وكون بعض الفصول يغني عن البعض لا يسوغ الحذف في التعاريف فكان ينبغي اتباع الأصل بذكر هذا القيد لإخراج نحو العدول والتحصيل، وإلا فلا تخرج إلا بقيد بحيث ... إلخ .
Affirmation and negation, and the author did not restrict by that because his statement (1): 'such that it entails... etc.' suffices for it. And differences other than it, like difference by restriction/inclusiveness, modification/emphasis, are excluded, so they are not called contradiction terminologically. Al-Attar: The original here is by definition, and in the five universals by description; you judge it is not hidden, so difference is a remote genus, whether the definition is by definition or description; because the common accident is not taken in the definition according to the later scholars. And he did not say 'difference of two propositions by affirmation and negation' as the author of the original and others said, because the condition 'by itself' suffices for it, since difference other than by affirmation and negation does not entail by itself that one is true and the other false. Rather, they mentioned this condition to realize the concept of contradiction and clarify it, not to be cautious about something, because its concept is only applied to this difference, and if it were left, it would not harm the definition. And he specified the definition to contradiction of propositions because it is the intended consideration and beneficial in syllogisms. As for contradiction in single terms, al-Sayyid said: it is known by analogy, so there is no need to include it in the definition of contradiction. Al-'Isam objected to him that knowing the technical term by analogy is unintelligible. 'Abd al-Hakim answered: the meaning of 'known by analogy' is that after knowing that the contradictory of everything is its removal, and that truth and falsehood in single terms are in the sense of predication, the definition of contradiction in single terms is obtained: it is their difference by affirmation and negation such that by itself the predication of one entails the non-predication of the other. End. If you say: specifying the discussion to contradiction of propositions contradicts what is established that the rules of the art must be general and applicable to all particulars. The answer: the generality of their discussions must only be with respect to their purposes and objectives, and since they have no purpose concerning contradiction between single terms that would be restricted by it, their consideration is restricted to contradiction of propositions. (1) (His statement: Because his saying ... etc.) This is the opinion of the author in his commentary on the original, so he omitted this condition from his definition while the original mentioned it, but it is said to him: his saying 'such that... etc.' also suffices that the difference is between two propositions, so there is no need to mention 'two propositions' just as there is no need to mention 'affirmation and negation'. But the fact that some parts suffice for others does not justify omission in definitions, so he should have followed the original by mentioning this condition to exclude things like modification and emphasis, otherwise they are not excluded except by the condition 'such that... etc.'

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition of logical contradiction (tanāquḍ) and a justification for restricting its study to propositions (qadāyā) rather than single terms (mufradāt). The commentary first notes that the author omitted the explicit condition 'by affirmation and negation' because the phrase 'such that it entails by itself that one is true and the other false' already implies it. Differences that are not by affirmation and negation, such as differences in restriction (hasr) or modification (ta'dīl), are not terminological contradictions. The commentator al-Attar explains that the definition uses 'difference' as a remote genus, and the omission of 'two propositions' is compensated by the condition 'by itself' which inherently implies two propositions. The purpose of mentioning 'by affirmation and negation' is to clarify the concept, not to be cautious, because the term 'contradiction' is only applied to this specific difference. Then the passage discusses why logicians focus on contradictory propositions (tanāquḍ al-qadāyā) rather than contradictory single terms: because it is beneficial in syllogisms. Al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani claimed that contradiction in single terms is known by analogy (muqāyasa), but al-'Isam al-Isfarayini objected that knowing a technical term by analogy is unintelligible. 'Abd al-Hakim al-Siyalkuti defended al-Sayyid by explaining that the meaning is that after knowing that the contradictory of a term is its negation (raf'uhu) and that truth in terms means predication (ḥaml), the definition of contradiction in single terms can be derived: two single terms differ by affirmation and negation such that one's predication entails the non-predication of the other. The passage concludes with an objection and answer: if the rules of logic should be general, why restrict to propositions? The answer is that the generality is only with respect to the logicians' purposes; since they have no purpose (gharaḍ) in studying contradictory single terms in themselves, they restrict it to propositions. The footnote critiques the author's omission of the condition 'two propositions' in his definition, arguing that even if 'such that...' implies it, one should still explicitly mention it to exclude non-contradictory differences like modification and emphasis.

Key Terms
التناقض (al-tanāquḍ): The opposition between two propositions such that by their very nature one is true and the other false, i.e., difference by affirmation and negation that entails truth and falsity. [Note: Stable across the tradition; this commentary refines the definition without altering its core.]
قضية (qadiyya): A proposition (declarative statement) that is the subject of logical contradiction and syllogistic reasoning. [Note: No significant shift.]
مفردات (mufradāt): Single terms (non-propositional expressions), as opposed to propositions. [Note: No significant shift.]
المقايسة (al-muqāyasa): Analogy or comparative reasoning; here it refers to deriving the definition of contradiction in single terms from general principles. [Note: No significant shift.]
غرض (gharaḍ): Purpose or objective; the logicians' purposes determine the scope of their study. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to 'Abd al-Hakim's defense, how is contradiction in single terms known?
A) By direct definition.
B) By analogy from the general principle that the contradictory of something is its removal.
C) By textual evidence from revelation.
D) By intuitive knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Logicians restrict contradiction to propositions because only such contradiction serves their primary goal of analysing syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imagine a student of logic says: 'The definition of contradiction should cover both propositions and single terms, because rules must be general.' Write a response using the arguments from this passage to justify the logicians' restriction.

intermediate
8 min
Distinguishing Genuine Contradiction from Non-Contradictory Differences
Author defines contradiction and excludes three types of non-contradictory differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish genuine contradiction (tanāquḍ) from non-contradictory differences by applying the condition that the difference must entail one true and one false by its own essence.

Core Idea
Not every difference of affirmation and negation is a contradiction; only when the difference alone forces one true, one false.

This passage from the Sharh on contradiction refines the definition by excluding three cases that look like contradictions but are not. Understanding these exclusions sharpens your ability to identify logical conflict in argumentation—crucial for rigorous debate and proof in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Extract the condition of contradiction
The author states that genuine contradiction requires the difference between two propositions to necessitate (لزم) by itself (لذاته) that one is true and the other false. Identify this condition in the first sentence: 'خرج الاختلافُ الذي لا يلزم منه ذلك'—the excluded differences are those from which that result does not follow.
The key phrase is 'لذاته'—by its own essence, not via an intermediary or material specificity.
2
Examine the first excluded difference
The pair 'Zayd is still-fingered' (زيد ساكن الأصابع) and 'Zayd is not moving' (زيد ليس بمتحرك) can both be true. Why? Because 'still-fingered' implies 'not moving'. The difference does not entail that one must be false, so it is not a contradiction.
This shows that when two statements are essentially synonymous, their difference is merely verbal, not logical.
3
Examine the second excluded difference
The pair 'Zayd is human' (زيد إنسان) and 'Zayd is not rational' (زيد ليس بناطق) seems contradictory, but the author explains that truth-falsity does not follow from the difference itself. Instead, it follows because affirming one is equivalent to affirming the other (إيجاب إحداهما في قوة إيجاب الأخرى) and negating one is equivalent to negating the other. The mutual implication (tasāwī) makes the negation of one equivalent to affirming the other, not a direct contradiction.
This type arises when the two terms are logically equivalent, so the difference is not essential.
4
Examine the third excluded difference
Universal and particular opposites (e.g., 'Every human is animal' vs 'No human is animal') can both be false if the subject is broader than the predicate. For example, if we take 'Every animal is human' and 'No animal is human', both are false. Thus the contradiction is not due to the form (quality and quantity) but due to the specific material (المادة).
The author's counterfactual: if it were due to essence, it would hold for all such pairs, but it does not.
5
Apply the condition to new examples
Test a pair like 'This act is obligatory' and 'This act is not obligatory'. This is a genuine contradiction because the difference alone forces one true and one false; no equivalence or material specificity interferes.
Practice identifying which of the three excluded types might apply to pairs like 'Zayd is a father' and 'Zayd is not a parent' (second type: equivalence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خرج الاختلافُ الذي لا يلزم منه ذلك، فإنَّه لا يوجِبُ تحقق التناقض، كالاختلافِ الَّذي بين قولنا : زيد ساكن الأصابع، زيد ليس بمتحرك»، فإنَّه لا يُوجِبُ تحققَ التَّناقض ؛ لصدقِ كلِّ منَ القضيَّتين. وكالاختلافِ الَّذي بينَ قولِنَا : «زيد إنسان، زيد ليس بناطق، فإنّه وإِنْ لزم من صدقٍ كلِّ كذبُ الأخرى وبالعكس ، لكن لا لذات الاختلاف، بل بواسطة أنّ إيجاب إحداهما في قوَّة إيجاب الأخرى، وسلب إحداهما في قوَّة سلب الأخرى . وكالاختلافِ الَّذي بينَ الموجبة والسالبة الكليتين أو الجزئيتين، نحو قولنا : كلُّ إنسان حيوان»، و«لا شيء من الإنسان بحيوان»، و«بعض الإنسان حيوان»، وبعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان»، فإنه وإن لزم منه ذلك، لكن لا لذات الاختلاف، بل لخصوص المادة، ولو كان (١) لذاتِ الاختلافِ لَزِمَ تحقُّقُ التَّناقض في كلِّ كُلِّيَّتين أو جزئيتين، وليس كذلك.
Excluded is the difference from which that (i.e., one true and one false) does not follow, for it does not necessitate the realization of contradiction, such as the difference between our saying: 'Zayd is still-fingered' and 'Zayd is not moving'; it does not necessitate the realization of contradiction because both propositions are true. And like the difference between our saying: 'Zayd is human' and 'Zayd is not rational'—even if from the truth of each follows the falsity of the other and vice versa, it is not due to the essence of the difference, but by intermediary: because affirming one of them is equivalent to affirming the other, and negating one is equivalent to negating the other. And like the difference between the universal or particular affirmative and negative, such as our saying: 'Every human is animal' and 'No human is animal', and 'Some human is animal' and 'Some human is not animal'—for even if that (i.e., one true, one false) follows from it, it is not due to the essence of the difference, but because of the specific material; and if it were due to the essence of the difference, contradiction would necessarily occur in every pair of universals or particulars, but it is not thus.
Source pages p. 638

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh on the chapter of contradiction (tanāquḍ) clarifies the precise condition for genuine contradiction. The author excludes three types of difference that might appear to be contradiction but are not. The condition for contradiction is that the difference between two propositions must, by itself (li-dhātihi), entail that one is true and the other false. The first excluded type is exemplified by 'Zayd is still-fingered' and 'Zayd is not moving'. Here, both propositions can be true simultaneously because 'still-fingered' and 'not moving' are essentially synonymous in this context; hence no contradiction. The second excluded type is 'Zayd is human' and 'Zayd is not rational'. Although it seems that if one is true the other is false, this is not due to the difference itself but because 'human' implies 'rational' and 'non-rational' implies 'non-human'. The mutual implication (tasāwī) makes the negation of one equivalent to affirming the other. The third excluded type involves universal or particular affirmative and negative pairs, such as 'Every human is animal' and 'No human is animal'. Here, it is possible for both to be false if the subject is broader than the predicate (e.g., 'every human is animal' is true, 'no human is animal' is false; but if we take 'every animal is human' and 'no animal is human', both are false). Thus the contradiction does not arise from the mere form of universal/particular opposition but depends on the specific subject-predicate material (mādda). The author concludes that if the difference were due to essence, it would hold for all such pairs, but it does not.

Key Terms
تناقض (tanāquḍ): Contradiction: a relation between two propositions such that they can never be both true or both false; one must be true and the other false by virtue of the difference itself. [Note: No significant shift; stable across logic tradition.]
اختلاف (ikhtilāf): Difference between propositions in terms of affirmation and negation; not every difference is contradiction.
لذاته (li-dhātihi): By its own essence: the property of entailing a logical consequence intrinsically, without external intermediary or specific material. [Note: Common in falsafa and mantiq to distinguish essential from accidental entailment.]
مادة (mādda): The specific subject-predicate content of a proposition that determines its truth value, as opposed to the form (i.e., quantity and quality). [Note: In later logic, 'mādda' can also refer to the matter of a syllogism; here it denotes the semantic content.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following pairs qualifies as a genuine contradiction (tanāquḍ)?
A) Zayd is human and Zayd is not rational.
B) Every human is animal and No human is animal.
C) Zayd is sitting and Zayd is not sitting.
D) Some human is animal and Some human is not animal.

Key Takeaway
Genuine contradiction requires that the truth-falsity follow from the difference alone, not from material content or logical equivalence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the pair of propositions: 'This act is obligatory' (هذا الفعل واجب) and 'This act is not obligatory' (هذا الفعل ليس بواجب). Is this a genuine contradiction? Apply the condition from the passage and explain why or why not.
?
Identify which of the three excluded types applies to the pair: 'Zayd is a Muslim' (زيد مسلم) and 'Zayd is not a believer' (زيد ليس بمؤمن). (Hint: consider the relationship between the terms Muslim and believer.)

Defining Contradiction: Formal Conditions and the Role of Material
Author defines contradiction and explains why form alone is insufficient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the necessary conditions of logical contradiction and determine whether a given pair of propositions is genuinely contradictory using both formal and material criteria.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires difference in quality and quantity, but the material determines whether formal opposites are actually contradictory.

In this lesson, we examine the classical Islamic logical definition of contradiction (tanāquḍ). You will learn that while difference in quality (affirmation/negation) and quantity (universal/particular) is necessary, it is not sufficient alone. The actual subject-predicate relation—the 'material'—must also be such that the propositions cannot be both true or both false. This distinction is crucial for correctly applying contradiction in theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and genus
Locate the term 'tanāquḍ' and note how the author defines it as a difference (ikhtilāf) between two propositions.
2
Extract the formal conditions
The author explicitly requires difference in quality (kayf) and quantity (kamm). Write down these two conditions.
3
Test the conditions with examples
Take the pair 'Every human is an animal' and 'No human is an animal'. It satisfies quality difference (affirmative vs negative) but not quantity difference (both universal). Yet it is contradictory. Why? This reveals that the formal conditions are necessary but not sufficient.
4
Identify the material factor
The author attributes the contradiction to 'the particularity of the material' (khuṣūṣ al-mādda). This means the subject-predicate relation itself (e.g., 'human' is a species of 'animal') forces the truth-values.
5
Apply the material test
For a pair to be genuinely contradictory, it must be impossible for both to be true and impossible for both to be false, given the actual relation between subject and predicate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَتْنُ: فَصْلٌ: فِي التَّنَاقُضِ. وَالتَّنَاقُضُ اخْتِلَافُ الْقَضِيَّتَيْنِ بِالْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ بِحَيْثُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ إِحْدَاهُمَا فِي قُوَّةِ سَلْبِ الْأُخْرَى. وَلاَ بُدَّ فِي التَّنَاقُضِ مِنَ الِاخْتِلَافِ فِي الْكَيْفِ وَالْكَمِّ. وَالِاخْتِلَافُ الَّذِي بَيْنَ الْمُوجَبَةِ وَالسَّالِبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّتَيْنِ أَوِ الْجُزْئِيَّتَيْنِ نَحْوَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَيَوَانٍ، وَبَعْضُ الْإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ وَبَعْضُ الْإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانٍ – فَإِنَّهُ وَإِنْ لَزِمَ مِنْهُ صِدْقُ إِحْدَاهُمَا وَكَذِبُ الْأُخْرَى، لَكِنْ لَا لِذَاتِ الِاخْتِلَافِ بَلْ لِخُصُوصِ الْمَادَّةِ. وَلَوْ كَانَ لِذَاتِ الِاخْتِلَافِ لَلَزِمَ تَحَقُّقُ التَّنَاقُضِ فِي كُلِّ كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ أَوْ جُزْئِيَّتَيْنِ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ. فَإِنَّ الْكُلِّيَّتَيْنِ قَدْ تَكْذِبَانِ مَعًا كَقَوْلِنَا: كُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ إِنْسَانٌ وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْحَيَوَانِ بِإِنْسَانٍ. وَالْجُزْئِيَّتَيْنِ قَدْ تَصْدُقَانِ مَعًا كَقَوْلِنَا: بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ وَبَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ.
Chapter: On Contradiction. Contradiction is the difference of two propositions in affirmation and negation such that from one of them follows, in the strength of the negation of the other. In contradiction, difference in quality and quantity is necessary. The difference between the universal affirmative and negative, or the particular affirmative and negative, like our saying: 'Every human is an animal' and 'No human is an animal', and 'Some human is an animal' and 'Some human is not an animal' – for even if from that follows the truth of one and the falsehood of the other, it is not due to the nature of the difference but due to the particularity of the material. And if it were due to the nature of the difference, then contradiction would necessarily hold in every [pair of] universal or particular propositions, but it is not so. For the two universal propositions may both be false, as in our saying: 'Every animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human'. And the two particular propositions may both be true, as in our saying: 'Some animal is a human' and 'Some animal is not a human'.
Source pages p. 639

Commentary
This passage defines logical contradiction (tanāquḍ) and clarifies its necessary conditions. The author first gives the definition: two propositions differ in quality (affirmation/negation) such that one implies the falsehood of the other. Then he adds that difference in both quality and quantity (universal/particular) is required. Using examples, he shows that mere difference in quality and quantity is not sufficient for contradiction; the specific subject and predicate (the material) must be such that the propositions cannot be both true or both false. The universal pair 'Every human is an animal' and 'No human is an animal'—the first is true, the second false—are contradictory, but this is due to the material (human is a proper species of animal). If contradiction were due only to the formal difference, then every universal affirmative/negative pair would be contradictory. But that is not the case: 'Every animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human' are both false, since some animals are human and some are not. Similarly, particular propositions like 'Some animal is a human' and 'Some animal is not a human' are both true. Therefore, genuine contradiction requires that the propositions are exact opposites, which depends on the relation between subject and predicate. The commentary (from al-Dusuqi and al-‘Attar) further explains that the condition of difference in quantity prevents cases where both universals are false or both particulars true.

Key Terms
التناقض
الكيف
الكم
خصوص المادة

Key Takeaway
True contradiction requires not only formal opposition in quality and quantity but also a material relation that makes simultaneous truth or falsehood impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Determine whether they meet the formal conditions for contradiction. Then consider the material: is it possible for both to be false? Use your knowledge of Islamic rulings on intoxicants.

Indefinite Propositions Are Treated as Particulars in Contradiction
Author explains that indefinite propositions are considered quantified and follow the same contradiction rules as particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an indefinite proposition and correctly determine its contradictory by applying the rule that it is treated as a particular.

Core Idea
An indefinite proposition without a quantifier is logically equivalent to a particular – its contradictory is a universal negative.

In this passage, the author clarifies a key rule about indefinite propositions (المهملة). Many statements in everyday language and Islamic sciences lack a quantifier, e.g., 'Water is pure'. How do we determine the contradictory of such a statement? The author explains that indefinite propositions are actually considered among quantified propositions because they have the same logical force as particular propositions. This ensures we can apply contradiction rules correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Check if the proposition has a quantifier (all, none, some) or no quantifier. If no quantifier, it is indefinite (المهملة).
2
Recognize its force as particular
Recall that an indefinite proposition is treated as a particular (جزئية) in logical strength. For example, 'Man is animal' affirms animality for at least some individuals, not necessarily all.
This is because the truth of the indefinite requires only that the predicate holds for some individuals of the subject.
3
Determine the contradictory
If the indefinite is affirmative, its contradictory is a universal negative. If it is negative, its contradictory is a universal affirmative (by analogy; the passage explicitly states the affirmative case).
For indefinite affirmative: contradictory is 'No S is P'. For indefinite negative: contradictory is 'Every S is P'.
4
Verify with an Islamic example
Test with a fiqh example: 'الماء طاهر' (Water is pure) is indefinite affirmative. Its contradictory is 'لا شيء من الماء بطاهر' (No water is pure). This rule prevents errors in legal debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي التَّنَاقُضِ واعلَمْ أنَّ المهملة من المحصوراتِ في الحقيقة؛ لما مر من أنَّها في قوَّة الجزئية، فحكمها كحكمها .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْمُهْمَلَةَ ... إلخ) جواب عمَّا يرد على الشَّارحِ مِنْ أَنَّهُ ذكرَ أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ مِنَ الاختلافِ في الكيف في الشَّخصيَّتين، والكيف والكم في المحصورتين، وأهمل المهملتين .
(قَوْلُهُ : فِي قُوَّةِ الْجُزْيَّيَّةِ) أي : لأَنَّكَ إِذا قُلْتَ: الإنسانُ حيوان؛ كانَ ثبوت الحيوانية لبعض أفرادِ الإنسانِ مُحقَّقاً ؛ سواءٌ أردتَ مِنَ الموضوع كُلَّ أفرادِهِ أَوْ بعضَها .
(قَوْلُهُ : فَحُكْمُهَا ) أي : المهملة (كَحُكْمِهَا)؛ أي: الجزئية في التناقض، فإنْ كانت المهملة موجبةٌ، فَنقيضُها سالةٌ كُلّتة .
Chapter on Contradiction. And know that the indefinite proposition is among the quantified propositions in reality; because it has been mentioned that it is in the strength of the particular, so its ruling is like its ruling.
Al-Dasūqī: His statement: 'And know that the indefinite... etc.' is a response to what is raised against the commentator: that he stated that there must be a difference in quality in the two singular propositions, and in quality and quantity in the two quantified propositions, but he neglected the two indefinite propositions. His statement: 'in the strength of the particular' means that when you say: 'Man is animal', the affirmation of animality for some individuals of man is realized, whether you intended by the subject all its individuals or some of them. His statement: 'so its ruling' i.e., the indefinite (is like its ruling) i.e., the particular in contradiction. So if the indefinite is affirmative, its contradictory is a universal negative.
Source pages p. 643

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of how indefinite (unquantified) propositions are treated in the logic of contradiction. It begins with a chapter heading 'On Contradiction' and then states the rule: indefinite propositions are actually considered among the quantified propositions because, as previously mentioned, they are equivalent in logical force to particular propositions. Therefore, the rules of contradiction that apply to particular propositions also apply to indefinite ones.

Al-Dasūqī's commentary clarifies the context: the main text had already discussed conditions for contradiction in singular and quantified propositions, but omitted the indefinite. This passage fills that gap. The commentary explains why indefinite propositions are like particulars: when you say 'Man is animal' without a quantifier, the affirmation holds for at least some individuals of man, regardless of whether you meant all or some. So the truth condition is the same as a particular affirmative. Consequently, the contradictory of an indefinite affirmative is a universal negative, exactly as with a particular affirmative.

This is an important point in Islamic logic because many everyday statements (e.g., 'Water is pure' in fiqh) are indefinite and must be understood correctly for contradiction. The rule ensures that the indefinite is not mistakenly treated as universal, which would change its contradictory.

Key Takeaway
