The Conditions of Contradiction: Union and the Indefinite Rule
Author explains the condition of union and corrects a mistaken view about indefinite contradictories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the conditions for a valid contradiction, including the correct treatment of indefinite propositions.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires union in all but three aspects; an indefinite’s contradictory is a universal.

In this lesson, we examine the precise requirements for two propositions to be genuine contradictories. The author explains that difference is needed in quality, quantity, and modality, while union is needed in everything else. He then clarifies a common error: some think an indefinite proposition's contradictory is simply its opposite in quality, but that is incorrect. An indefinite must be treated as a particular, so its contradictory is a universal of the opposite quality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two requirements for contradiction
Recognize that difference is required in quality, quantity, and modality, while union (identity) is required in all other respects such as subject, predicate, time, place, condition, etc.
Union means the two propositions must share the same subject and predicate, and be about the same time and place.
2
Understand what 'union' includes
List the elements that must be identical: subject, predicate, time, place, condition, relation, etc. For example, 'Zayd is standing in the house at noon' and 'Zayd is not standing in the house at noon' share all these elements.
The only allowable differences are in quality (affirmative/negative), quantity (universal/particular/singular), and modality (necessity/possibility/impossibility).
3
Recognize the error in forming the contradictory of an indefinite
Some hold that simply changing the quality of an indefinite proposition yields its contradictory. For example, they claim the contradictory of 'Man is a writer' (indefinite affirmative) is 'Man is not a writer' (indefinite negative). Test this: both can be true if some men write and some do not, so they are not contradictories.
The correct contradictory must be such that one is true and the other false with no middle ground.
4
Apply the correct rule for indefinite propositions
Treat an indefinite proposition as equivalent to a particular proposition in force. Therefore, its contradictory is the universal of the opposite quality. For the indefinite affirmative 'Man is a writer,' the contradictory is the universal negative 'No man is a writer.'
This ensures that the two propositions cannot both be true nor both false.
5
Verify with a concrete example
Consider 'An animal is a human' (indefinite affirmative). Its contradictory is 'No animal is a human' (universal negative). The pair 'An animal is a human' and 'An animal is not a human' (two indefinites) can both be false if some animals are human and some not, so they are not contradictories.
The example comes from al-ʿAṭṭār's commentary and illustrates the rule clearly.

وَالاتِّحَادِ) بالجرّ ؛ عطف على قوله : «الاختلاف»؛ أي: كَمَا لَا بدَّ في تحققِ التَّناقض من الاختلافِ في الأمور الثلاثة المذكورة، وهي: الكَيف ، والكم ، والجِهَةُ ؛ كذلك لا بدَّ في التناقض من الاتحادِ (فِيمَا عَدَاهَا؛ أي: فِيمَا عَدا الكيف والكمّ والجِهَة .
فلا بد في التناقض من اختلاف واتّحادٍ ؛ أما الاختلاف، ففي الأمور الثَّلاثة المذكورة، وأمّا الاتحاد، ففيما عداها .
واختلف في ذلكَ، فَقِيلَ :
الدسوقي
وإنْ كانَتْ سالبةٌ؛ فَنقيضُهَا مُوجبةٌ كُلِّيَّة، وَلَا يكتفى فيها بمجرد الاختلافِ بالكيف كَما هو صريح متنِ السُّلَّم في قوله :
فإن تكن شخصيَّةٌ أومُهمله فَنَقضُهَا بِالكيف أنْ تُبْدِلَة
فإِنَّهُ يقتضي أنَّ نقيض : الإنسانُ كاتب ؛ الإنسانُ ليس بكاتب، وليس كذلك
فالحق أنَّها مثل الجزئية. (قَوْلُهُ : فِي ذَلِكَ) أي : فيما عَدَاهَا
العطار
الاختلافُ بين المهملة والكُلِّيَّةِ ؛ تحقَّقَ التَّناقض بينَهُما كَمَا يتحقَّقُ بين الجزئيَّةِ والكُلِّيَّةِ كَقولِنَا : الحيوانُ إنسانُ وَلَا شيء مِنَ الحيوانِ بِإنسان، وَأَمَّا إذا وقعَ بينَ المهملتيْنَ؛ فَلَا يتحقَّقُ التَّناقضُ بينَهُما لِصدقِهَا ؛ لكونها في قوَّةِ الجزئيَّةِ كَقَولِنَا : الحيوان إنسان، الحيوان ليس بإنسان.
(قَوْلُهُ : بِالْجَرّ) والرَّفْعُ صحيحٌ أيضاً بجعله مبتداً، والخبرُ قوله : (فيما عداهَا)، أو يقدَّرُ لَا بُدَّ منه، إلَّا أَنَّ الأَوْلى الجرُّ لِسلامتِهِ عَن التَّقدير، وهذا شروع في ذِكْرِ شروط تَحقُّقِ التَّناقض بعدَ تَعريفِهِ؛ لأنَّ التَّعريفَ إِنَّمَا يُفيدُ معرفةً مَفهومِهِ، وتمييزه عَمَّا عداه لا طريق عمله .
(قَوْلُهُ : فَقِيلَ) حكاه بصيغة التّمريض؛ لِضَعفِهِ.
('And union') is in the genitive, coordinated with his saying 'the difference'; i.e., just as in the realization of contradiction, difference is necessary in the three mentioned matters, namely quality, quantity, and modality, so too in contradiction, union is necessary (in what is other than them), i.e., in what is other than quality, quantity, and modality. So in contradiction, difference and union are necessary. As for difference, it is in the three mentioned matters, and as for union, it is in what is other than them. And there is disagreement about that. Some said: al-Dasuqi: And if it is negative, then its contradictory is a universal affirmative, and it is not sufficient to merely differ in quality, as is clear from the text of al-Sullam in his saying: 'If it is singular or indefinite, then its contradictory is by quality, you change it.' For this implies that the contradictory of 'Man is a writer' is 'Man is not a writer,' and it is not so. So the truth is that it is like the particular. (His saying: 'in that') i.e., in what is other than them. al-ʿAṭṭār: The difference between the indefinite and the universal: contradiction is realized between them just as it is realized between the particular and the universal, as in our saying: 'An animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human.' But if it occurs between two indefinites, contradiction is not realized between them because they can both be true, since it is in the force of the particular, as in our saying: 'An animal is a human' and 'An animal is not a human.' (His saying: 'in the genitive') And the nominative is also correct by making it a subject, and the predicate is his saying (in what is other than them), or one may estimate 'it is necessary,' except that the genitive is better because it avoids estimation. This is a beginning in mentioning the conditions for the realization of contradiction after its definition, because the definition only gives knowledge of its concept and distinguishing it from what is other than it, not the way of its operation. (His saying: 'and some said') He narrates it in the form of expressing weakness, because of its weakness.
Source pages p. 644

Commentary
This passage establishes the conditions for a valid contradiction (تناقض). The author first explains that union (اتحاد) is required in everything except quality, quantity, and modality. That is, the two propositions must have the same subject, predicate, time, place, condition, etc. For example, 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not standing' are contradictories only if the same Zayd, same time, etc. Then the passage discusses a disagreement (اختلاف) regarding the indefinite proposition. Some hold that an indefinite singular proposition's contradictory is formed merely by changing the quality (e.g., 'Man is a writer' and 'Man is not a writer'), but this is rejected because two indefinites can both be true. The correct view is that an indefinite is like a particular: its contradictory is the universal of the opposite quality. So the contradictory of 'Man is a writer' (indefinite affirmative) is 'No man is a writer' (universal negative), not 'Man is not a writer' (indefinite negative). The commentator al-ʿAṭṭār illustrates: 'An animal is a human' (indefinite affirmative) and 'No animal is a human' (universal negative) are contradictories, but 'An animal is a human' and 'An animal is not a human' (two indefinites) can both be false if some animals are human and some not, so they are not contradictories. Finally, the passage refines the grammatical parsing: the word 'والاتِّحَادِ' is in the genitive to avoid needing an implied verb. This section marks the transition from defining contradiction to explaining its operational conditions.

Key Terms
اتحاد
اختلاف
مهملة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Concerning the condition of union (ittiḥād) in contradiction, which of the following must be identical between the two propositions?
A) Quality and quantity
B) Modality and quality
C) Subject and predicate
D) Quantity and modality

Key Takeaway
For a valid contradiction, the two propositions must differ in quality, quantity, and modality, but agree in all other respects; an indefinite proposition is treated like a particular, so its contradictory is a universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the pair: 'Every sale is permissible' (universal affirmative) and 'Some sales are not permissible' (particular negative). Do they satisfy the conditions for contradiction? Explain why or why not.
?
Correct the following claim: 'The contradictory of "A scholar is knowledgeable" (indefinite affirmative) is "A scholar is not knowledgeable" (indefinite negative).' Provide the correct contradictory and justify your answer using the rule from this lesson.

The Eight Conditions for Contradiction
Examples illustrating non-contradiction due to modal and part/whole differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an apparent contradiction between two propositions is genuine by applying the eight-units condition.

Core Idea
Not all differences are contradictions; eight conditions must match.

In logic, contradiction requires more than just opposite truth values. The ancients identified eight units—such as subject, predicate, time, place, part/whole, potentiality/actuality—that must be identical for two propositions to be truly contradictory. This lesson uses examples to illustrate two of those conditions.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the apparent contradiction
Look at the two statements about wine in the jar: one says it is intoxicating, the other says it is not. They seem contradictory at first glance.
This is a classic example from the text.
2
Identify the hidden difference
The first statement refers to potential intoxication (by power), the second to actual intoxication (by act). They differ in modality.
Modality is one of the eight units.
3
Apply the condition: modality
Since the two statements differ in mode (potential vs actual), they do not meet the condition of unity in modality. Therefore, they are not genuine contradictions.
The text explicitly says they differ due to 'al-quwwa wa al-fi‘l'.
4
Apply the second example
Consider the statements about a black man: 'He is black' (meaning part) and 'He is not black' (meaning entirety). The difference in part vs whole also prevents contradiction.
Part/whole is another of the eight units.
5
Generalize: the eight units must be unified
These examples illustrate two of the eight units. For a true contradiction, all eight units—subject, predicate, time, place, part/whole, potentiality/actuality, etc.—must be identical.
The passage mentions these as examples from the ancients.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي التَّنَاقُضِ. وَلَا الْخَمْرُ فِي الدَّنِّ مُسْكِرٌ أَيْ: بِالْقُوَّةِ، الْخَمْرُ فِي الدَّنِّ لَيْسَ بِمُسْكِرٍ أَيْ: بِالْفِعْلِ؛ لِاخْتِلَافِ الْقُوَّةِ وَالْفِعْلِ. وَلَا الزِّنْجِيُّ أَسْوَدُ أَيْ: بَعْضُهُ، الزِّنْجِيُّ لَيْسَ بِأَسْوَدَ أَيْ: كُلُّهُ؛ لِاخْتِلَافِ الْجُزْءِ وَالْكُلِّ. فَهَذِهِ الْوَحْدَاتُ الثَّمَانِيَةُ الَّتِي ذَكَرَهَا الْقُدَمَاءُ فِي تَحَقُّقِ التَّنَاقُضِ. قَالَ الْعِصَامُ: إِنَّمَا ذَكَرُوهَا مَعَ أَنَّ تَعْرِيفَ التَّنَاقُضِ يَتَكَفَّلُ بِتَمْيِيزِهِ عَمَّا عَدَاهُ؛ لِأَنَّهُ كَثِيرًا مَا يَعْرِضُ الْغَلَطُ لِلْمُتَعَلِّمِ مِنْ مُشَاهَدَةِ الِاخْتِلَافِ بَيْنَ الْقَضِيَّتَيْنِ فَيَظُنُّهُ مُوجِبًا لِلتَّنَاقُضِ لِعَدَمِ تَنَبُّهِهِ لِإِضْمَارِ مَا أَخْرَجَ الِاخْتِلَافَ مِنَ الِاقْتِضَاءِ الْمَذْكُورِ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ، إِمَّا بِإِخْرَاجِهِ عَنْ أَصْلِ التَّنَاقُضِ، أَوِ الِاخْتِلَافِ لِذَاتِهِ، فَذَكَرُوا عِدَّةً مِنَ الْأُمُورِ الْعَارِضَةِ لِلِاخْتِلَافِ تَمْكِينًا لِلْمُتَعَلِّمِ فِي مَقَامِ التَّنْبِيهِ وَتَمْيِيزًا لَهُ فِي التَّفَحُّصِ عَنْ تَحَقُّقِ الِاخْتِلَافِ الْمَذْكُورِ.
Chapter on Contradiction. And 'Wine in the jar is intoxicating' i.e., potentially, 'Wine in the jar is not intoxicating' i.e., actually, due to difference of potentiality and actuality. And 'The black man is black' i.e., part of him, 'The black man is not black' i.e., entirely, due to difference of part and whole. So these are the eight units that the ancients mentioned for the realization of contradiction. Al-‘Iṣām said: They only mentioned them even though the definition of contradiction suffices to distinguish it from what is other than it because often a student makes a mistake from observing the difference between two propositions and thinks it necessitates contradiction due to his lack of attention to the implication of what extracted the difference from the requirement mentioned in the definition, either by removing it from the origin of contradiction or the difference in its essence. So they mentioned a number of incidental matters to the difference to enable the student in the place of attention and to distinguish him in investigating the realization of the mentioned difference.
Source pages p. 647

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition on the conditions for contradiction. The author lists two examples that illustrate how apparent contradictions can be avoided by specifying the mode (potential/actual) and the quantity (part/whole). He then states that these examples are part of the eight units (conditions) that the ancients required for a genuine contradiction. The commentary by al-‘Iṣām explains why they listed these examples: the definition of contradiction already distinguishes true contradiction from apparent difference, but beginners often mistake any difference for contradiction. So they provided these examples to train the student to recognize when a difference is merely accidental (like differing in potentiality vs actuality or part vs whole) and not a true contradiction in the essence. All examples are from everyday statements about wine and skin color, relevant to Islamic legal reasoning (e.g., wine in the jar is potentially intoxicating but not actually, so no contradiction). The eight units are not detailed here but are implied by these and similar examples.

Key Terms
التناقض
الوحدات الثمانية
القوة والفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because they are not about the same subject.
B) Because the first means potentially and the second actually.
C) Because one is affirmative and the other negative.
D) Because they have different predicates.

Key Takeaway
Genuine contradiction requires unity in eight conditions, including modality and part/whole.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Reduction of the Eight Unities to Two
Author states reduction of eight unities to two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the eight unities required for contradiction and explain how later logicians reduce them to two.

Core Idea
Contradiction only needs unity of subject and predicate; the rest are subsumed.

Before you can determine whether two propositions truly contradict each other, you must know what conditions (unities) they must share. Classical logicians enumerated eight such unities, but later scholars simplified this to two. This lesson walks you through that reduction, showing how the remaining six are subsumed under subject or predicate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the eight traditional unities
The eight unities are: subject, predicate, time, place, condition, relation, potentiality, actuality, part/whole. The passage mentions condition, part, whole, time, place, relation, potentiality, and actuality. Note that subject and predicate are the two primary ones.
Part and whole are sometimes considered one unity.
2
Understand the later reduction
Later logicians state that only two unities are essential: unity of subject and unity of predicate. The remaining six are subsumed: condition, part, and whole are subsumed under subject; time, place, relation, potentiality, and actuality are subsumed under predicate.
This is stated clearly in the passage after 'فَصْلٌ فِي التَّنَافُضِ'.
3
Apply the reduction to examples
The passage provides examples: 'Zayd is standing by night' vs 'Zayd is not standing by night' — time modifes the predicate, so it belongs to predicate unity. 'The one standing by night is Zayd' vs 'The one standing by night is not Zayd' — time modifies subject, so it belongs to subject unity. Check how each secondary unity can be attached to either subject or predicate depending on phrasing.
This demonstrates that the reduction is not arbitrary but depends on grammatical role.
4
Interpret the classification rule
The final classification says: condition, part, whole go with subject; time, place, relation, potentiality, actuality go with predicate. Memorize this as a practical rule for checking contradiction in any pair of propositions.
The rule is found in the 'فصل في التناقض' paragraph.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنطقية
لَمْ يَختلفًا في الشَّرط والجزء والكلِّ ؛ لأنّ المهملتين يصدقان(۱) وإن اتفقا الوحدات كُلِّها.
وأما عند المتأخرين؛ فيكفي وَحْدَتان: وحدة الموضوع، ووحدة المحمول، والوحدات الباقية مندرجةً فيهما
الدسوقي
في
وقد
(۲)
(قَوْلُهُ: وَحْدَتَانِ: وَحْدَةُ الْمَوْضُوع، وَوَحْدَةُ الْمَحْمُوْلِ) الأَوْلَى عدمُ التَّقييد؛ إذْ هذه الوحداتُ الثَّمانية قد ترجع إلى المحمول بتمامها، وقد ترجع إلى الموضوع، يرجعُ ما ذكرَ أَنَّهُ مُندرج في وحدة الموضوع؛ لوحدة المحمول، وَمَا ذكرَ أَنَّهُ مندرج في وحدة المحمول لوحدة الموضوع، مثلاً : زيد قائم ليلاً، زيد ليس بقائم ليلاً، وحدةُ الزَّمانِ فيه ترجع لوحدة المحمول، ونحو : القائم (٢) ليلاً زيد، ليسَ القائم ليلاً زيداً، وحدةُ الزَّمانِ فيه ترجع إلى وحدة الموضوع، وَكَذا وحدةُ الشَّرط، ووحدة الجزء أو الكُلِّ ، أمَّا رجوعُهُما إلى وحدة الموضوع؛ فَظاهر، وأمَّا رجوعُهُمَا إلى وحدة المحمول؛ فكما لَو قُلْتَ : المفرِّقُ للبصر الجسم، وليس المفرِّقُ للبصر الجسم ؛ تعيَّن بشرط كون الجسم أبيض فيهما ، أو أسود فيهما، وكما لو قُلْتَ: الأسودُ الزّنجي، ليس الأسودَ الزّنجيّ، تعيَّن كلُّه فيهما ، أو بعضُهُ فيهما ، هذا مُحصِّلُ مَا اعترض به السَّعْد .
وأجابَ بعضُ أشياخِنَا بِمَا مُحصِّلُهُ : أَنَّ الزَّمان والمكانَ وَمَا بعدهما ؛ إذا رجعت للموضوع؛ كانَتْ شروطاً، فتكون داخلةٌ في الشَّرطِ، فتأمله . اهـ من خطّ شيخنا .
العطّار
(قَوْلُهُ : فَيَكْفِي وَحْدَتَانِ قال في شرح المطالعِ : واكتفى الفارابي منها بثلاث وحدات؛ وحدة الموضوع والمحمول والزَّمانِ.
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ : يصدقان ... إلخ) نحو الحيوان إنسان، الحيوان ليس بإنسان. (۲) (قَوْلُهُ : ونحو : القائم ... إلخ) لا يخفى ما فيه من التكلف إذ بجعل الزمان قيداً للموضوع يجعل القضيتين مهملتين بعد أن كانتا شخصيتين وقد سبق أن المهملة لا تناقضها مثلها .
فَصْلٌ فِي التَّنَافُضِ
فوحدةُ الشَّرطِ والجزء والكلِّ مندرجة في وحدة الموضوع، ووحدة الزَّمانِ، والمكان، والإضافة، والقوة، والفعل مندرجة في وحدة المحمول، وذلك ظاهرُ عند المتأمّل .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : فَوَحْدَةُ الشَّرْطِ . . . إِلَخ ، مُنْدَرِجَةٌ ... إلخ ) أي : لأنَّ الشَّرطَ في الحقيقة وصف للموضوع. وحاصله : أنَّ هذا الاختلاف لفظي ؛ لأنَّها في الحقيقة ترجع لمعنى واحد. (قَوْلُهُ : مُنْدَرِجَةٌ فِي وَحْدَةِ الْمَوْضُوع) مثلاً : اللُّونُ مُفرِّقُ للبصرِ بشرط كونه - أي : اللون - أبيض، اللون ليسَ مُفرّقاً للبصر بشرط كونه أبيض، يرجع إلى قولنا : اللَّونُ الأبيضُ مُفرّقٌ لِلبصر، اللَّونُ الأبيضُ ليسَ مُفرِّقاً للبصر ، فوحدةُ الشَّرطِ فيه رجعَتْ للموضوع.
وَكَذا يرجعُ قولنا : الزّنجي أسودُ؛ أي: كُلُّهُ، الزّنجي ليس بأسود؛ أي: كلُّه؛ إلى قولنا : كلُّ الزّنجي أسود، كلُّ الزّنجي (١) ليس بأسود، وَكَذا يرجعُ قولنا : الزّنجيُّ أسود؛ أي: بعضُهُ، الزّنجي ليس بأسود؛ أي: بعضُه، إلى قولنا : بعضُ الزنجي أسود، بعضُ الزنجي (٢) ليس بأسود. (قَوْلُهُ : مُنْدَرِجَةٌ فِي وَحْدَةِ الْمَحْمُوْلِ) أي : فيرجعُ قولنا : زيد قائم؛ أي : ليلاً زيد ليس بقائم؛ أي: ليلاً، إلى قولنا : زيد قائم في اللَّيلِ، زيدٌ ليس بقائم في اللَّيلِ. (قَوْلُهُ : فَوَحْدَةُ الشَّرْطِ ... إِلَخ) لأنَّ الجسم الأبيض غيرُ الجسمِ الأسود، وكُلّ العين غير بعضها
(قَوْلُهُ : وَوَحْدَةُ الزَّمَانِ وَالمَكَانِ وَالإِضَافَةِ وَالقُوَّةِ وَالفِعْل مُنْدَرِجَةٌ تَحْتَ وَحْدَةِ الْمَحْمُوْلِ) لأنَّ النَّائمَ لَيلاً مثلاً ليسَ بِنائم نهاراً ، والقائم في السُّوقِ غيرُ القائم في
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ : كل الزنجي ... إلخ) الصواب : بعض الزنجي ليس بأسود؛ إذ نقيض الموجبة الكلية سالبة جزئية .
(۲) (قَوْلُهُ : بعض الزنجي ... إلخ) الصواب: لا شيء من الزنجي بأسود؛ إذ نقيض الموجبة الجزئية سالبة كلية . أ . هـ.
الشرنوبي .
The logical. They did not differ regarding condition, part, and whole, because the two indefinite [propositions] are true even if they agree in all unities. And as for the later [scholars], two unities suffice: unity of subject and unity of predicate, and the remaining unities are subsumed under them. Al-Dasūqī: His statement: 'Two unities: unity of subject and unity of predicate' — better would be not to restrict, for these eight unities may revert entirely to the predicate, and may revert to the subject. What he mentioned as subsumed under unity of subject reverts to unity of predicate, and what he mentioned as subsumed under unity of predicate reverts to unity of subject. For example: 'Zayd is standing by night, Zayd is not standing by night' — the unity of time here reverts to unity of predicate. Similarly: 'The one standing by night is Zayd, the one standing by night is not Zayd' — the unity of time here reverts to unity of subject. Likewise the unity of condition, and the unity of part or whole. As for their reverting to unity of subject, it is apparent. As for their reverting to unity of predicate, as if you said: 'The thing that differentiates vision is body, and the thing that differentiates vision is not body' — it is specified by the condition of the body being white in both, or black in both. And as if you said: 'The black [thing] is the black person, the black [thing] is not the black person' — it is specified that it is all in both, or some of it in both. This is the summary of what al-Sa‘d objected. And one of our teachers responded with a summary: that time, place, and what follows them, if they revert to the subject, become conditions, so they enter into the condition. So reflect. End of the writing of our teacher. Al-‘Aṭṭār: His statement: 'So two unities suffice' — he said in Sharh al-Maṭāli‘: Al-Fārābī sufficed with three unities: unity of subject, predicate, and time. Footnote (1): His statement: 'The two are true... etc.' such as 'Animal is human, animal is not human.' Footnote (2): His statement: 'And similar: the one standing... etc.' It is not hidden what is in it of strained interpretation, for by making time a qualification of the subject, he makes the two propositions indefinite after they were individual, and it has preceded that an indefinite does not contradict its like. Chapter on Contradiction. So the unity of condition, part, and whole is subsumed under unity of subject, and the unity of time, place, relation, potentiality, and actuality is subsumed under unity of predicate, and that is apparent to the one who reflects. Al-Dasūqī: His statement: 'So the unity of condition... etc., subsumed... etc.' means because the condition is in reality a description of the subject. The gist is that this difference is verbal, because in reality they revert to one meaning. His statement: 'subsumed under unity of subject' — for example: 'Color differentiates vision with the condition of it being white, color does not differentiate vision with the condition of it being white' reverts to our statement: 'White color differentiates vision, white color does not differentiate vision'; so the unity of condition here reverted to the subject. And similarly our statement: 'The black person is black, meaning all of him, the black person is not black, meaning all of him' reverts to our statement: 'Every black person is black, every black person is not black.' And similarly our statement: 'The black person is black, meaning some of him, the black person is not black, meaning some of him' reverts to our statement: 'Some black person is black, some black person is not black.' His statement: 'subsumed under unity of predicate' means our statement: 'Zayd is standing, meaning at night, Zayd is not standing, meaning at night' reverts to our statement: 'Zayd is standing at night, Zayd is not standing at night.' His statement: 'So the unity of condition... etc.' because the white body is not the black body, and the whole eye is not some of it. His statement: 'And the unity of time, place, relation, potentiality, and actuality is subsumed under unity of predicate' because the one who sleeps at night, for example, is not sleeping during the day, and the one standing in the market is not the one standing in... Footnote (1): His statement: 'Every black person... etc.' — the correct is: 'Some black person is not black' because the contradictory of the universal affirmative is a particular negative. Footnote (2): His statement: 'Some black person... etc.' — the correct is: 'No black person is black' because the contradictory of the particular affirmative is a universal negative. End. Al-Sharnūbī.
Source pages p. 648

Commentary
This passage is primarily definitional, clarifying the conditions (unities) required for contradiction (tanāquḍ) between two propositions. The discussion revolves around the eight traditional unities (subject, predicate, time, place, condition, relation, potentiality, actuality, part/whole) and how they are reduced to just two by later logicians.

The text opens with a statement that early and later scholars agreed on three of the unities (condition, part, whole) but differed on others. The later logicians held that only two unities are essential: unity of subject (wihdat al-mawḍū‘) and unity of predicate (wihdat al-maḥmūl); the remaining six are subsumed under these two. This is the core claim.

Al-Dasūqī then objects, arguing that it is artificial to restrict the unities to two, because any of the eight can be seen as reverting to either subject or predicate depending on how the proposition is phrased. He gives examples: 'Zayd is standing by night' vs. 'Zayd is not standing by night' – here time modifies the predicate, so unity of time belongs to predicate. But if we say 'The one standing by night is Zayd' vs. 'The one standing by night is not Zayd', time modifies the subject, so unity of time belongs to subject. Similarly, condition and part/whole can be attached to either. He thus argues that the reduction is not absolute.

A response from one of his teachers is then given: time, place, and similar elements, when they revert to the subject, are conditions (shurūṭ) and thus fall under the unity of condition, which itself is subsumed under the subject. This suggests the difference is merely verbal.

Al-‘Aṭṭār adds a historical note that Al-Fārābī required three unities (subject, predicate, and time), indicating a middle position.

The text then presents a formal section heading 'Chapter on Contradiction' and states the definitive classification: unity of condition, part, and whole are subsumed under unity of subject; unity of time, place, relation, potentiality, and actuality are subsumed under unity of predicate. This is explained with examples: color with a condition reverts to 'white color', and 'every black person' etc. The commentary expands on each, showing how the eight are reducible to two in principle.

Overall, the passage teaches that the elaborate list of eight unities is not arbitrary; it can be simplified to the two fundamental elements of any proposition: subject and predicate. The intermediate details (condition, part/whole) are aspects of the subject; the rest (time, place, etc.) are aspects of the predicate. This reduction is accepted by later logicians and is seen as a refinement for practical application in determining contradiction in dialectical argumentation.

Propositions
advanced
10 min
Al-Dasūqī's Objection to the Reduction
Author presents objection and response to reduction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze al-Dasūqī's objection to the reduction of unities and evaluate the teacher's response.

Core Idea
Al-Dasūqī argues the reduction is not absolute; the teacher responds that time/place become conditions when attached to subject.

The neat reduction of eight unities to two was not accepted without question. Al-Dasūqī raised a penetrating objection: the assignment of secondary unities to subject or predicate is not fixed but depends on grammatical expression. His critique and the subsequent response deepen our understanding of how the unities actually function. This lesson dissects that dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Dasūqī's objection
Al-Dasūqī points out that the same secondary unity (e.g., time) can be attached to either subject or predicate depending on how the proposition is phrased. For example, 'Zayd is standing by night' puts time with predicate; 'The one standing by night is Zayd' puts time with subject. So it is not absolute that time always falls under predicate.
This challenges the neat classification given in the main reduction.
2
Examine al-Dasūqī's examples
He provides two sets: (1) 'Zayd is standing by night' vs its negation – time is with predicate. (2) 'The one standing by night is Zayd' vs its negation – time is with subject. Also condition and part/whole: he argues they too can flip sides.
This shows the classification is not inherent but depends on the logical subject of the proposition.
3
Understand the teacher's response
The teacher (one of al-Dasūqī's teachers) says that when time, place, etc., revert to the subject, they become conditions (shurūṭ) and thus fall under the unity of condition, which in turn is subsumed under subject. Similarly, when they revert to predicate, they remain as part of predicate. So the overall classification is preserved, but the path may be indirect.
The response aims to show that the reduction is still valid, though the route may vary.
4
Evaluate the resolution
Consider whether the teacher's response fully resolves al-Dasūqī's objection. Does it simply rename the issue? The teacher's point is that the eight unities are not distinct in essence; they are just aspects of the two primary terms. The difference is verbal. Assess if this effectively saves the reduction.
This is a subtle point: the objection may reveal that the reduction is a useful simplification but not metaphysically definitive.

The logical. They did not differ regarding condition, part, and whole, because the two indefinite [propositions] are true even if they agree in all unities. And as for the later [scholars], two unities suffice: unity of subject and unity of predicate, and the remaining unities are subsumed under them. Al-Dasūqī: His statement: 'Two unities: unity of subject and unity of predicate' — better would be not to restrict, for these eight unities may revert entirely to the predicate, and may revert to the subject. What he mentioned as subsumed under unity of subject reverts to unity of predicate, and what he mentioned as subsumed under unity of predicate reverts to unity of subject. For example: 'Zayd is standing by night, Zayd is not standing by night' — the unity of time here reverts to unity of predicate. Similarly: 'The one standing by night is Zayd, the one standing by night is not Zayd' — the unity of time here reverts to unity of subject. Likewise the unity of condition, and the unity of part or whole. As for their reverting to unity of subject, it is apparent. As for their reverting to unity of predicate, as if you said: 'The thing that differentiates vision is body, and the thing that differentiates vision is not body' — it is specified by the condition of the body being white in both, or black in both. And as if you said: 'The black [thing] is the black person, the black [thing] is not the black person' — it is specified that it is all in both, or some of it in both. This is the summary of what al-Sa‘d objected. And one of our teachers responded with a summary: that time, place, and what follows them, if they revert to the subject, become conditions, so they enter into the condition. So reflect. End of the writing of our teacher. Al-‘Aṭṭār: His statement: 'So two unities suffice' — he said in Sharh al-Maṭāli‘: Al-Fārābī sufficed with three unities: unity of subject, predicate, and time. Footnote (1): His statement: 'The two are true... etc.' such as 'Animal is human, animal is not human.' Footnote (2): His statement: 'And similar: the one standing... etc.' It is not hidden what is in it of strained interpretation, for by making time a qualification of the subject, he makes the two propositions indefinite after they were individual, and it has preceded that an indefinite does not contradict its like. Chapter on Contradiction. So the unity of condition, part, and whole is subsumed under unity of subject, and the unity of time, place, relation, potentiality, and actuality is subsumed under unity of predicate, and that is apparent to the one who reflects. Al-Dasūqī: His statement: 'So the unity of condition... etc., subsumed... etc.' means because the condition is in reality a description of the subject. The gist is that this difference is verbal, because in reality they revert to one meaning. His statement: 'subsumed under unity of subject' — for example: 'Color differentiates vision with the condition of it being white, color does not differentiate vision with the condition of it being white' reverts to our statement: 'White color differentiates vision, white color does not differentiate vision'; so the unity of condition here reverted to the subject. And similarly our statement: 'The black person is black, meaning all of him, the black person is not black, meaning all of him' reverts to our statement: 'Every black person is black, every black person is not black.' And similarly our statement: 'The black person is black, meaning some of him, the black person is not black, meaning some of him' reverts to our statement: 'Some black person is black, some black person is not black.' His statement: 'subsumed under unity of predicate' means our statement: 'Zayd is standing, meaning at night, Zayd is not standing, meaning at night' reverts to our statement: 'Zayd is standing at night, Zayd is not standing at night.' His statement: 'So the unity of condition... etc.' because the white body is not the black body, and the whole eye is not some of it. His statement: 'And the unity of time, place, relation, potentiality, and actuality is subsumed under unity of predicate' because the one who sleeps at night, for example, is not sleeping during the day, and the one standing in the market is not the one standing in... Footnote (1): His statement: 'Every black person... etc.' — the correct is: 'Some black person is not black' because the contradictory of the universal affirmative is a particular negative. Footnote (2): His statement: 'Some black person... etc.' — the correct is: 'No black person is black' because the contradictory of the particular affirmative is a universal negative. End. Al-Sharnūbī.

Commentary
This passage is primarily definitional, clarifying the conditions (unities) required for contradiction (tanāquḍ) between two propositions. The discussion revolves around the eight traditional unities (subject, predicate, time, place, condition, relation, potentiality, actuality, part/whole) and how they are reduced to just two by later logicians.

The text opens with a statement that early and later scholars agreed on three of the unities (condition, part, whole) but differed on others. The later logicians held that only two unities are essential: unity of subject (wihdat al-mawḍū‘) and unity of predicate (wihdat al-maḥmūl); the remaining six are subsumed under these two. This is the core claim.

Al-Dasūqī then objects, arguing that it is artificial to restrict the unities to two, because any of the eight can be seen as reverting to either subject or predicate depending on how the proposition is phrased. He gives examples: 'Zayd is standing by night' vs. 'Zayd is not standing by night' – here time modifies the predicate, so unity of time belongs to predicate. But if we say 'The one standing by night is Zayd' vs. 'The one standing by night is not Zayd', time modifies the subject, so unity of time belongs to subject. Similarly, condition and part/whole can be attached to either. He thus argues that the reduction is not absolute.

A response from one of his teachers is then given: time, place, and similar elements, when they revert to the subject, are conditions (shurūṭ) and thus fall under the unity of condition, which itself is subsumed under the subject. This suggests the difference is merely verbal.

Al-‘Aṭṭār adds a historical note that Al-Fārābī required three unities (subject, predicate, and time), indicating a middle position.

The text then presents a formal section heading 'Chapter on Contradiction' and states the definitive classification: unity of condition, part, and whole are subsumed under unity of subject; unity of time, place, relation, potentiality, and actuality are subsumed under unity of predicate. This is explained with examples: color with a condition reverts to 'white color', and 'every black person' etc. The commentary expands on each, showing how the eight are reducible to two in principle.

Overall, the passage teaches that the elaborate list of eight unities is not arbitrary; it can be simplified to the two fundamental elements of any proposition: subject and predicate. The intermediate details (condition, part/whole) are aspects of the subject; the rest (time, place, etc.) are aspects of the predicate. This reduction is accepted by later logicians and is seen as a refinement for practical application in determining contradiction in dialectical argumentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write a short jadal exchange: one person claims 'All actions are created by Allah', another objects 'Some actions are not created by Allah'. Using al-Dasūqī's logic, explain whether the objection is a valid contradiction, assuming the same time and place.
?
Evaluate the teacher's response: Does it genuinely solve al-Dasūqī's objection, or does it just rename the problem? Give one reason for each side.

Classification of Unity Conditions for Contradiction
Classification of eight unities under subject or predicate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify each of the eight unities required for contradictory propositions as belonging to either the subject or the predicate, and reduce examples with conditions to unified subject or predicate forms.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires eight unities; three pertain to the subject, five to the predicate.

Two propositions are contradictory only if they share a unified subject and predicate. This passage introduces the eight specific unities and shows how they reduce to subject or predicate unity. Through examples, you will see how conditions, parts, wholes, times, places, and other modifiers are actually part of the subject or predicate, not separate logical elements.

Guided Path
1
List the eight unities
From the passage, identify the eight conditions for contradiction: condition, part, whole, time, place, relation, potentiality, actuality.
2
Sort subject vs predicate
According to the matn, three (condition, part, whole) fall under unity of subject; five (time, place, relation, potentiality, actuality) fall under unity of predicate. Write them in two columns.
3
Analyze reduction examples
Work through al-Dasuqi's examples: 'color on condition of being white' becomes 'white color' – the condition attaches to the subject. Similarly, part/whole reduce using quantifiers. Time and place attach to the predicate, e.g., 'standing at night' becomes 'standing at night' as a unified predicate.
Notice the commentators' marginal corrections about logical conversions.
4
Apply the tool to new examples
Given a pair of propositions that differ only in a condition or time, rewrite them as unified subject or predicate propositions to test for contradiction.

فوحدةُ الشَّرطِ والجزء والكلِّ مندرجة في وحدة الموضوع، ووحدة الزَّمانِ، والمكان، والإضافة، والقوة، والفعل مندرجة في وحدة المحمول، وذلك ظاهرُ عند المتأمّل .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : فَوَحْدَةُ الشَّرْطِ . . . إِلَخ ، مُنْدَرِجَةٌ ... إلخ ) أي : لأنَّ الشَّرطَ في الحقيقة وصف للموضوع. وحاصله : أنَّ هذا الاختلاف لفظي ؛ لأنَّها في الحقيقة ترجع لمعنى واحد. (قَوْلُهُ : مُنْدَرِجَةٌ فِي وَحْدَةِ الْمَوْضُوع) مثلاً : اللُّونُ مُفرِّقُ للبصرِ بشرط كونه - أي : اللون - أبيض، اللون ليسَ مُفرّقاً للبصر بشرط كونه أبيض، يرجع إلى قولنا : اللُّونُ الأبيضُ مُفرّقٌ لِلبصر، اللَّونُ الأبيضُ ليسَ مُفرِّقاً للبصر ، فوحدةُ الشَّرطِ فيه رجعَتْ للموضوع.
وَكَذا يرجعُ قولنا : الزّنجي أسودُ؛ أي: كُلُّهُ، الزّنجي ليس بأسود؛ أي: كلُّه؛ إلى قولنا : كلُّ الزّنجي أسود، كلُّ الزّنجي (١) ليس بأسود، وَكَذا يرجعُ قولنا : الزّنجيُّ أسود؛ أي: بعضُهُ، الزّنجي ليس بأسود؛ أي: بعضُه، إلى قولنا : بعضُ الزنجي أسود، بعضُ الزنجي (٢) ليس بأسود. (قَوْلُهُ : مُنْدَرِجَةٌ فِي وَحْدَةِ الْمَحْمُوْلِ) أي : فيرجعُ قولنا : زيد قائم؛ أي : ليلاً زيد
العطار
ليس بقائم؛ أي: ليلاً، إلى قولنا : زيد قائم في اللَّيلِ، زيدٌ ليس بقائم في اللَّيلِ. (قَوْلُهُ : فَوَحْدَةُ الشَّرْطِ ... إِلَخ) لأنَّ الجسم الأبيض غيرُ الجسمِ الأسود، وكُلّ العين غير بعضها

الدسوقي
ويرجعُ قولنا : زيد جالس، وتريد في المسجد، زيد ليس بجالس، وتريد في المسجد، إلى قولنا : زيد جالس في المسجدِ، زيد ليس بجالس في المسجد.
العطار
المسجدِ، والأبَ لعمرو غيرُ الأبِ لِبَكرٍ ، والمسكر بالقوَّةِ غير المسكر بالفعل، فعدمُ التَّناقض في الصُّوَرِ المذكورةِ لِعَدَمِ الاتحادِ في الموضوع والمحمول.
Chapter on Contradiction: The unity of condition, part, and whole are subsumed under unity of subject; and the unity of time, place, relation, potentiality, and actuality are subsumed under unity of predicate. And that is apparent to the considerer. [Al-Dasuqi:] His statement: 'The unity of condition... etc. are subsumed... etc.' means: because the condition is in reality a description of the subject. And its summary is that this difference is verbal, because they in reality return to one meaning. His statement: 'subsumed under unity of subject' – for example: 'Color is sight-distinguishing on condition of being white' and 'Color is not sight-distinguishing on condition of being white' reduces to our saying: 'White color is sight-distinguishing' and 'White color is not sight-distinguishing'; thus the unity of condition therein reverted to the subject. Similarly, our saying: 'The black man is black – i.e., all of him' and 'The black man is not black – i.e., all of him' reduces to our saying: 'All of the black man is black' and 'All of the black man is not black'. And similarly, our saying: 'The black man is black – i.e., some of him' and 'The black man is not black – i.e., some of him' reduces to our saying: 'Some of the black man is black' and 'Some of the black man is not black'. His statement: 'subsumed under unity of predicate' means: so our saying: 'Zayd is standing – i.e., at night' and 'Zayd is not standing – i.e., at night' reduces to our saying: 'Zayd is standing at night' and 'Zayd is not standing at night'. [Al-Attar:] His statement: 'And the unity of time, place, relation, potentiality, and actuality are subsumed under unity of predicate' because the one who sleeps at night, for example, is not the one who sleeps during the day, and the one standing in the market is not the one standing in the house. (1) (His statement: 'All of the black man... etc.') The correct is 'Some of the black man is not black', since the contrary of the universal affirmative is a particular negative. (2) (His statement: 'Some of the black man... etc.') The correct is 'None of the black man is black', since the contrary of the particular affirmative is a universal negative. End. [Al-Sharnubi.] [Al-Dasuqi:] And our saying: 'Zayd is sitting – meaning in the mosque' and 'Zayd is not sitting – meaning in the mosque' reduces to our saying: 'Zayd is sitting in the mosque' and 'Zayd is not sitting in the mosque'. [Al-Attar:] The mosque, and 'the father of Amr' is other than 'the father of Bakr', and 'that which intoxicates potentially' is other than 'that which intoxicates actually'. So the lack of contradiction in the mentioned forms is due to the lack of unity in subject and predicate.
Source pages p. 649

Commentary
This passage defines the eight conditions for two propositions to be contradictory. The matn (main text) states that three unities (condition, part, whole) fall under unity of subject, and five unities (time, place, relation, potentiality, actuality) fall under unity of predicate. Al-Dasuqi explains that this classification is merely verbal; each reduces to either subject or predicate. He gives examples: condition reduces to subject (e.g., 'color on condition of being white' becomes 'white color'); part and whole reduce to subject via quantifiers (all/some). Time reduces to predicate (e.g., 'standing at night' becomes predicate with time). Al-Attar further illustrates with examples: time (night/day), place (market/house), relation (father of Amr vs. father of Bakr), potentiality vs. actuality (intoxicating potentially vs. actually). The marginal note by al-Sharnubi corrects the logical conversions: the contrary of a universal affirmative is a particular negative, not a universal negative. The passage concludes that contradiction fails in these examples because the subject or predicate lacks unity.

وحدة الموضوع
وحدة المحمول
شرط

Key Takeaway
The eight unities for contradiction are not arbitrary; they systematically reduce to either the subject or the predicate of the propositions involved.

Unity of Judgmental Relation in Contradiction
Author states that muhaqqiqun consider unity of judgmental relation essential for contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition of unity of judgmental relation and distinguish it from modality in assessing contradiction.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires unity of the relational predicate, not unity of modality.

The muhaqqiqun identified unity of the judgmental relation (nisba hukmiyya) as the essential condition for contradiction. This lesson clarifies how this condition works and addresses a common objection regarding modality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition
The muhaqqiqun state that for contradiction, the affirmative and negative must have the same judgmental relation between subject and predicate. This automatically implies other unities like subject, predicate, time, place.
2
Recognize the objection
An objector says requiring unity of relation conflicts with allowing difference in modality, because modality (e.g., necessity vs. possibility) changes the relation.
The objection is that if modality differs, the relations differ, so unity of relation cannot be maintained.
3
Grasp the response
The author clarifies that modality is the 'how' of occurrence, not part of the judgmental relation itself. Two propositions with different modalities can have the same judgmental relation. For example, 'The world is created necessarily' and 'The world is created possibly' share the same relation (created) but differ in modality; they are not contradictory because both could be false (if the world is eternal).
Thus, modality difference does not violate unity of relation; the objection is invalid.
4
Apply to an example
Consider 'Zaid is writing necessarily' and 'Zaid is not writing possibly'. They have the same subject and predicate relation ('writing') but different modalities. Check if they can both be false: if Zaid is not writing at all, both are false, so they are not contradictory. Unity of relation alone is necessary but not sufficient; truth values must also be opposite.
This shows why the condition is unity of judgmental relation, not modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي التَّنَاقُضِ. وعند المحققين أنَّ المعتبر في تحقق التناقض وحدةُ النِّسبةِ الحكميَّة، حتَّى يردَ الإيجابُ والسَّلبُ على شيءٍ واحدٍ فإنَّ وحدتها تستلزم الوحدات السابقة. قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنَّ وَحْدَتَهَا أي: لأنَّ وحدتها علَّةٌ لِقَولِهِ : (إِنَّ المعتبر ... إلخ). لا يقالُ : الرَّدُّ إلى وحدةِ النِّسبةِ يُنافي اشتراط الاختلافِ في الجهة مع أنَّهُ باختلافِ الجهةِ تختلفُ النِّسبتانِ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : الجهة : كيفية الوقوع واللوقوع، والنسبةُ الَّتي يشترط وحدتها النسبة الحكميَّة ولو لم تختلف في النّسبةِ الواحدة الوقوع واللا وقوع بالضَّرورة والإمكان مثلاً؛ لإمكان اجتماعِهِمَا على الكذب .
Chapter: On Contradiction. According to the verifiers (muhaqqiqun), what is considered in the realization of contradiction is the unity of the judgmental relation (nisba hukmiyya), so that affirmation and negation bear upon one thing. For its unity necessitates the previous unities. His statement: 'for its unity' means: because its unity is the cause of his statement: 'Indeed what is considered... etc.' It cannot be said: The return to unity of the relation contradicts the condition of difference in modality, since with difference in modality the two relations differ; because we say: modality is the how of occurrence and non-occurrence, and the relation whose unity is required is the judgmental relation, even if occurrence and non-occurrence do not differ in the same relation with necessity and possibility, for example, because it is possible for both to be false.
Source pages p. 651

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading on contradiction. It presents the view of the muhaqqiqun (the verifiers) that the fundamental condition for contradiction is the unity of the judgmental relation (nisba hukmiyya). This means that the affirmative and negative propositions must have the same relation between subject and predicate; only then can they be contradictory. This unity of relation automatically implies the previously mentioned unities (like unity of subject, predicate, time, place, etc.). The commentator explains that 'for its unity' indicates that the unity of the relation is the cause of requiring the other unities. An objection is raised: if we require unity of relation, how can we allow difference in modality (e.g., one is necessary, the other possible)? Because modality changes the relation. The response clarifies that modality is the 'how' of occurrence, not the relation itself. The judgmental relation is the core, and it can remain the same even if modality differs; for example, 'The world is created necessarily' and 'The world is created possibly' are not contradictory because both could be false (if the world is eternal). So the condition is unity of the relational predicate, not modality.

جهة
وقوع
لا وقوع

Key Takeaway
The essential condition for contradiction is unity of the judgmental relation, not unity of modality.

The Challenge of Modal Contradiction
Author distinguishes contradiction in non-modal vs modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rule for contradiction in non-modal propositions from the added complexity introduced by modality.

Core Idea
Modal propositions add a third dimension—modality—complicating the simple rules of contradiction.

In non-modal propositions, contradiction is straightforward: you just flip quality and quantity. But when modality is added, things become complex. The author points out that simply changing quality, quantity, and modality is not enough, because modalities are many and it's unclear which ones contradict each other. This lesson unpacks that challenge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of propositions
Find the passages that contrast non-modal (ghayr muwajjaha) with modal (muwajjaha) propositions. Note that the author treats them separately.
2
Recall contradiction in non-modal propositions
Remember that for non-modal categoricals, contradiction is determined by difference in quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular), as per the square of opposition.
Example: 'Every A is B' contradicts 'Some A is not B'.
3
Recognize the added dimension
Modal propositions include a modality (jiha) such as necessity, possibility, or contingency. The author states that varying quality, quantity, AND modality does not automatically yield contradiction.
4
Understand the problem
Because there are many modalities, it is not known which modality contradicts which. Simply picking a different modality does not guarantee a contradictory pair.
For example, does 'necessarily A is B' contradict 'possibly A is B'? Or 'necessarily A is not B'? The answer is not immediate.
5
Appreciate the implication
This means that modal logic requires additional rules or a modal square of opposition to determine contradiction, beyond the simple non-modal case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعْلَمْ أنَّ كيفيَّةَ التَّناقض في القضايا الغير الموجهة معلومة بمجرَّدِ الاختلافِ في الكيف والكمّ، وأما القضايا الموجهة؛ فلا يُعلَمُ حالها بمجرَّدِ الاختلافِ في الكيف والكمّ والجهة؛ إذ الجهات كثيرة، لا يُعرَفُ أنَّ هذهِ الجهة مثلاً مناقضة لأي جهةٍ،
And know that the manner of contradiction in non-modal propositions is known merely by difference in quality and quantity. As for modal propositions, their state is not known merely by difference in quality, quantity, and modality; for modalities are many, and it is not known, for example, that this modality contradicts which modality.
Source pages p. 653

Commentary
This passage addresses the problem of determining contradiction (tanāquḍ) in modal propositions (qaḍāyā muwajjaha). The author states that for simple, non-modal categorical propositions, contradiction is identified simply by checking the quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) according to the standard square of opposition. However, modal propositions—those that include necessity, possibility, contingency, etc.—are more complex. Simply varying the quality, quantity, and modality does not automatically reveal whether two modal propositions are contradictory. Because there are many possible modalities (e.g., necessary, permanent, possible), it is not obvious which modality contradicts which. The passage implies that additional rules or a modal square of opposition are needed to determine modal contradiction.

Key Terms
قضية موجهة
كيف
كم
جهة

Checkpoint

C) Because modal propositions have no quality or quantity
D) Because contradiction only applies to non-modal propositions

Key Takeaway
Contradiction in modal propositions cannot be determined by simply flipping quality, quantity, and modality; it requires understanding which modalities are genuine opposites.

Contradiction of Absolute Necessary Propositions
Defines the contradictory of absolute necessary propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the contradictory of any absolute necessary proposition.

Core Idea
The contradictory of 'necessarily A is B' is 'possibly some A is not B'.

The author explains that the contradictory of an absolute necessary proposition is not a simple negation but a denial of the necessity itself. This is crucial for modal logic in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality and quantity of the original proposition
Look for the modal term 'necessarily' (ḍarūra) and the quantifier (e.g., 'every' or 'no').
Example: 'Every khamr is necessarily haram' is universal affirmative necessary.
2
Change the quantity and quality for contradiction
The contradictory must differ in both quantity (universal ↔ particular) and quality (affirmative ↔ negative).
3
Replace the modality with 'general possible'
For absolute necessary, the contradictory modality is the general possible (mumkina ‘āmma), which negates the necessity.
The resulting proposition asserts that it is not necessary that the predicate holds for some subject.
4
Verify the example
Check that the contradictory satisfies the definition: one must be true and the other false.
Example: 'Every khamr is necessarily haram' is contradicted by 'Some khamr is not haram possibly' (i.e., it is not necessary that all khamr is haram).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي التَّنَاقُضِ
هو المُمْكِنَةُ العَامَّةُ؛ لأنَّ إثباتَ الضَّرورةِ في جانب الإيجاب — وهو مفهوم الضَّروريَّةِ الموجبة — مناقض لسلبِ الضَّرورة عن جانب الإيجاب؛ وهو مفهوم السَّالبة الممكنة.
وكذا إثباتُ الضَّرورةِ في جانبِ السَّلبِ — وهو مفهوم الضرورية السالبة — مناقض لسلبِ الضَّرورةِ عن جانبِ السَّلبِ؛ وهو مفهوم الموجبة الممكنة.
مثلاً : كُلُّ إنسانٍ حيوان بالضَّرورةِ؛ يُناقضه بعضُ الإنسانِ ليس بحيوان بالإمكان العام، فإِنَّ مَعناه سلبُ الضَّرورةِ عَنِ الجانبِ المخالفِ والجانب المخالف هنا هو الإيجاب، فيكونُ حاصلُ المعنَى أَنَّهُ لَا ضرورة في ثبوتِ الحيوانية لبعض الإنسان، وهو يُناقض قولنا : كُلُّ إنسان حيوان بالضَّرورةِ.
والنَّقيضُ لِلدَّائِمَةِ هو المُطْلَقَةُ العَامَّةُ. والدَّائمة المطلقةُ: ما حكم فيها بدوامِ النِّسبةِ ما دامت ذاتُ الموضوع موجودةً، والمطلقة العامَّةُ: ما حكم فيها بفعليَّةِ النِّسبةِ، ومثال ذلكَ في الموجبة: كُلُّ إنسان حيوان دائماً، فَنقيضُهَا: بعضُ الإنسان ليس بحيوان بالإطلاق العام، وفى السَّالبة: لا شيء مِنَ الإنسان دائماً، فنقيضُها: بعضُ الإنسانِ حجرٌ بالإطلاقِ العام.
Chapter: On Contradiction. It (the contradictory of the absolute necessary) is the general possible; because the affirmation of necessity in the affirmative side — which is the concept of affirmative necessary — contradicts the negation of necessity from the affirmative side; which is the concept of negative possible. Likewise, the affirmation of necessity in the negative side — which is the concept of negative necessary — contradicts the negation of necessity from the negative side; which is the concept of affirmative possible. For example: 'Every human is necessarily an animal' is contradicted by 'Some human is not an animal by general possibility' — for its meaning is the negation of necessity from the opposite side, and the opposite side here is affirmation, so the resultant meaning is that there is no necessity in the establishment of animality for some human, and it contradicts our statement 'Every human is necessarily an animal.' And the contradictory of the perpetual is the general absolute. The perpetual absolute is that in which the judgment is made of the permanence of the relation as long as the subject's essence exists, and the general absolute is that in which the judgment is made of the actuality of the relation. An example of that in the affirmative: 'Every human is perpetually an animal' — its contradictory is 'Some human is not an animal by general absolute'; and in the negative: 'No human is perpetually a stone' — its contradictory is 'Some human is a stone by general absolute.'
Source pages p. 655

Commentary
This passage defines the contradictory (tanāquḍ) for two kinds of modal propositions: the absolute necessary (ḍarūriyya muṭlaqa) and the perpetual (dā’ima). The author states that the contradictory of an absolute necessary proposition is the general possible (mumkina ‘āmma). The reasoning is given: a necessary affirmative proposition asserts necessity of the predicate for the subject. Its contradictory does not simply deny the predicate; rather, it denies the necessity of that predicate for some individuals. So the contradictory is a particular negative proposition with the modality of possibility (i.e., it is possible that some individual lacks the predicate). Similarly, for a necessary negative proposition, the contradictory is a particular affirmative possible. The author illustrates with the example: 'Every human is necessarily an animal' is contradicted by 'Some human is not an animal possibly' (i.e., it is not necessary that every human is an animal). Then the passage moves to perpetual (dā’ima) propositions. A perpetual proposition asserts that the predicate holds for the subject at all times the subject exists. Its contradictory is the general absolute (muṭlaqa ‘āmma), which asserts that the predicate holds actually (at some time) but not perpetually. Two examples are given: for the affirmative perpetual 'Every human is always an animal', the contradictory is 'Some human is not an animal at some time' (i.e., absolutely). For the negative perpetual 'No human is always a stone', the contradictory is 'Some human is a stone at some time'. This treatment is standard in Islamic logic for modal opposition, ensuring that the contradictory of a quantified modal proposition must differ in quantity, quality, and modality appropriately.

Key Terms
تناقض
الضرورية المطلقة
الممكنة العامة

C) No khamr is necessarily haram.

Contradiction of Perpetual Propositions
Defines the contradictory of perpetual propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the contradictory of any perpetual proposition.

Core Idea
The contradictory of 'always A is B' is 'at some time A is not B'.

Perpetual propositions assert that a predicate holds for the subject at all times the subject exists. Their contradictory asserts that the predicate actually does not hold at some time. This distinction is important for temporal arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality and quantity of the perpetual proposition
Look for the temporal adverb 'dā’iman' (perpetually) and the quantifier.
Example: 'Every human is perpetually an animal' is universal affirmative perpetual.
2
Change the quantity and quality for contradiction
The contradictory must differ in quantity (universal ↔ particular) and quality (affirmative ↔ negative).
3
Replace the modality with 'general absolute'
For perpetual, the contradictory modality is the general absolute (muṭlaqa ‘āmma), which asserts the actuality of the relation at some time, not its permanence.
The resulting proposition asserts that the predicate does not hold for some subject at some time.
4
Verify the example
Check that the contradictory satisfies the definition: one must be true and the other false.
Example: 'Every human is perpetually an animal' is contradicted by 'Some human is not an animal at some time' (i.e., absolutely).

هو المُمْكِنَةُ العَامَّةُ؛ لأنَّ إثباتَ الضَّرورةِ في جانب الإيجاب — وهو مفهوم الضَّروريَّةِ الموجبة — مناقض لسلبِ الضَّرورة عن جانب الإيجاب؛ وهو مفهوم السَّالبة الممكنة.
وكذا إثباتُ الضَّرورةِ في جانبِ السَّلبِ — وهو مفهوم الضرورية السالبة — مناقض لسلبِ الضَّرورةِ عن جانبِ السَّلبِ؛ وهو مفهوم الموجبة الممكنة.
مثلاً : كُلُّ إنسانٍ حيوان بالضَّرورةِ؛ يُناقضه بعضُ الإنسانِ ليس بحيوان بالإمكان العام، فإِنَّ مَعناه سلبُ الضَّرورةِ عَنِ الجانبِ المخالفِ والجانب المخالف هنا هو الإيجاب، فيكونُ حاصلُ المعنَى أَنَّهُ لَا ضرورة في ثبوتِ الحيوانية لبعض الإنسان، وهو يُناقض قولنا : كُلُّ إنسان حيوان بالضَّرورةِ.

Chapter: On Contradiction. It (the contradictory of the absolute necessary) is the general possible; because the affirmation of necessity in the affirmative side — which is the concept of affirmative necessary — contradicts the negation of necessity from the affirmative side; which is the concept of negative possible. Likewise, the affirmation of necessity in the negative side — which is the concept of negative necessary — contradicts the negation of necessity from the negative side; which is the concept of affirmative possible. For example: 'Every human is necessarily an animal' is contradicted by 'Some human is not an animal by general possibility' — for its meaning is the negation of necessity from the opposite side, and the opposite side here is affirmation, so the resultant meaning is that there is no necessity in the establishment of animality for some human, and it contradicts our statement 'Every human is necessarily an animal.' And the contradictory of the perpetual is the general absolute. The perpetual absolute is that in which the judgment is made of the permanence of the relation as long as the subject's essence exists, and the general absolute is that in which the judgment is made of the actuality of the relation. An example of that in the affirmative: 'Every human is perpetually an animal' — its contradictory is 'Some human is not an animal by general absolute'; and in the negative: 'No human is perpetually a stone' — its contradictory is 'Some human is a stone by general absolute.'

The Possible Temporal as Contradictory of the Universal Conditional
Definition of the possible temporal proposition as the contradictory of the universal conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the possible temporal proposition as the contradictory of a universal conditional in both affirmative and negative forms.

Core Idea
The contradictory of necessity is not impossibility, but possibility.

In logic, every universal conditional proposition has a specific contradictory. The author introduces the 'possible temporal proposition' as that contradictory. Understanding this relationship is crucial for correctly negating conditional statements. This lesson walks through the definitions and examples.

Guided Path
1
Recall the universal conditional
A universal conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma) asserts necessity as long as a description holds, e.g., 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer.'
2
Understand the contradictory structure
The contradictory (naqīḍ) must differ in truth value. For a universal conditional, the contradictory is a particular possible temporal: it asserts the possibility of the opposite during the time of the description.
It is a simple proposition not among the basic simples, needed for contradicting some simple propositions.
3
Apply to affirmative examples
For 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer,' the contradictory is 'Some writer is not moving his fingers with universal possibility while he is a writer.'
The negation is particular and uses 'universal possibility' (al-imkān al-ʿāmm).
4
Apply to negative examples
For 'No writer is still of fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer,' the contradictory is 'Some writer is still of fingers with universal possibility while he is a writer.'
The same pattern applies: negate the necessity and change quantity to particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي التَّنَاقُضِ وَالنَّقيضُ لِلْمَشْرُوْطَةِ العَامَّةِ هُوَ الْحِينِيَّةُ الْمُمْكِنَةُ الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيْهَا بِسَلْبِ الضَّرُورَةِ بِحَسَبِ الْوَصْفِ عَنِ الْجَانِبِ الْمُخَالِفِ لِلْحُكْمِ وَهِيَ قَضِيَّةٌ بَسِيطَةٌ لَمْ تُذْكَرْ فِي الْبَسَائِطِ وَاحْتِيجَ إِلَيْهَا فِي نَقِيضِ بَعْضِ الْبَسَائِطِ وَنِسْبَتُهَإِلَى الْمَشْرُوْطَةِ الْعَامَّةِ كَنِسْبَةِ الْمُمْكِنَةِ الْعَامَّةِ إِلَى الضَّرُورِيَّةِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ فِي الْمُوجِبَةِ كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الْأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً فَنَقِيضُهَا بَعْضُ الْكَاتِبِ لَيْسَ بِمُتَحَرِّكِ الْأَصَابِعِ بِالْإِمْكَانِ الْعَامِّ حِينَ هُوَ كَاتِبٌ وَفِي السَّالِبَةِ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْكَاتِبِ بِسَاكِنِ الْأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً فَنَقِيضُهَا بَعْضُ الْكَاتِبِ سَاكِنُ الْأَصَابِعِ بِالْإِمْكَانِ الْعَامِّ حِينَ هُوَ كَاتِبٌ
Section on Contradiction. The contradictory of the universal conditional is the possible temporal proposition, which is that in which necessity is negated with respect to the description from the side opposing the judgment. It is a simple proposition not mentioned among the simple propositions, and it is needed for the contradictory of some simple propositions. Its relation to the universal conditional is like the relation of the universal possibility to the essential necessary. An example in the affirmative: 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer' — its contradictory is 'Some writer is not moving his fingers with universal possibility while he is a writer.' Commentary
This passage defines the contradictory (naqīḍ) of the universal conditional proposition (al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma) and introduces the possible temporal proposition (al-ḥīniyya al-mumkina). The author first states that the contradictory of the universal conditional is the possible temporal. The commentator then expands by defining the possible temporal: a proposition in which necessity is negated with respect to the description (the condition) from the side opposite the original judgment. That is, while the universal conditional asserts necessity as long as the subject's description holds, its contradictory asserts possibility of the opposite. For example, if the universal conditional says 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer,' the contradictory is 'Some writer is possibly not moving his fingers while he is a writer.' The Temporal Possible Proposition: Definition and Contradictory
Author defines the temporal possible proposition and its relation to the general conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the temporal possible proposition and construct its contradictory from a given general conditional proposition.

Core Idea
The temporal possible negates descriptive necessity; it is the contradictory of the general conditional.

The author introduces a type of proposition not covered in the basic list: the temporal possible. It is essential for forming the contradictory of certain conditional propositions. Understanding its structure and relation to descriptive necessity allows us to handle complex propositional logic in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Look at the structure: it negates necessity with respect to a description, from the side opposite the judgment. Recognize that it asserts possibility at some time when the description holds.
This is a simple proposition, though omitted from the standard list of simple propositions.
2
Contrast with general conditional
The general conditional (المشروطة العامة) asserts necessity as long as the description holds. The temporal possible is its contradictory. Compare the logical forms: the general conditional has universal affirmative necessity; the temporal possible is particular negative possibility.
3
Apply the contradictory relation
Given a general conditional, derive its temporal possible contradictory by replacing necessity with possibility, changing the quantifier from 'every' to 'some', and negating the predicate. The description remains the same but is expressed as 'when the description holds'.
The contradictory of 'Every S is P necessarily as long as S is described' is 'Some S is not P possibly when S is described'.
4
Test with examples
Verify the pattern using the examples from the passage: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily when he is a writer' has its contradictory 'Some writer does not move his fingers possibly when he is a writer'.
The author uses a different wording: 'Some writer is still-fingered with possibility when he is a writer'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التي حكم فيها بسلبِ الضَّرورة، بحسب الوصف عن الجانب المخالف للحكم، وهي قضيَّةٌ بسيطة لم تُذكر في البسائط، واحتيج إليها في نقيض بعض البسائط، ونسبتها إلى المشروطة العامة كنسبة الممكنة العامة إلى الضَّروريَّةِ الذَّاتية . ومثال ذلك في الموجبة : كلُّ كاتب مُتحرك الأصابع بالضرورة بالإمكان العام حين هو كاتب، وفي السَّالبة : لا شيء من الكاتب يساكن الأصابع بالضرورة ما دامَ كاتباً، فَنقيضُها بعضُ الكاتب ساكن الأصابع بالإمكان العام حين هو كاتب. فكما أنَّ الضَّرورةَ الذَّاتِيَّة تنافي الإمكانَ الذَّاتي، كذلكَ الضَّرورةَ الوصفية تنافي الإمكان الوصفي. ومن ههُنا يُعلَمُ ... إلخ
[The proposition] in which the judgment is made of negating necessity, according to the description, from the side contrary to the judgment. It is a simple proposition not mentioned among the simple propositions, yet needed for the contradictory of some simple ones. Its relation to the general conditional is like the relation of the common possible to the essential necessary. An example of this in the affirmative: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily, with common possibility, when he is a writer.' In the negative: 'No writer is still-fingered necessarily, as long as he is a writer.' So its contradictory is 'Some writer is still-fingered with common possibility when he is a writer.' For just as essential necessity contradicts essential possibility, so too descriptive necessity contradicts descriptive possibility. From this one knows... etc.

Commentary
This passage defines a type of proposition called the 'temporal possible' (الممكنة الحينية). The author explains that it is a proposition in which necessity is negated with respect to the description, from the side opposite to the judgment. In other words, it asserts that the predicate can be absent from the subject at some time when the description applies. This proposition is simple but was not listed among the simple propositions in the standard enumeration, yet it is needed for the contradictory of certain simple propositions, specifically the general conditional (المشروطة العامة). The author compares its relation to the general conditional with the relation of the common possible (الممكنة العامة) to the essential necessary (الضرورية الذاتية). That is, just as the common possible is the contradictory of the essential necessary, so the temporal possible is the contradictory of the general conditional. An example is given: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily when he is a writer'—its contradictory is 'Some writer is still-fingered with possibility when he is a writer.' The underlying principle is that essential necessity and essential possibility are contradictory, and likewise descriptive necessity and descriptive possibility are contradictory. From this, one can infer the contradictory of absolute temporal propositions and absolute widespread propositions.
