Contradictories of Temporal, Extended, and Conventional Propositions
Defines contradictories of three modal proposition types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct contradictory for any absolute temporal, absolute extended, or general conventional proposition based on temporal scope and modality.

Core Idea
Contradictories oppose in modality and temporal scope: necessity vs. possibility with matching time restrictions.

In Islamic logic, modal propositions carry restrictions of time and description. Understanding their contradictories is essential for valid reasoning in fiqh and kalam. The author defines three key types and their correct contradictories, with examples from everyday life and human actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Read the proposition carefully. Determine if it uses a specific time (waqt mu'ayyan), some time (waqt ma), or a description (wasf). These correspond to absolute temporal, absolute extended, and general conventional respectively.
Look for keywords like 'time of writing' (specific), 'at some time' (extended), or 'as long as' (conventional).
2
Determine the modality and quantity
Note whether it is universal affirmative (كل) or universal negative (لا شيء). The contradictory must change quantity and modality: from affirmative to negative or vice versa, and from necessity to possibility or vice versa.
Affirmative contradictories become particular negative; negative become particular affirmative.
3
Apply the temporal scope rule
The temporal restriction of the contradictory must exactly oppose the original. For a specific time, negate necessity at that same time. For some time, negate necessity in all times. For a description, assert actuality of the opposite in some instance of that description.
Use the definitions given: absolute temporal → temporal possible (same time); absolute extended → permanent possible (all times); general conventional → absolute momentary (some times of the description).
4
Verify with an example
Construct an example from the passage, such as 'Every writer moves his fingers at the time of writing by necessity.' Its contradictory is 'Some writer is not moving his fingers at the time of writing by general possibility.' Check that the time and modality match the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنَّ نقيض الوقتيَّةِ المطلقة هو الممكنة الوقتيَّة؛ لأنَّ الضَّرورة بحسب الوقت المعيَّن، تناقض سلبها بحسب ذلك الوقت. الوقتية المطلقة : هي التي حكم فيها بضرورةِ النّسبةِ في وقت معيَّن والممكنة الوقتيَّةُ : هي التي حكم فيها بسلب الضَّرورةِ عن الجانب المخالفِ للحكم في وقت معين. ومثال ذلك في الموجبة : كلُّ كاتب متحرّك الأصابع وقت الكتابة بالضرورة، فَنقيضُها : بعضُ الكاتب ليس بمتحرِّكِ الأصابع وقت الكتابة بالإمكان العام، وفي السَّالبةِ : لا شيء مِنَ الكاتب بساكن الأصابع وقت الكتابة بالضرورة، فنقيضُها : بعضُ الكاتب ساكن الأصابع وقت الكتابة بالإمكان العام.

وكذا نقيضُ المنتشرة المطلقة، هو الممكنة الدائمةُ ؛ لأنَّ الضَّرورة في وقت ما ; تنافي سلبها في جميع الأوقات. المنتشرة المطلقة : هي التي حكم فيها بضرورةِ النّسبة في وقتٍ مَا والممكنةُ الدَّائمةُ : ما حكم فيها بسلب الضرورة عن الجانب المخالفِ في جميعِ الأوقات. ومثال ذلك في الموجبة : كلُّ إنسانٍ مُتنفّسُ بالضَّرورة وقتاً ما، فنقيضُهَا : بعضُ الإنسان ليسَ بِمتنفّس بالإمكان العام دائماً، وفي السَّالبةِ : لَا شيءَ مِنَ الإِنسانِ يمتنفّس وقتاً ما بالضَّرورةِ، فَنقيضُها : بعضُ الإنسانِ متنفّس بالإمكان العام دائماً.

وَ) النَّقيضُ لِلْعُرْفِيَّةِ العَامَّةِ) هو (الجِيْنِيَّةُ المُطْلَقَةُ) الَّتي حُكِمَ فيها بفعليَّة النّسبةِ في بعض أوقاتِ وصفِ الموضوع.
That the contradictory of the absolute temporal proposition is the temporal possible proposition; because necessity according to a specific time contradicts its negation according to that time. The absolute temporal proposition is that in which is judged the necessity of the relation in a specific time, and the temporal possible proposition is that in which is judged the negation of necessity from the side opposite to the judgment in a specific time. An example of that in the affirmative: 'Every writer is moving his fingers at the time of writing by necessity,' so its contradictory is 'Some writer is not moving his fingers at the time of writing by general possibility.' And in the negative: 'No writer is still-fingered at the time of writing by necessity,' so its contradictory is 'Some writer is still-fingered at the time of writing by general possibility.'

And likewise the contradictory of the absolute extended proposition is the permanent possible proposition; because necessity in some time opposes its negation in all times. The absolute extended proposition is that in which is judged the necessity of the relation in some time, and the permanent possible proposition is that in which is judged the negation of necessity from the side opposite to the judgment in all times. An example of that in the affirmative: 'Every human is breathing by necessity at some time,' so its contradictory is 'Some human is not breathing by general possibility always.' And in the negative: 'No human is breathing at some time by necessity,' so its contradictory is 'Some human is breathing by general possibility always.'

And the contradictory for the general conventional proposition is the absolute momentary proposition in which is judged the actuality of the relation in some times of the description of the subject.
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This passage defines the contradictories of three types of modal propositions: the absolute temporal (al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa), the absolute extended (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa), and the general conventional (al-'urfiyya al-'amma). For each, the author first states the contradictory type, then provides a logical justification based on the temporal scope of necessity, and finally gives examples in both affirmative and negative forms.

1. For the absolute temporal proposition: it asserts necessity of the relation in a specific time, e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers at the time of writing by necessity.' Its contradictory is the temporal possible proposition, which negates that necessity for that specific time. Thus, the contradictory is 'Some writer is not moving his fingers at the time of writing by general possibility.' The justification is that necessity restricted to a particular time contradicts its negation at that very time.

2. For the absolute extended proposition: it asserts necessity in some unspecified time, e.g., 'Every human breathes by necessity at some time.' Its contradictory is the permanent possible proposition, which negates necessity in all times. So the contradictory is 'Some human is not breathing by general possibility always.' The reasoning: necessity occurring at some time is incompatible with its absence at all times.

3. For the general conventional proposition: it asserts the necessity of the relation as long as the subject's description exists (e.g., 'Every breathing animal moves at the time of breathing'). Its contradictory is the absolute momentary (hiniyya mutlaqa), which asserts actuality of the opposite relation in some time when the description applies. This is given without a separate justification.

All examples use Islamic-relevant scenarios: writer's finger movement during writing, human breathing. The passage reinforces the principle that contradictories must oppose in quantity (universal affirmative vs. particular negative, and vice versa) and in modality (necessity vs. possibility) with matching temporal scope.

الممكنة الوقتية
المنتشرة المطلقة
الممكنة الدائمة
العرفية العامة
الجينية المطلقة

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a modal proposition must oppose it in quantity, quality, and modality, while preserving the exact temporal or descriptive restriction that defines its type.

Propositions

10 min
The Contradictory of a Compound is a Disjunction Excluding Emptiness
Author defines the contradictory of a compound as a disjunction excluding emptiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contradictory of a compound proposition (conjunction) as a disjunction of the contradictories of its parts that excludes emptiness.

Core Idea
The contradictory of 'A and B' is 'not-A or not-B', ensuring at least one is true.

In classical logic, negating a compound proposition like 'A and B' requires more than a simple 'not'. This lesson from Tadhhib al-Mantiq teaches that the proper contradictory is a disjunction of the contradictories of each part, such that it cannot be false when both parts are false. This ensures it captures exactly the conditions under which the original is false.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the compound
A compound proposition (murakkaba) is composed of two simple propositions, say A and B, connected by 'and' (implicitly a conjunction). The compound is true only when both A and B are true.
2
Take the contradictory of each part
For each part A and B, form its contradictory: not-A and not-B. These are the opposites that are true when the part is false.
3
Form a disjunction from the two contradictories
Combine not-A and not-B with 'or' to create the disjunction 'not-A or not-B'. This is the candidate for the contradictory of the original compound.
4
Ensure the disjunction excludes emptiness
The disjunction must be of the type 'māni‘at al-khulū' (excludes emptiness), meaning it cannot have both disjuncts false. This ensures that whenever the compound is false, at least one part's contradictory is true, and vice versa.
This is not a simple 'or' that allows both false; it is a specific logical relation.
5
Verify the truth conditions match
Check: when the compound is true (A true, B true), the disjunction 'not-A or not-B' is false (both contradictories false). When the compound is false (at least one of A or B false), the disjunction is true (the corresponding contradictory is true). Thus they are perfect opposites.
This four-case verification is detailed in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي التَّنَاقُضِ
بالحقيقة: منفصلةٌ مانعة الخلوّ، مركَّبَةٌ من نقيضي الجزأين، فيكون طريق أخذِ نقيض المركَّبةِ، أن تُحَلَّلَ المركَّبةُ إلى الجزأين، ويؤخذ لكلِّ جزء نقيضُه، ويركَّبَ من نقيضي الجزأين منفصلة مانعة الخلو
الدسوقي
.
وهو : أنَّ المركَّبَةَ تُناقضُها مُنفصلةٌ مانعة خلوّ ؛ إذ النَّقيضُ على هذا الوجه ليس بشرطية أصلاً، وإِنَّما كانَ النَّقيضُ هو المفهوم المردَّدَ الَّذِي هو مُنفصلة مانعة خلو
في الحقيقة؛ لأنَّ المركَّبَ يكذب بكذب أحدِ جُزأَيْهِ .
(قَوْلُهُ : بِالْحَقِيقَةِ) أي: في الحقيقةِ .
(قَوْلُهُ : الْجُزْأَيْن) أي: اللذين تركَّبَتْ منهما المركبة.
(قَوْلُهُ : فَيَكُونُ . . . إِلَخ) تفريحٌ على قوله : (مُركَّبة من ... إلخ).

(قَوْلُهُ: مَانِعَةُ الْخُلُو) أي: لا مانعةَ الجمع، فإنَّه يمكن أن تصدق المنفصلة

بالمنفصلة؛ إنَّما أورد على سبيل البحثِ معهُم كَمَا سَننقلُه .
(قَوْلُهُ : مُنْفَصِلَةٌ مَانِعَةُ الْخُلُوِّ إِنَّما اعتبر ذلك ليكونَ مُكذِّباً للمركَّبة على كُلِّ احتمال، فإنَّ المركَّبة لا تكون صادقةٌ إلَّا بصدق جزأيْهَا ، والمفهوم المردَّدُ إنْ كانَ صادق الجزأين أوِ الأَوَّلِ فقط أوِ الثَّاني فقط ؛ يُكذِّبُ جُزأي: المركبةِ قطعاً بكذب مُجزأيْهَا معاً أو الأوَّلِ فقط أوِ الثَّاني فقط، فإِنَّهُ بخلافِ مَا لَو اعتُبِرَ الانفصالُ الحقيقي، فإنَّهُ لا يُشيرُ حينئذٍ إلى تكذيبها بكذب جزئيْهَا معاً، أو منع الجمع فقط فإِنَّهُ لا يشير إلى تكذيبها بكذبِ جُزأيْهَا ،معاً ، وجَعْلُ النَّقيض مُنفصلة مانعة خُلُوِّ هوَ ما في شرح المصنفِ لِلرِّسالة وغيره
Chapter on Contradiction. In reality, it is a disjunction that excludes emptiness (i.e., at least one true), composed of the two contradictories of the parts. So the method of taking the contradictory of a compound is to analyze the compound into its two parts, take the contradictory of each part, and form from the two contradictories a disjunction that excludes emptiness. [Dassūqī:] It means that a compound is contradicted by a disjunction that excludes emptiness; for the contradictory in this respect is not a conditional at all. Rather, the contradictory is the disjunctive concept (al-mafhūm al-muraddad), which is in reality a disjunction excluding emptiness, because the compound is false by the falsity of any one of its parts. (His statement 'in reality' means 'in actual fact.' His statement 'the two parts' means the two from which the compound is composed. His statement 'so the method...etc.' is a branch from his statement 'composed of...etc.' His statement 'analyze' means 'disassemble.' His statement 'excludes emptiness' means 'not excluding conjunction,' because the disjunction can be true by one of its parts. [al-‘Aṭṭār:] With their explicit statement that in compounds it is a disjunction similar to a categorical proposition, but making it a categorical proposition similar to a disjunction was only adduced by way of investigation with them, as we will transmit. His statement 'a disjunction that excludes emptiness' was considered such to make it falsifying the compound on every possibility; for the compound is only true by the truth of both its parts, and the disjunctive concept, if it is true in both parts or in the first only or in the second only, falsifies the two parts of the compound absolutely by the falsity of both its parts together or the first only or the second only. This is contrary to what if genuine disjunction were considered, for then it would not indicate its falsity by the falsity of both its parts together, or preventing conjunction only, as it would not indicate its falsity by the falsity of both its parts together. And making the contradictory a disjunction excluding emptiness is what is in the author's commentary on the Risāla and other works.
Source pages p. 667

Commentary
This passage defines the contradictory (naqīḍ) of a compound proposition (murakkaba) and explains the method for deriving it. The author begins by stating that the true contradictory of a compound is a disjunction (munfaṣila) that excludes emptiness (māni‘at al-khulū), meaning a disjunction that cannot have both parts false—i.e., at least one part is true. This disjunction is composed of the contradictories of the two parts of the original compound. The method is: break the compound into its two parts, take the contradictory of each part, and then recombine those two contradictories into a disjunction of the type that excludes emptiness. Dassūqī clarifies that the contradictory is not a conditional proposition but rather a disjunctive concept that is fundamentally a disjunction excluding emptiness. The reason is that the compound (which is false if any one part is false, as indicated by 'because the compound is false by the falsity of any one of its parts') requires a contradictory that captures all possibilities of its falsehood. The subsequent glosses explain each phrase: 'in reality' means in actual fact, 'the two parts' refers to the components of the compound, 'analyze' means to disassemble, and 'excludes emptiness' means it is not of the type that excludes conjunction (māni‘at al-jam‘), because a disjunction can be true by just one part. Al-‘Aṭṭār notes that the scholars explicitly state that for compounds, the contradictory is a disjunction resembling a categorical (ḥamliyya) proposition, but the idea of making it a categorical resembling a disjunction is mentioned only for discussion. The core justification is that this specific disjunction ensures that the contradictory falsifies the compound in every scenario: if the compound is true (both parts true), the disjunction is false because both its disjuncts (the contradictories) are false; if the compound is false because both parts are false, the disjunction is true because both disjuncts are true; if the compound is false because only the first part is false, the disjunction is true because the first disjunct is true; and similarly if only the second part is false. This coverage is not achieved by other types of disjunction (e.g., genuine disjunction or exclusivity). The passage concludes by noting that this rule is what is found in the author's commentary on the Risāla and other works.

Key Terms
التَّنَاقُض
منفصلة مانعة الخلو
المركَّبة
نقيض
المفهوم المردَّد

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a conjunction is the disjunction of the contradictories, of the type that excludes emptiness (at least one disjunct true).

The Contradictory of an Exclusive Disjunction
Author explains that the contradictory of a compound disjunction is a disjunction of the contradictories of its parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of a compound exclusive disjunction (man'at al-khuluw) and express it as a disjunction of the contradictories of its parts.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a compound disjunction is itself a disjunction of the contradictories of its parts.

In this passage, the author explains how to find the contradictory (naqid) of a compound proposition like an exclusive disjunction. This is crucial for constructing valid arguments in Islamic logic, especially when dealing with conditional propositions in kalam and usul al-fiqh. We will walk through the steps to correctly form the contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound disjunction
Locate the exclusive disjunction in the passage. The author discusses compound disjunctions like the special conditioned proposition (al-mashrutah al-khassah). Recognize that it has two parts, each a simple proposition.
The exclusive disjunction (man'at al-khuluw) is a compound proposition where exactly one part is true.
2
Recognize the condition for the contradictory
Understand that when both parts are true or both false, the contradictory can be the conjunction of the contradictories; when one is true and the other false, the contradictory is just one of the contradictories.
The author says: 'when each of the two propositions is true or false, the contradictory may be both; but if one is true and the other false, the contradictory is one of them.'
3
Construct the contradictory as a disjunction
The contradictory of an exclusive disjunction is a disjunction of the contradictories of its parts. For example, if the original is 'A or B (exclusive)', the contradictory is 'Not A or Not B (inclusive)'.
This holds because the negation of an exclusive OR is a logical inclusive OR of the negations.
4
Apply the rule to a concrete Islamic example
The author gives the example: 'Every writer is a human being actually not permanently.' Its contradictory is 'Either some human being is not permanently, or some human being is a writer permanently.' This illustrates the rule.
The original is a universal affirmative non-permanent proposition; its contradictory is a disjunction of two particular propositions.
5
Note the author's caution about the form
The author cautions that the undetermined concept (al-mafhum al-muraddad) between the two contradictories should not be restricted to a disjunction; it could also be expressed as a categorical or divided predicate. So the form is flexible.
The phrase 'specifying the undetermined concept as a disjunction is not good' indicates that other proposition types may be valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَيُقَالُ : إِمَّا هَذَا النَّقِيْضُ، وَإِمَّا ذَاكَ. يعني : أنَّ النَّقيض باطرادِ أحدهما، وهذا لا يُنافي أنَّ النَّقيض قد يكون كلا منهما، وذلك فيما كان كلٌّ مِنَ القضيَّتين اللتين تَضمَّنَتْهُما المركَّبةُ المنفصلة صادقاً أو كاذباً ، أما إذا كان أحدهما صادقاً والآخر كاذباً ; فالنَّقيضُ أحدهما . وقولُه : (إمَّا هذا النَّقيضُ وإمَّا ذاكَ) كَقولِكَ في نقيض المشروطة الخاصَّةِ المركَّبَةَ إِنْ كانَت كُلِّيَّةٌ، فَجزاها بسيطتانِ كُلِّيَّتَانِ، ونقيضَاهُمَا بسيطتان جزئيتانِ؛ فَنقيضُهما المفهوم المردَّدُ بينَ هَاتَيْن الجزئيتين، والمتبادرُ مِنَ المفهوم المردَّدِ بينَهُما إِمَّا مُنفصلة مانعة الخلوّ مُركَّبةٌ منهُمَا ، أو حمليَّةٌ أو مُردَّدةُ المحمول بَينَهُما، فيكونُ نقيضُ الوجوديَّةِ اللَّادائمة الموجبة الكُلِّيَّةَ، مثلاً قولنا : إِمَّا أَنْ تصدق هذهِ الدَّائمةُ السَّالبة الجزئية أو تصدق هذهِ الدَّائمةُ الموجبة الجزئيّةُ، على أنَّ النَّقيضَ مُنفصلة، أو قولنا الصَّادق : إمَّا هذهِ الدَّائمةُ السَّالبة الجزئية أو هذه الدائمة الموجبة الجزئية، فنقيضُ قولنا : كُلُّ كاتب إنسان بالفعل لا دائماً ; قولنا : إِمَّا أَنَّ بعضَ الإنسانِ ليسَ دائماً ، أو أنَّ بعضَ الإنسانِ كاتب دائماً ، وَقِسِ البقيَّة، فتخصيص المفهوم المردَّدِ بالمنفصلة ليس بجيد ; تأمل .
So it is said: Either this contradictory or that. Meaning: The contradictory is one of them uninterruptedly, and this does not contradict that the contradictory may be both of them, and that is when each of the two propositions contained in the compound disjunction is true or false, but if one is true and the other false, then the contradictory is one of them. And his statement: 'Either this contradictory or that' is like your statement regarding the contradictory of the special conditioned [proposition] when it is universal, its parts are two universal simples, and their contradictories are two particular simples; so their contradictory is the concept undetermined between these two particulars, and what is understood from the undetermined concept is either an exclusive disjunction composed of them, or a categorical [proposition] or one with a divided predicate between them. Thus the contradictory of the positive non-permanent universal, for example our statement: 'Either this particular negative permanent [proposition] is true or this particular positive permanent [proposition] is true,' on the assumption that the contradictory is a disjunction, or our statement as the truthful one: 'Either this particular negative permanent or this particular positive permanent.' So the contradictory of our statement: 'Every writer is a human being actually not permanently' is our statement: 'Either some human being is not permanently, or some human being is a writer permanently.' And analogize the rest. So specifying the undetermined concept as a disjunction is not good; ponder.
Source pages p. 668

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of the contradictory of a compound disjunction (man'at al-khuluw). The author begins by stating a general rule: the contradictory is one of two, but when both parts of the disjunction are true or both false, the contradictory can be both. However, when one is true and the other false, only one is contradictory. He then applies this to the special conditioned proposition (al-mashrutah al-khassah). If it is universal, its parts are two universal simples, and their contradictories are two particular simples. The contradictory is the undetermined concept between these two particulars. Three possibilities for expressing this undetermined concept are given: an exclusive disjunction, a categorical, or a divided predicate. The author gives a concrete example: the contradictory of 'Every writer is a human being actually not permanently' is 'Either some human being is not permanently, or some human being is a writer permanently.' He concludes that restricting the undetermined concept to only the disjunction is not accurate, and calls for reflection.

Key Terms
النَّقِيض (al-naqid): The contradictory of a proposition; for compound disjunctions, it can be a disjunction of the contradictories of the parts. [Note: No significant shift; standard usage across logic texts.]
المفهوم المردَّد (al-mafhum al-muraddad): The undetermined concept that is the contradictory of a compound disjunction, referring to the two possible contradictories of its parts. [Note: This term is specific to discussions of compound propositions in later logic commentaries.]
المشروطة الخاصَّة (al-mashrutah al-khassah): A particular conditioned proposition that is compound, consisting of a universal conditioned proposition and a universal absolute proposition. [Note: Stable; part of the classification of propositions in post-Avicennan logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Either this action is not wajib or it is not haram
C) Both this action is not wajib and it is not haram
D) Either this action is not wajib and it is not haram

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of an exclusive disjunction is itself a disjunction of the negations of each part.

Propositions

Definition of Special Conditioned and Temporal Propositions
Author defines the special conditioned proposition and the temporal proposition as compound propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the special conditioned proposition (al-mashrutah al-khassah) and the temporal proposition (al-waqtiyyah) by their compound structure and components.

Core Idea
Special conditioned and temporal propositions are compound: they combine a modality with a denial of permanence.

In previous lessons, we studied simple modal propositions like the universal conditioned and the universal absolute. Now, we encounter more complex propositions that combine these. The special conditioned and temporal propositions are compound, meaning they contain two parts: a modal proposition and a universal absolute. Understanding their structure is crucial for correctly finding their contradictories, which the author will cover next.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of the special conditioned proposition
The definition states it is composed of a universal conditioned proposition (e.g., 'Necessarily, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer') and a universal absolute proposition (e.g., 'Nothing of the writer is moving' — implied by 'not permanently'). Note that the universal absolute is the negation of permanence.
The universal absolute here is the same in quality (affirmative/negative) as the conditioned part.
2
Parse the example for special conditioned
Example: 'Necessarily, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently.' Identify the first part up to 'not permanently' as the universal conditioned, and the second part (the meaning of 'not permanently') as the universal absolute.
The term 'not permanently' (la dawam) indicates essential non-permanence.
3
Identify the two components of the temporal proposition
The temporal proposition is composed of a temporal absolute (e.g., 'Necessarily, every writer is moving his fingers at the time of writing') and a universal absolute (e.g., 'Nothing of the writer is moving' — implied by 'not permanently').
The temporal absolute specifies a particular time for the necessity.
4
Compare the special conditioned and temporal propositions
Both have a universal absolute as the second component. The difference is in the first component: one is a universal conditioned (conditioned on the subject's description) and the other is a temporal absolute (conditioned on a specific time).
Both are restricted by essential non-permanence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي الشَّنَاقُضِ ثمَّ مَن أحاط بحقائقِ المركَّبات. وحاصلها : أنَّ المشروطة الخاصَّة : ما حكم فيها بضرورةِ النِّسبةِ مَا دامَ وصفُ الموضوع، وقُيِّدَتْ باللادوامِ الذَّاتي، فتكونُ مُركّبة من مشروطةٍ عامة، ومُطلقة عامة، وذلك نحو : بالضَّرورةِ كلُّ كاتب مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً لا دائماً . وهي موافقة للقضيَّة في الكيف والمطلقة العامة : هي المفهومة من لا دائماً ; أعني لا شيء مِنَ الكاتب بمتحرك : فَالمشروطة العامة : هي ما سوى لا دائماً. وأنَّ الوقتية : ما حكم فيها بضرورةِ النِّسبة في وقتٍ مُعيَّن، وقُيِّدَتْ باللادوام الذَّاتي، فتكونُ مُركَّبَةً مِن وقتيَّةٍ مُطلقة، ومُطلقة عامة، وذلكَ نحو : بِالضَّرورة كلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع وقتَ الكتابة لا دائماً ، فالوقتيَّةُ المطلقة ما سوى لا دائماً، وهي موافقة للقضيَّةِ في الكيف.
Chapter on contradictories. Then, whoever encompasses the realities of compound [propositions]. Its summary: The special conditioned [proposition] is that in which necessary relation is judged as long as the description of the subject exists, and it is restricted by essential non-permanence, so it is composed of a universal conditioned and a universal absolute, such as: 'Necessarily, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently.' And it agrees with the proposition in quality, and the universal absolute is understood from 'not permanently'; I mean 'nothing of the writer is moving' so the universal conditioned is what is other than 'not permanently.' And the temporal [proposition] is that in which necessary relation is judged at a specific time, and it is restricted by essential non-permanence, so it is composed of a temporal absolute and a universal absolute, such as: 'Necessarily, every writer is moving his fingers at the time of writing, not permanently.' So the temporal absolute is what is other than 'not permanently,' and it agrees with the proposition in quality.
Source pages p. 669

Commentary
This passage introduces a chapter on contradictories and then defines two types of compound propositions: the special conditioned (al-mashrutah al-khassah) and the temporal (al-waqtiyyah). The special conditioned proposition judges a relation as necessary as long as the description of the subject holds, but restricts it with essential non-permanence (la dawam dhati). This compound consists of two parts: a universal conditioned proposition (e.g., 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer') and a universal absolute proposition (e.g., 'Nothing of the writer is moving' — the meaning of 'not permanently'). The example given is: 'Necessarily, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently.' The temporal proposition similarly judges necessity at a specific time, also with essential non-permanence, composed of a temporal absolute and a universal absolute. Example: 'Necessarily, every writer is moving his fingers at the time of writing, not permanently.' In both, the first part (the conditioned or temporal absolute) agrees with the overall proposition in quality (affirmative or negative). This classification is essential for understanding how compound propositions are analyzed and how their contradictories are derived.

Key Terms
المشروطة الخاصَّة
الوقتية
اللادوام الذَّاتي
مطلقة عامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first part of the special conditioned proposition 'Necessarily, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently'?
A) 'Necessarily, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer'
B) 'Not permanently'
C) 'Nothing of the writer is moving'
D) 'Every writer is moving his fingers'

Key Takeaway
Special conditioned and temporal propositions are compound; their analysis into a modal part and an absolute part is essential for finding correct contradictories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a special conditioned proposition using an Islamic example (e.g., about praying, fasting, or hajj). Identify its two components: the conditional modal part and the absolute part.
?
Construct a temporal proposition using an Islamic example (e.g., about the obligation of prayer at a specific time). Identify its two components.
